HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: UPPER WEST SIDE;
Planners' Radical Traffic Idea: Close Part of Broadway

BYLINE: By JENNIFER KINGSON BLOOM 

 BODY:
With one giant tower after another rising in a concentrated area near Lincoln Square, city planners have become so concerned about the area's growing traffic problems that they are offering a radical solution: closing the southbound lanes of Broadway to vehicles from 65th to 63d Street.
In a draft report, the Planning Department calls on the Department of Transportation to consider diverting traffic in Lincoln Square from Broadway to Columbus Avenue, turning Broadway into a pedestrian mall, and changing the directions of certain crosstown streets. 
 In making the recommendation, city planners say, they have sided with neighborhood residents who have long complained about the difficulty of navigating the area by foot and by car, and who predict that the situation will grow worse.
"There are big problems out there now," said Jack Schmidt, a program coordinator in the Planning Department's transportation division. "Given the new development that's coming, we wanted to go out and solve them before they get worse."
For example, the Planning Department has acknowledged that crossing 65th Street at the Columbus Avenue and Broadway intersection takes 26 seconds, while the green light lasts only 14 seconds.
Last year, the construction frenzy near Lincoln Square prompted city planners to change zoning regulations, restricting the size of what could be built. Since, one big project has been finished -- the building on Broadway and 66th Street owned by Millennium Partners that houses the Sony movie theater -- and others are progressing -- like Donald Trump's Riverside South.
But planning commission members said they felt that transportation problems were ignored when the district was rezoned; after the laws were changed, they asked the Planning Department for solutions.
Several of the study's recommendations have already met with neighborhood approval, but support has been mixed for the idea of closing part of Broadway and for a proposal to build "neckdowns," or sidewalk extensions, at certain intersections.
"Those of us who have discussed it are very dubious about closing off any streets," said Barbara Adler, co-chairwoman of Community Board 7's Transportation Committee. Lincoln Center officials oppose the idea.
But Daniel L. Berta, manager for the Chase Manhattan Bank at Lincoln Plaza, said the idea made sense. "I have a lot of trouble crossing the street here," he said. "If they put some thought into how they're going to do it and expand the park, it could work out quite well."
City transportation officials, who would be responsible for implementing most of the proposed changes, are expected to comment on the recommendations in June.
Arlene Simon, the president of Landmark West!, and a consultant, Daniel L. Gutman, proposed the Broadway closing to the Planning Department; under their plan, street directions would be changed on parts of 64th and 63d Streets and Dante Park would more than triple in size. "What we're looking for is what would become most pedestrian-friendly," Mrs. Simon said.
Elizabeth Starkey, chairwoman of Community Board 7, said she thought the idea merited further study, but she preferred an idea that city planners rejected: a pedestrian bridge or tunnel across Columbus Avenue and Broadway at 65th Street. JENNIFER KINGSON BLOOM

HEADLINE: MARKET TIMING

 BODY:

Monday
Memorial Day holiday. Banks and exchanges are closed in the United States. They are also closed in Britain for the spring bank holiday.
 
Tuesday
The Conference Board index of consumer confidence for May is released today, one of several economic indicators scheduled this week that are expected to show a slowing economy. Forecast: the perception of gloomier business conditions is expected to weaken the index to 102.3, down from 105.5 in April.
 
Richard A. Grasso becomes the new chairman and chief executive of the New York Stock Exchange, succeeding William H. Donaldson.
 
Wednesday
The Commerce Department announces new home sales for April. Forecast: a 1.2 percent rise, to 584,000 homes.
 
The second estimate of gross domestic product is expected. Forecast: 3.1 percent growth, up from 2.8 percent announced last month.
 
Thursday
The National Association of Purchasing Managers releases its May indices. Forecast: 50.4 down from 52 in April, indicating a further slowdown in manufacturing.
 
The Commerce Department reports personal income and spending for April. Forecast: income up 0.3 percent and spending unchanged from March.
 
Factory orders for April are to be released. Forecast: down 0.1 percent, after falling 0.1 percent in March and 0.3 percent in February.
 
Friday
The Labor Department releases May unemployment numbers. Forecast: 5.7 percent, with 193,000 jobs added to the nonfarm payroll.
 
The Commerce Department announces leading economic indicators for April. Forecast: down 0.4 percent. This would be the third monthly decline in a row, which in the past has often signaled recessions.
 
April construction spending is scheduled. Forecast: down 0.4 percent.

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.

 BODY:

New Nest for Night Owls On the Upper East Side
 The French bistro Le Taxi has been at 37 East 60th Street, between Madison and Park Avenues, for more than a year. Starting on June 6, a local party promoter, Noel Ashman, will take over the 90-seat restaurant and bar each night from 11:30 P.M. to 4 A.M. to create what he calls a "party scene," offering hors d'oeuvres, sandwiches, fruit platters, hamburgers and desserts.
Le Taxi's owner, Julian Davis, says, "This little area is becoming hot for the 30- and 40-year-olds."
 
Downtown Jamaica Looks Forward To a New Store: Toys 'R' Us
 Toys "R" Us plans to open a 45,000-square-foot-store in Queens, on Jamaica Avenue at 168th Street. Michael P. Miller, a senior vice president at Toys "R" Us, calls the neighborhood "solid working class," adding, "It's a market that we haven't tapped to its fullest."
F. Carlisle Towery, the president of the Greater Jamaica Development Corporation, agrees. "We're happy Toys 'R' Us is moving into the heart of the Jamaica retail area, attracting additional customers and creating new jobs," he said.
The company, in Paramus, N.J., plans to open the store by early November.
 
In Search of New Students, Business School Moves to New Site
 For 67 years, Drake Business School occupied part of a building on Grand Concourse and Fordham Road, in the Fordham section of the Bronx. Recently, in an effort to attract more students, it moved to a new two-story center in Bronxdale, at 2122 White Plains Road, at Pelham Parkway. The new building was specially designed for the school and includes a laboratory for courses leading to certification as a medical assistant, a new program, said Gwen Sumter, the assistant to the president of Drake.
"Enrollment had dropped off in the Bronx because of economic reasons," Ms. Sumter said.
Drake, which also has branches in Manhattan, Queens and Staten Island, specializes in teaching word processing, legal secretarial skills and accounting. The student body is 70 percent women, Ms. Sumter said. Students must be 17 or older to enroll and are not required to have a high school or college diploma.

HEADLINE: The Plots, and Subplots, of a City Garden ...

BYLINE: By ANDREA KANNAPELL 

 BODY:
HOW do you measure 16 years? At the community gardens on the Lower East Side, where existence is carved out of lulls in housing construction and time is measured in the life cycles of tomatoes, peppers and daffodils, 16 years is the stuff of legend.
That is the age of the garden at Ninth Street and Avenue C, at least as close as anyone can tell. Neighborhood and garden old-timers are quick to recollect sometimes contradictory histories. But they all agree that it was Augustin (Nin) Garcia, a crotchety maintenance worker at the housing projects along Avenue D, who, perhaps drawn by the enormous and beautiful willow tree that still guards the Ninth Street side, first cleared a small plot in 1979. 
 He was joined by his son. Then came Peter Nieves, who watched their garden blooming from the American Legion hall across the street and started bringing Nin a beer now and then. Then Mr. Nieves invited some of his neighbors from Masaryk Towers, a 21-story brick Mitchell-Lama complex near the Williamsburg Bridge. Those neighbors invited a few friends, a few passers-by wandered in, and a few buildings collapsed . . . and now the garden runs from Ninth Street almost to 10th, and a quarter of a block east from Avenue C. It is one of the city's oldest and largest, and many say one of its most beautiful.
And it is a good place to take a look at the chemistry of a community garden -- that breed of urban space that is cropping up more and more on the city's landscape, where people who might not ordinarily have anything to do with one another find themselves shoulder to shoulder, pulling weeds in a kind of public backyard. People will be people. Sometimes they argue and fight.
The location of the garden, too, is significant. There are more than 700 city-sanctioned community gardens, and untold numbers of more ad hoc affairs, scattered throughout the city. But the Lower East Side, the birthplace of the community gardening movement, stands out for the complexity and concentration of its gardens: there are about 40 operating on city-owned land under year-to-year Green Thumb leases and a smattering of others.
There is the crowded, artsy Green Thumb garden at Sixth and Avenue B, notable for its community programs and its main decoration, a giant wooden derrick hung with hundreds of stuffed animals, a kind of East Village Watts Tower. And there's the Liz Christy garden, at Bowery and Houston, named for the late visionary who wedded the activism of the antiwar movement to growing environmental concerns and founded the Green Guerrillas, and who made a campaign of lobbing "seed grenades" into vacant lots to see what would take root. There are even squatter gardens, like the Bimbo Rivas Garden on 13th Street, which doubles as a dog run. But even against such an array of horticultural diversity, the Ninth and C garden is huge and varied. It spreads across perhaps an acre of city-owned land, with nearly 40 plots, tended by a cross section of New Yorkers: retired workers from Schrafft's and May's department store, the post office, the Army; working-class people who need a hobby, and artists, mothers and professionals who aren't so upwardly mobile that they have access to Hamptons backyards. There are Puerto Ricans, black Southerners, South Africans, South Americans, an Australian, Brits, Germans, Brazilians and an Iranian.
There are differences of opinion and approach, which cut across class, ethnic and age lines. Some of the gardeners like tidy little beds of perennials and ornamentals; some festoon their plots with whimsical statuary; some are no-nonsense farmers, who dismiss tales of lead leaching into their plants and aim for a good crop of tomatoes, peppers, cucumbers and eggplants.
These are not inconsequential differences in a place where people and their plots are referred to interchangeably, so that a name evokes not only a person, but also that person's effect. "Over by Howard," someone will say, meaning Howard Nudli's rosebed.
Not surprisingly, there are conflicts. Is a fence too high? A bush untrimmed? A prominent plot untidy? And even in a garden, somebody has to take out the garbage.
"The funny thing about community gardening, and it's no secret, is that the hardest part is not growing the plants -- it's getting along with other people," says Kim Mulcahy, a Liz Christy gardener who has been keeping tabs on Ninth and C.
"Everybody has different ideas of what beautiful is," he continues. "You think there's some base level you can agree on, like weeds don't look nice. But in fact some people think weeds are wonderful. There's often a lot of fighting going on. No one truly likes everybody. So you find ways to tolerate each other. You decide: what should we cooperate on, and what should we leave each other alone on? And that's pretty good, really."
Negotiating the sometimes surprisingly bitter eddies of conflicts has become a way of life for the gardeners. It is the price they pay, along with $12 in annual dues, for the daily joy of seeing a new flower burst into being, or dining on the vegetables they started from seed months earlier, or having access to a quiet green space -- or for a deeper kind of contentment that for some, verges on the religious.
 
THE MARCH OF TIME

On a recent Sunday, under a clear sun and vivid skies, Avenue C resembled a small town.
There was a parade: a brassy Peruvian marching band and a compact crowd of perhaps 100 marched down Seventh Street, carrying a life-size statue of a saint. Mothers, surrounded by skipping children, rolled their carts past aging, low-rise, brick and stone apartment buildings to the grocery. The Pedro Bakery at Eighth, where the gardeners often stoke up on cafe con leche, was crowded, the video games in the corner bleeping enticingly. Every so often, an M21 bus roared by, obliterating the strains from boomboxes at the new vendors market across the way. Inside the garden, quieter sounds prevailed: the chirp of sparrows, the rustle of wind through willow fronds, the dribble of water leaking from a hose and the smack of a mallet on wood.
Kitty Sheppard was building a new fence around her plot. She says: "George Washington Carver used to talk to his plants. For inspiration. They told him what to do next."
The garden is old enough to have witnessed the rise and fall of distinct social and botanical eras. Back in '79, the garden was a junkyard frontier, a forgotten piece of land graced by the giant willow and a sturdy cover of weeds.
Like homesteaders, the earliest gardeners sweated to make it arable. They sifted the dirt for chunks of brick, they carted away loads of trash. They became a club: new people who applied for plots, which at most gardens requires a few months of showing up and weeding, were ignored. They celebrated, with barbecues that lasted deep into summer nights. And they warred over plot sizes: the old tales evoke a kind of Wild West era, complete with land grabs, screaming fights and border disputes.
Ninette Kiddon, a feisty 63-year-old former City Ballet dancer and Avenue D resident, recalled the early years: "Nin was afraid to open it up to everyone. He worried they'd have different gardening practices, that they'd be on top of his stuff. He was a farmer -- he had an intense commitment. And as time went on, in this group, which was never homogeneous to begin with, rifts developed socially." It appears that the Masaryk people, Mr. Nieves's group, grew to be a powerful network, out-elbowing Nin's Alphabet City crew by the mid-80's.
"Nin and his son had the larger expanse," Mrs. Kiddon said. "And he was a grumpy man. So he was targeted as one who would be asked to leave." Others, she says, who tried to insist that the garden operate by its bylaws, rather than by whim, were also asked to leave.
Still, the garden was lush and beautiful, and it became part of the fabric of Avenue C. Vito DiBiasi, whose 79-year-old father lives directly across the avenue from the garden, watched it grow from Nin's tiny patch to its current, organized glory. "It's a pleasure to come out," he said. "You know what would have been here? Abandoned cars and garbage." Mr. DiBiasi keeps an eye on the garden at odd hours. "You get the 13-year-olds going over the fence. I open the window up and yell."
And some more unexpected neighborhood denizens were supporters. Norma Gahegan, a Masaryk resident, found that out a few years ago, when she couldn't get the lock on the garden gate to work. Drug dealers working the avenue told her they'd watch it for her overnight. "I guess they liked to look at it, too," she said.
On the other hand, Avenue C sometimes infringed on the garden. Paul Francois, a serious rock gardener, remembers the morning he came to the garden to discover a bullet hole through his prize Opuntia (a kind of cactus).
Low rents and the area's frontier mystique brought an influx of young people and artists into its aging tenements. By the end of the 80's the Green Thumb gardens began to fear that their long reprieve from housing construction was coming to an end, and Ninth and C opened up a crack, letting in a few new members to expand its community base of support.
Of the gardeners of this generation, some reveled in the garden's raw energy, like Mari-Claire Charba, an artist who staged annual "Dream Events" there -- performance pieces like a re-enactment of the garden's history. Some were serious botanists, like Mr. Francois, absolutely focused on their collections of peculiar plants and just as resolutely uninterested in community.
And some, like Tom Versella were another breed: the landscape types, who envisioned the garden as a coordinated statement of classical beauty.
Mr. Versella, a freelance landscaper, joined two years ago, with his friend Xavier Rodriguez, a cabinetmaker, both 38. They turned their corner plot into a showpiece, and began throwing themselves into the garden as a whole. "Tom and Xavier have energy beyond what some people comprehend as being normal," said Carol Kissling, a 36-year-old mother of two. "They have connections with nurseries. They can get plants. They get donations. They have a lot of drive; they're both youthful, strong; they can do a lot of heavy lifting. And they're single: they don't have any commitments keeping them at home."
That won them friends -- so many that last summer, Mr. Versella was elected the garden's president by private ballot, and Mr. Rodriguez became the head of the membership committee.
By all accounts, they have transformed the garden. They put in stone and brick pathways, linked plots with lush communal areas, installed new plots, had truckloads of soil brought in, cleared away the rubble from a building collapse in 1992 that expanded the garden nearly by half, lent their hands to help the other gardeners upgrade their plots, enlisted about 10 new members and tried to make it more of a neighborhood resource.
Their way, they say, can help protect the garden's Green Thumb lease. For if the economy improved enough to start another construction boom, the garden's only protection would be Community Board 3, which can decline to approve a city development plan or the sale of the property to a private developer. The fear of extinction is a constant theme in community gardens.
That fear, occasionally inflamed by a chance encounter with a city agent, or another garden's demise, is sometimes a stick Mr. Versella uses to win approval for some changes, like pulling down the ivy that covered the garden's outer fence in order to open it up visually to the public.
Mr. Versella, a native of Staten Island, describes his philosophy like this: "My involvement past my own plot is my own little stab at urban renewal. My parents don't have lots of money, and most people around here don't have lots of money. But even though you're poor it's not like you have to be dirty, or you can't be creative.
"Words like 'urban renewal' can be construed as gentrification. But I think this neighborhood can use a better quality of life."
But quality of life, it appears, is also in the eye of the beholder. He has lost support from some gardeners who don't share his vision; some don't like his authoritarian streak. Some have left; some are thinking of leaving.
Mrs. Kiddon confesses: "What I'm really worried about is that we're becoming suburbanized. A lot of the plots look nicely manicured, with lovely little paths. It'll get to be just like a walk in Secaucus! If everybody wants a little walk!" She shudders.
 
FENCE POLITICS

Even an issue as simple as a fence can trigger deep conflicts. For Mrs. Sheppard -- a neighborhood resident for 33 years -- a good plot fence is one her beans can climb up and that fence is tall. Mr. Versella says tall fences, which are impenetrable when covered with mature beans and such, are not wise: they block views of the main gate, and gardeners might not know if an unwelcome visitor is intruding, or worse.
But his objections, privately, are deeper. He says the fences are symbolic of the divisiveness that used to characterize the garden, when a few people tended to do things to suit themselves.
"With any group, there's politics," says Mr. Versella, his face lean and garden-tanned. "But in all the greening institutions around, I've heard over and over, this garden had a reputation of being a little private club. They wanted barbed wire around the fence! And most of the people in that past ruling junta, the ones that wanted the fence, they also have fences around their plots. It just ripples all the way through."
At the first meeting of the season, at the end of March, Mr. Versella and Mr. Rodriguez had introduced the idea of making fences around any central plots low and uniform. But Mrs. Sheppard said sternly, "My beans run up that fence," and the issue was dropped.
A few weeks later, as the Peruvians marched, she was putting up a new, solid fence, which reached over six feet high at one side. Helping her was Mr. Rodriguez, who seems to alternate between carrying out Mr. Versella's wishes and making peace by subverting them.
At the garden's next meeting, which overflowed the porch and the barbecue area in front of it, Mr. Versella sat with his back to Mrs. Sheppard's fence, at the head of a sun-dappled circle of about 30 gardeners. In deliberate tones, Mr. Versella spoke of his concerns for safety.
Mrs. Sheppard responded sharply: "I've been in this garden 15 years, and we've had breaches of security before. But I don't think you can blame it on those beans!"
Various members weighed in, for and against Mrs. Sheppard's right to her fence. Mrs. Sheppard offered to move to a less central plot, where the fence wouldn't be a problem. But the gardeners seemed almost embarrassed by the offer. When it came down to brass tacks, they wanted her to have her plot, and her beans, too. Finally, Mr. Rodriguez suggested reconstructing the fence to make it tall only where it runs perpendicular to the gate.
"Can we leave it that something that is satisfactory to everybody will be done?" Mr. Versella asked. Nods all around. A breath of relief passed over the group.
 
THE PRICE OF LEADERSHIP
 It's not just older members, who remember a freer time, who find Mr. Versella's constant scrutiny unpleasant.
Ms. Charba, the artist, drops by her plot, under the willow tree. Amid the flourishing ground cover punctuated with salvaged details from the collapsed buildings, she finds a note in a plastic Ziploc bag, secured by a rock. "Work party Saturday," it says, adding pointedly: "Sweeping under the willow is nice. But can you help with other things?"
Mr. Versella walks by. "Oh, did you get my note?" he says.
"Yes," she answers. "What are the other things?"
"There's a list," he says, gesturing at the bulletins he updates and posts weekly.
"Well, there's a work party on Saturday, right? I'll be there," she finishes. Mr. Versella walks away with an air of strained patience. Ms. Charba later confesses that she's thinking of giving up her plot.
"He has a spit list," says another member, who didn't want to be named criticizing Mr. Versella. "People told me, and I didn't believe it till I saw it. If he's upset with you or doesn't like you, he walks by and spits."
Mr. Versella says newer members don't realize what he's up against. "The interesting thing I found -- me coming in, trying to clarify, so there wouldn't be this chatter and animosity -- newer members don't know what it was like before and feel I'm too authoritative."
Mr. Versella says he is so tired of the difficulties of leadership that he is seeking a second plot in another garden so he can plant in peace, a place "where no one will ask me any questions." Meanwhile, he takes out his frustrations in quiet protests, like ignoring the garbage bags piling up at the garden's back gate. "I'm just going to wait till somebody does it."
Mr. Mulcahy, the Liz Christy gardener, says that the conflicts troubling Ninth and C are normal, and the payoff worthwhile. "When Tom and Xavier came, the garden was really looking for a big infusion of new energy and creativity.
"I don't know all the details," he adds, "but I know it was paralyzed, there were all these factions. And they were able to inspire people, they were able to show people what could be at the garden. I know how much energy it takes to re-energize a whole garden. It simply blows me away. I see the sweat in every brick and every bench."
And Jane Weissman, head of Operation Green Thumb, says that most Lower East Side gardens are led by "benevolent dictators." She says, matter of factly: "They're the ones that get everything going."
 
THROUGH IT ALL, VIVID LOVE
 Fraught as it may be, the garden remains vital. If tensions sometimes threaten to overwhelm the garden, the art of compromise is everywhere apparent.
"I'm a happy man," Mr. Versella said with a wide smile one sunny day. "Somebody took out the garbage."
And since their essential need to be with nature remains otherwise elusive, the gardeners will make what peace they can.
In her plot, Marie Soares, a 50ish Brazilian, grows reminders of home: "Peppers, collard greens, corn, squash -- and of course the flowers." She gets breathless, remembering. "The roses! We lived in the jungles of Brazil, and my mother grew white roses, beautiful, you could put in a salad and eat them. It was beautiful."
Mrs. Kiddon says: "You connect with the earth, go down into it, into your sub-sub-subconscious. The good thing you bring up is an element of regeneration and regrowth. And not just for you! And when you have that, you don't worry about Tom and Xavier, and who has the best kind of mulch.
"It's like the ancient Greek mysteries, in the spring and the fall," she says. "You went down into a cave, with all these anxieties and all this process. You were never supposed to talk about what you experienced. But people would come up, and just say: Seed. Grow."

HEADLINE: Japanese Put Stability Above Competition, in Life as in Business

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Although the news media have devoted much attention to the escalating trade conflict between the United States and Japan, little mention was made of differences between the two countries in fundamental values.
While both Americans and Japanese are more or less believers in the free market, the Japanese are much more ambivalent. This ambivalence is based on a more skeptical view in Japan of competition: whereas most Americans believe that vigorous competition is a good thing, most Japanese do not. Rather, the Japanese tend to place more value on stability, both in their personal lives and in business. 
 For instance, while American workers readily switch jobs to obtain higher incomes, Japanese workers prefer modest but stable incomes through lifetime employment.
Similarly, Japanese companies avoid keen competition, in part by banding together in associations known as keiretsu. Rather than striving to offer low prices, companies put much more stress on the quality of their products and services.
It is well known that competition among retailers is much less keen in Japan than America. For their part, Japanese consumers devote relatively little effort to shopping for the lowest prices.
At the most basic level, the Japanese dislike the quest for "money, money, money" because it conflicts with their traditional but still firmly held conceptions of virtue.
Thus, Japanese consumers are not different from consumers everywhere, and all else being equal, they like low prices. However, Japanese consumers understand that all else is not equal, and that low prices for goods and services entail other costs. Thus, that a Chrysler Cherokee that began in the United States with a factory price of $19,100 winds up in a Japanese showroom with a sticker price of $31,372 arouses no more than mild grumbling.
Japanese society has been structured to avoid strong competition. In this, government regulations play only a small part. In many cases, government regulation has gone too far, and the Japanese know this and are working to correct these cases. However, deregulation will take a long time, since it touches on values that to the Japanese are basic.
For my part, I regret that the traditional virtues of Japan are fading, although thus far the changes have been modest. United States negotiators would be well advised to bear firmly in mind just how sensitive these matters are for the Japanese.
 
KEN YANAGISAWA
Chief Representative
Mitsubishi Research Institute
Washington, May 22, 1995

SECTION: Section 4; Page 10; Column 4; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 305 words

HEADLINE: Don't Let Sharp Operators Near Social Security

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Senators Bob Kerrey and Alan K. Simpson (Op-Ed, May 23) exaggerate Social Security's funding problem, and their proposal to redirect one-third of the employee contribution to personal individual retirement accounts (some $50 billion a year) would obviously worsen it. 
 That money in private hands would invite every sharpster in the country to a fleecing party. Surely the Senators recently read how the Foundation for New Era Philanthropy hoodwinked some of the nation's most august institutions into turning over millions on its unsupported promise to find matching donors.
And did they not read that Prudential Securities repaid $700 million in partial settlement of Securities and Exchange Commission fraud charges? Every week, almost every day, one reads of private investment gone haywire, often because of fraud. Meanwhile, the Republican Congress seeks to cut back on investor protections against sharp practices.
Senators Kerrey and Simpson argue that their proposal will increase private investment. But economists divide on whether I.R.A.'s add to savings or simply divert savings from other less tax-favored vehicles.
For example, when people in employment-based pension plans could also make tax-deductible individual retirement account contributions, my wife and I increased our mortgage borrowing to make maximum I.R.A. contributions. We thereby got two tax breaks -- one on the I.R.A. and the second on the mortgage interest deduction -- but without increasing our savings. Many did the same.
The Kerrey-Simpson proposal sounds like the old remedy of "bleeding." That often sped the patient into the grave.
 
MERTON C. BERNSTEIN
Prof. of Law, Washington U.
St. Louis, May 23, 1995
 
The writer served as principal consultant to the 1982 National Commission on Social Security.

HEADLINE: THE NIGHT;
The Parties Got Rough, Arty, Chic and Tuneful

BYLINE: By BRYAN MILLER 

 BODY:
UPTOWN -- "I want to bring boxing back to Broadway," the restaurateur Arthur Cutler was saying the night of May 19 at a packed-to-the-ropes opening of Strykers Sporting Club, at 145 West 46th Street. The only contest going on in the room involved black-tie guests trying to reach the bar, but a few minutes later a bell rang and two welterweights furiously hammered at each other up in the ring.
 The crowd cheered. But not Vanessa Riker, a real estate agent from Los Angeles, who had been invited to the opening of Strykers, owned by Mr. Cutler and a group of partners. "This is my first fight experience," she said, cringing with every thud of leather against cranium. "Let me be polite and say that I might not accept the next invitation." 
 Mr. Cutler, who hopes to revive interest in Friday night fights, is also an owner of Carmine's restaurants (of the heavyweight portions), as well as of Ollie's, Docks and many more establishments around town.
 The crowd included friends and associates of Mr. Cutler, the boxing press and a fair number of stocky, tough-looking palookas right out of a Jimmy Cagney movie. Strykers is in the basement of what used to be the Romanian Club, and Mr. Cutler pointed out its perfectly preserved terrazzo floors and ornate moldings.
 "It's very exciting in here, I think," he said. "Manhattan hasn't had anything like this for a long while."
 As it got later, the boxers got bigger and meaner looking. Hard-core fans stayed to the end. Some, like Olivia Tracey, a reporter for The Irish Echo newspaper who had come to write about the social scene, made an exit halfway through. "The little guys are fun, but this is too much," she said.

DOWNTOWN -- Martin Mull, the guitar-playing, leisure-suited comedian and television pitchman, started his professional life as a painter and teacher at the Rhode Island School of Design.
 "I had a master's from RISD and thought I would teach or something, but I had no idea I would go into show business," he said on May 23 at the Rizzoli Bookstore on West Broadway in SoHo, where he was signing his new book, "Martin Mull: Paintings, Drawings and Words," published by Journey Editions, in Boston. "Back in the 60's, everybody was playing guitar, so I got a guitar and started performing."
 A fan asked if his new book was a retrospective of his work.
 "Retrospective?" he replied. "That sounds too much like 'posthumous.' "
 Rex Doane, a recent transplant from San Francisco, arrived carrying two Martin Mull humor books, "A History of White People in America" and "A Paler Shade of White."
 "Your books have had such a great influence on my life," Mr. Doane said, asking Mr. Mull to inscribe them.
 "And you're still standing?" the comedian asked.
 "Well, I am unemployed," Mr. Doane replied.

UPTOWN -- There was more blond hair than at a Stockholm soccer match on May 23 at the Fashion Cafe in Rockefeller Center during a bibulous benefit for the Dishes Project, an organization of supermodels dedicated to fighting pediatric AIDS.
 The party, complete with a Latin band, was sponsored by Bacardi-Martini U.S.A., which used the occasion to promote its new lemon-flavored rum.
 As the tropical drinks gushed and video lights glared, the crowd surged in the direction of the models, who included Hunter Reno, Yasmin, Frederique Vanderwal and Daniela Pestova, who were giving interviews and smiling into the flashbulbs.
 One of the main activities of the evening, aside from posing with models, was an auction for items like a silk blouse worn by Melissa Etheridge, a blue silk lame column dress designed by Mark Eisen and a signed T-shirt from Sharon Stone.
 Bonnie Pfeifer, the executive director of Dishes, which stands for Determined Involved Supermodels Helping to End Suffering, estimated that Barcardi's contribution to the organization had already exceeded $110,000.
 Among the organization's future fund-raising projects is a cookbook with recipes from models. Is that like asking a group of Mormons to write a guide called "Cocktail Parties for the 90's"?
 "It's a big misperception that models don't eat," Ms. Reno said. "They have to eat to stay healthy, in moderation, but they do eat."
 Dutch-born Ms. Vanderwal added that she not only likes to eat, but also likes to roll up her sleeves behind the stove.
 "I make a great pasta and great steak and potatoes," she said. "But for everything else, I definitely need a recipe next to me at all times."

 
 
That's Old-Time Entertainment
 Jean-Claude Baker, an unofficially adopted son of Josephine Baker and the owner of the bistro Chez Josephine on West 42d Street, spent 20 years researching the history of black entertainers in America for his recent book, "Josephine: The Hungry Heart" (Random House).
 Along the way, Mr. Baker befriended many old-time entertainers, some of whom drop in to Chez Josephine from time to time to perform. On a recent Friday night, the dinner crowd was being entertained by Al Bundy, a pianist, and Ray Blue, who plays saxophone. At one point Mr. Baker jumped up, grabbed the microphone, and announced, "We have in the audience one of the great singers of the jazz age, Laura Watson, who is still performing at 86 years of age."
 Ms. Watson, smooth skinned, animated and playful, ran through five numbers to resounding applause. She returned to her seat, graciously acknowledged the adulation, then turned to a companion.
 "What's this 86 business?" she said. "I'm 84 all day -- unless I'm counting my beat wrong."

HEADLINE: IDEAS & TRENDS: The Gulf War Veteran;
Victorious in War, Not Yet at Peace

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
AMERICA is fickle about its wars, and how it treats the men and women who fought them.
 The Allied victory in World War II seemed to transform veterans into heroes-for-life; the nation embraced them from the moment they returned, with the G.I. Bill and a booming economy, and has feted them again 50 years later. Korea and Vietnam were messier conflicts with ambiguous endings that left returning soldiers forgotten, bitter or worse. 
 When the Persian Gulf war came along, a friendless Iraqi enemy was dispatched in a 43-day blitz of high-tech weaponry with relatively few American casualties. Troops basking in homecoming parades across the country seemed poised to recapture the respect that World War II veterans enjoyed.
 But veterans' advocates and counselors say that expectation has gone unfulfilled. And as the nation tomorrow honors those who have died in battle, they say, an emerging profile of gulf war veterans suggests they may have more in common with Vietnam-era soldiers than World War II troops.

 
 
'Disappointed'
 Thousands of gulf veterans remain in the military, many using their combat records to climb swiftly through the ranks. But thousands of others are suffering mysterious ailments. Gulf veterans are experiencing higher rates of unemployment, divorce and alcohol abuse than Vietnam-era vets, counselors say.
 "I'm disappointed the way our country has treated us," said Charles Sheehan-Miles, 24, a tank loader in the gulf who is now executive director of the Gulf War Veterans of New England. "I'm afraid we're getting closer to the public image the Vietnam veterans had."
 That image of a disillusioned and embittered soldier may make it tempting to force into the gulf profile someone like Timothy J. McVeigh, an Army sergeant in the war who is charged in the Oklahoma City bombing. But experts say there is no evidence that gulf veterans are more likely to become political extremists or violent criminals.
 The gulf war veterans have problems enough, however, and some seem all the more puzzling because the military's demographics have changed dramatically since a largely conscripted United States army fought in Southeast Asia. In the Middle East, the Pentagon fielded a better-educated and better-trained all-volunteer force, supported by tens of thousands of reservists called to active duty. Nearly 60 percent of the more than 550,000 soldiers sent to the gulf were married and had dependents, proportionally three times as many as the soldiers in Vietnam. About 35,000 women were sent to the gulf, as were 16,337 single parents and 1,231 married couples.
 Popular support for the gulf warriors soared as it never did for those who fought in Vietnam or Korea. But four years after the war's end, doctors, counselors and veterans advocates realize the ebullient, brief welcome-home was a poor augury. "Desert Storm vets got a parade, something we didn't get, but then the country looked away," said Richard Fitzpatrick, executive director of the National Coalition for Homeless Veterans.
 Within a year of returning home, many gulf veterans who joined the military for a career were handed pink slips, casualties of the Pentagon's post-cold war cuts. Unlike those returning after World War II, these new civilians faced a sinking economy -- an era of restructuring and corporate downsizing, of disorienting shifts in the demand for labor.
 More than 30,000 veterans have complained of a gulf war syndrome, whose symptoms include fatigue, muscle pain and breathing trouble. Preliminary reports say most of those examined have ordinary, diagnosable illnesses, while a small portion have symptoms that are unexplained. President Clinton signed an order on Friday creating a committee to examine the Government's efforts to cope with these baffling cases.
 Psychiatrists say that about 10 to 15 percent of gulf war veterans in several small studies suffered from post-traumatic stress disorder, an anxiety disorder caused by a stressful event, like combat or a natural disaster. The rate is comparable to that found among Vietnam-era vets, and the numbers could have been higher, officials say, had military doctors not learned from Vietnam how to recognize and treat the warning signs of combat trauma more quickly.
 Many gulf veterans say their problems stem from a dissonance between what they saw and what the public saw. In August 1992, a 33-year-old Army reservist who worked as a supervisor cataloguing the Iraqi war dead came to Bill Sautner, a veterans' counseling therapist in Orlando, with photographs he and his friends had taken of burned-out tanks, bloody bunkers and charred bodies of Iraqi soldiers.
 "He and many other gulf war vets saw the full range of war's destruction -- images that he still relives today," said Mr. Sautner, whose patient requested anonymity. "But the public only saw a sanitized, impersonal view on TV."
 Haunted by such memories, veterans had mixed feelings about their homecoming. "The gulf war vets came back from a four-day ground war as heroes, but when in groups with other combatants who fought a year or more in the jungles, they'll be apologetic and feel a certain amount of guilt," said Dr. Joseph Gelsomino, who runs 28 veterans centers in the Southeast.
 The combination of a weak economy, medical problems and a lack of affordable housing have helped make gulf veterans the fastest-growing segment of the nation's 271,000 homeless veterans. Vincent Torrez, a 29-year-old former military police officer, lives at a Salvation Army shelter in Long Island City, Queens, while looking for work. "It's very competitive and there are a lot of people with degrees," said Mr. Torrez, a high-school graduate who attended Marist College for a year. "But I think the Lord will look out for me. I might not get what I want, but I'll get what I need."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Like Vietnam veterans, gulf veterans have high levels of homelessness and joblessness. Vincent Torrez lives at a shelter in Queens. (Edward Keating/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE NATION: Welfare States;
Why Lump Sums Mean Some Lumps

BYLINE: By CELIA DUGGER 

 BODY:
THE Republican proposals most likely to radically revamp the nation's welfare system are not those that have gotten the greatest attention by appealing to the stern moral judgments of Americans: the denial of cash benefits to teen-age mothers and women who give birth while on welfare.
 Rather, the greatest potential to wither the welfare state lies in the Republican plan to upend the historic Federal and state partnership in providing for poor families. 
 Republicans in the House and Senate want to end the six-decade Federal guarantee of cash support to all poor women and children who qualify. Instead, Washington would send states a fixed, lump sum of money -- a block grant -- that would not grow beyond last year's level even if the number of poor people and the cost of caring for them did. States, in turn, would no longer have to put up a dime of their own money to match the Federal contribution. They could run welfare programs with almost no restrictions or minimum standards imposed by Washington.
 The Republican plan would also give states a reason to spend less on Aid to Families with Dependent Children, the main welfare program. Under the block grant, states would lose Federal dollars if they spent more of their own.
 "The block grant is the secret device for cutting welfare benefits," says Prof. Paul E. Peterson, director of the Center for American Political Studies at Harvard University. "It is a way of avoiding blame for loading deficit reduction onto the backs of the poor."
 The block grant approach, adopted by the House of Representatives in March as part of the Republican Contract With America, got a major boost Friday when the Senate Finance Committee endorsed it, while rejecting those provisions of the House bill that would forbid the use of Federal funds to provide benefits to unmarried mothers under 18 and to women who have more children while on welfare.

 
 
Scrooges and Midases
 Some proponents of the block grant say that freeing the states of Federal red tape will set off a rush of innovation in welfare policy, not a rush of spending cuts.
 "I find legislators and governors as compassionate as members of Congress and Presidents," said Senator Bob Packwood, the Oregon Republican who is chairman of the Finance Committee, which has jurisdiction over welfare. "For us to presume they're Scrooges and we're Midases is wrong."
 But other Republicans readily acknowledge that the plan is likely to sharply reduce welfare spending -- and they argue that is a good thing. In their view, welfare itself is a cause of poverty.
 "In welfare, you get what you pay for," said Robert Rector, a senior policy analyst at the Heritage Foundation and an architect of the House plan. "We paid for nonwork and nonmarriage and we've gotten dramatic increases in both. The United States of America spends too much on welfare."
 Critics of the Republican plan, including many mayors, contend that it will starve the welfare system of money, stifle innovation and worsen the plight of cities, and may leave millions of women and children in free fall from the safety net.
 Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, the New York Democrat who dominated the Senate's welfare debate until he lost his chairmanship of the Finance Committee this year, maintains that innovation costs more than many critics of the current system are prepared to spend. States that have done the best job of moving single parents off welfare have spent more on child care, health care, transportation and other work-related expenses to keep former welfare recipients employed. Mailing them a check is much cheaper.
 "A block grant advertised as an inducement to innovation could be the end of innovation if resources are cut back," Mr. Moynihan said.
 The Congressional Budget Office has estimated that under the House-approved plan, Federal spending on welfare by the year 2000 would be at least 20 percent less than if the status quo were maintained and would decline at least 10 percent over the next seven years under the Senate version. The Congressional Budget Office concluded that even if the states managed to impose the Senate bill's proposed work requirements on welfare recipients, the states would wind up spending most of their Federal welfare money on job training, child care and similar services rather than on direct payments to poor people.
 Southern congressmen, meanwhile, expressed concern that freezing Federal welfare spending would impose "severe budget and human consequences" on states with growing populations.
 The Republican plan would reverse the financial incentives for states to spend on welfare; states would lose 30 cents of Federal funds for every $1 of their own they spent because of the interrelationship between welfare and food stamps.
 Food stamp benefits, which are Federally financed, are calculated according to a family's cash income. If the state spends $1 of its own to increase a poor family's welfare benefits, that same family would then lose 30 cents worth of food stamps. In effect, states would be spending $1 for only 70 cents worth of benefits.
 Turning welfare over to the states to run as they choose could also set off what Professor Peterson called "the race to the bottom." Fueling the competition among states to slash benefits and restrict eligibility for welfare would be the common perception that generous welfare programs are a magnet for the poor.
 "When you shift the locus of decision-making down, each state wants to undercut the others' welfare benefits so as not to attract low-income people," he said.
 Even under the current system, states have allowed inflation to erode the value of cash welfare benefits by more than 40 percent since 1972. And already, some governors are asking Washington to waive current Federal welfare rules to impose time limits and other restrictions.
 For example, Massachusetts is seeking a two-year limit on welfare to employable single parents and their children; Connecticut's Governor, John G. Rowland, wants a 21-month limit. Mr. Rowland said he believes the policy, which would be the harshest in the nation, will discourage poor families from moving there.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Unique Morris, a welfare mother, with her children in Camden, N.J. (Librado Romero/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE NATION: Past Masters;
Blowing the Dust Off the Constitution That Was

BYLINE: By LINDA GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
THE Supreme Court gift shop keeps stacks of pocket-sized Constitutions by the cash registers and sells them to tourists by the thousands each year. The little booklets are 35 pages long and are, of course, completely authentic. But when the Justices meet upstairs to decide a case, the written Constitution is only one of several constitutions that are, in a manner of speaking, also on the table.
 There is the body of precedent that defines the Constitution as currently understood. 
 There is the Constitution that once was, as defined by precedents that have been formally overruled or less formally placed on a shelf.
 And there is, through a continual process of challenge and change dating back to the country's earliest years, another constitution struggling to be born.

 
 
Gun and Term Limits
 Recent events at the Court have moved that struggle to a new plane. Two 5-to-4 decisions in the last month suggest that a long-discarded set of constitutional principles -- a "Constitution-in-exile," to use a phrase coined by one of its advocates, Judge Douglas H. Ginsburg of the Federal appeals court here -- is about to assume its place at the table as a reincarnation of the Constitution that was.
 One of the decisions, United States v. Lopez, invalidated a Federal law that prohibited the possession of guns near a school. In doing so, the decision limited the scope of congressional power over interstate commerce for the first time since the New Deal.
 The other decision, U.S. Term Limits v. Thornton, barely struck down state-imposed term limits for members of Congress over a startling dissent by Justice Clarence Thomas that argued not only that term limits are valid but also that the Federal Government operates in large measure at the sufferance of the states.
 In an essay in the current issue of Regulation magazine, published by the Cato Institute, a libertarian think tank here, Judge Ginsburg offered an inventory of constitutional doctrines that the Court repudiated about 60 years ago when it began to endorse the exercise of national economic power at the heart of the New Deal.
 Among these were the doctrine that had viewed as illegitimate the delegation of power by Congress to the administrative agencies; the demise of that doctrine was the prerequisite for the growth of the modern regulatory state. Another was the commerce clause, which the Court had viewed until the mid-1930's as a brake on the exercise of national power over the economy, rather than a charter for it. Another was the takings clause, which requires compensation when private property is "taken for public use" and which served as a limitation on Government land use regulation.
 "The memory of these ancient exiles, banished for standing in opposition to unlimited government," Judge Ginsburg wrote, "is kept alive by a few scholars who labor on in the hope of a restoration, a second coming of the Constitution of liberty -- even if perhaps not in their own lifetimes."
 Those self-referential words, written before the Lopez decision, now sound quaint. A full restoration may not be imminent, but this agenda is hardly in exile; it is, for the first time in more than a generation, in play. For example, a year ago, in Dolan v. City of Tigard, a 5-to-4 decision written by Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist took a significant step toward reinvigorating the takings clause by requiring a new degree of justification by local governments before they impose land use restrictions on property-owners.
 On another front, the 10th Amendment, which reserves to the states powers not given to the Federal Government by the Constitution, is in the ascendance, invoked most recently in a 1992 majority opinion by Justice Sandra Day O'Connor. In that case, New York v. United States, the Court struck down a Federal environmental law that required states either to regulate the low-level radioactive waste generated within their borders or to "take title" to the waste and assume all legal liability for it.
 "States are not mere political subdivisions of the United States," Justice O'Connor said in concluding that the law infringed on state sovereignty.
 Even if the Court remains unwilling to go so far as to dismantle the Federal Government as administrative state, overturn the string of precedents that extended the Bill of Rights from the Federal Government to the states, or sharply curb the scope of Federal preemption of state law -- outcomes that still appear far-fetched if no longer completely inconceivable -- the polarities of the debate have shifted.
 The debate that has preoccupied modern constitutional law has been the question of rights: the right to privacy, to due process, to equal protection. Now the Court, for the first time in half a century, is engaging in a debate over power: where power resides in the Federal system and how the Constitution's grant of powers to the national Government is to be interpreted.

 
 
Marshall's Plan
 That "the Federal Government's powers are limited and enumerated," as Justice Thomas said in his dissenting opinion last week, is almost a truism. Article I, Section 8 gives Congress the power to impose taxes, regulate commerce, declare war, coin money, establish Federal courts below the Supreme Court level, protect copyrights, and so on.
 The textual list, of course, begs the questions of interpretation. Is the list meant to be exclusive? How strictly are the enumerated powers to be read? Does Congress have implied powers, or is it limited to those expressly granted? What is the meaning of Section 8's final clause, granting Congress the right "to make all laws which shall be necessary and proper" for carrying out its powers?
 These are ancient questions that Chief Justice John Marshall's opinions for the early Supreme Court had appeared to resolve in favor of a strong national government. The tone of Justice Thomas's dissent last week suggests that once again the assumptions underlying those early decisions' allocations of power are, to some members of the Court, far from self-evident truths.
 One question now is how activist the current Justices are prepared to be in overturning precedent in order to bring their constitutional vision home. The Lopez decision, striking down the Federal Gun-Free School Zones Act, did not actually overturn any of the Court's commerce clause precedents. Rather, Chief Justice Rehnquist's majority opinion adopted a narrow definition of economic activity that made a more frontal assault unnecessary. But if the Lopez majority is serious, it will not be satisfied for long with what it can accomplish through indirection and redefinition.
 A Federal Government whose authority was limited by the Constitution in exile could look dramatically different. If Congress could not delegate legislative authority to administrative agencies, a vast quantity of rules and regulations would wither along with the agencies that promulgated them. Land use and environmental regulation would be sharply curbed. The constitutional underpinning of Federal civil rights laws, based on the power of Congress to regulate interstate commerce, would be called into question.
 The Constitution in exile is not the only vision competing with the status quo. Liberals have theirs, too, under which the Government would have affirmative obligations to guarantee housing and minimum subsistence; actions that have the effect of discrimination would be seen as violating the constitutional guarantee of equal protection even in the absence of an intent to discriminate; and constitutional norms that now apply only to government action would also apply in the private sector on the theory that there is no longer a meaningful distinction between the public and private spheres. Government would be seen as violating an individual's right to due process not only by actual misconduct but also by inaction, by a failure to protect citizens from foreseeable harm.
 These principles cannot really be seen as a constitution in exile, because most have never been accepted as part of the Constitution. Rather, they make up a kind of shadow constitution that liberal scholars embrace as a natural extension of their golden age, the Supreme Court under Chief Justice Earl Warren, who retired in 1969.
 Which golden age would Chief Justice Rehnquist and his allies in the Lopez majority -- Justice Thomas and Justices O'Connor, Anthony M. Kennedy and Antonin Scalia -- invoke if they had to select one?
 Justice Kennedy, who departed from this group and deprived Justice Thomas of a majority in the term limits case, said in a concurring opinion in the Lopez case that "the wrong turn was the Court's dramatic departure in the 1930's" from the limited view of Congress's power that had lasted 150 years.
 Was the golden age the Court of the 30's then? Or earlier than that, before the Civil War amendments brought the full meaning of the Constitution and the Bill of Rights home to the states? Or was it earlier still, before the Federalists won the ratification battle by which the Constitution became the law of the land? A question that would have sounded preposterous only weeks ago is now merely provocative.

HEADLINE: Guatemalan Ex-Dictator on Rebound

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: GUATEMALA, May 26

 BODY:
When he was Guatemala's head of state, his 16-month tenure was among the bloodiest in this country's violent history, with thousands killed and more than a million displaced. When he was ousted in a coup in 1983, many Guatemalans assumed that like other deposed dictators before him, he would fade quietly into obscurity.
But in a remarkable political comeback, Gen. Efrain Rios Montt was elected Speaker of Guatemala's Congress in December. Now, with the presidential election less than six months away, he has emerged as the leading candidate. 
 He is vowing to run in defiance of a constitutional provision that bars him from seeking that office because he previously held it as a result of a coup. The courts have repeatedly upheld the prohibition.
"For me it's the presidency or nothing," General Rios Montt, 68, said during an interview on Wednesday in his spacious office at the ornate National Congress building.
"More than a campaign, this is a question of principle, a movement," he added, to clean up crime and corruption and "let the voice of the people be heard."
The retired armed forces chief says his current electoral crusade at the head of the rightist Guatemalan Republican Front is the culmination of "a struggle of more than 20 years" that began when he ran for President in 1974 and the election was stolen out from under him.
Since he became a born-again, evangelical Protestant in the late 1970's, he has viewed himself as Guatemala's man of destiny, chosen by God to lead the country out of a wilderness of immorality and suffering.
"I have not lied and I have not deceived these people," he said. "I have made a commitment to do what I ought to do, not what I want to do."
The resurgence of General Rios Montt comes at a moment of deep crisis for Guatemala and its relations with the United States. In particular, recent disclosures of CIA payoffs to Guatemalan military officials and accusations that Guatemalan soldiers killed an American innkeeper and a guerrilla leader who was the husband of an American lawyer have shaken the political system here.
He became President the last time as a result of a coup in March 1982, and immediately began a ferocious campaign against leftist guerrillas.
Thousands of Guatemalans, most of them Indian peasants, were massacred in the violence that followed, and a million others were forcibly resettled or driven into exile before Gen. Oscar Mejia Victores seized power in another military coup late in 1983.
In 1985 Guatemala approved a new Constitution that included an article apparently intended to put an end to General Rios Montt's efforts to return to the presidency to, as he often put it, finish the divine task he had begun.
Article 186 bars "the leader or leaders of a coup d'etat, armed revolution or similar movement" and "those who as a consequence of such acts become chief of state" from running for President.
When General Rios Montt tried to run for President in 1990, the Constitutional Court ruled that he was ineligible, and he withdrew from the race. But this time, he said, "I am not just a simple citizen," but rather head of the national legislature, able to "go on the attack" and orchestrate changes to make his candidacy legal.
Earlier this year, his party introduced revised election laws that would have eliminated the authority of the Supreme Electoral Tribunal, the national elections board, to disqualify candidates. But the constitutional court quickly struck down the proposal, citing procedural irregularities.
Now General Rios Montt's supporters, the largest bloc in Congress, have introduced a measure to replace the current members of the elections board.
"Everything I have done is legal," he said. "There has never been a sentence handed down against me -- only opinions, never a judged case."
General Rios Montt's critics see his insistence on running as a blatant disregard for democracy.
"If it was illegal five years ago, it is illegal now, because nothing has changed, not the law and not history," said Mario Antonio Sandoval, a leading political columnist for the newspaper Prensa Libre.
On Thursday, Msgr. Alvaro Ramazzini, spokesman for the Guatemalan Bishops' Confrence, called General Rios Montt a man whose behavior showed "a lack of ethics." While President, he added, the general was responsible for "criminal acts that the people ought not to forget."
His candidacy is also opposed by many of his former colleagues in the military.
Col. Francisco Luis Gordillo, who helped organize the 1982 coup, recently appealed to the general's "nationalism and patriotism to desist from his presidential aspirations," saying that they were not only illegal but that they would lead to international economic sanctions that would be "fatal for Guatemala."
But citing another section of the Constitution, General Rios Montt argues that the prohibition from office cannot be applied to him retroactively. He also maintains that the restriction is unconstitutional because it deprives voters of their right to choose their leaders in a democratic process.
"I have the right to choose" whether to run for President, General Rios Montt said. "They do not have the right to withhold that decision from me."
General Rios Montt's main opponent, a former Mayor of Guatemala City, Alvaro Arzu, who is the candidate of the conservative Party for National Advancement, argues that supporters of democracy need not worry about the general's prospects.
"With or without General Rios Montt, we are in a condition to defeat politically the myth of the all-powerful dictator with his magic wand of the iron fist," Mr. Arzu told reporters here this week.
But recent opinion polls seem to indicate otherwise. In a survey of voters published earlier this month by the newspaper Siglo XXI, only 33 percent of voters polled said they planned to vote on Nov. 12, but 48 percent of those favored General Rios Montt, compared with 27 percent for Mr. Arzu.
Guatemalan and foreign political analysts say General Rios Montt's popularity stems at least partly from the disillusionment Guatemalans feel after a decade of nominal civilian rule and democracy.
Corruption flourished under Vinicio Cerezo Arevalo, who took power as an elected civilian President to succeed General Mejia Victores. Mr. Cerezo's successor, Jorge Serrano Elias, who was elected in 1990, tried to dissolve the Government and suspend constitutional rights two years ago. He failed and was replaced by a weak caretaker President, Ramiro de Leon Carpio.
"The electorate wants a military man because we represent credibility, law and order," argued Gen. Hector Gramajo, a former Defense Minister who is also a presidential candidate but so far is not doing well in the opinion polls. "That is exactly where Cerezo, Serrano and this guy who is in there now have failed."
General Gramajo predicted that General Rios Montt would not be allowed to run and said that he expected to be the main beneficiary.
General Rios Montt said that if allowed to run, he might follow the counsel of his political advisers, who have told him to "just take it easy, because I've already won."
If his effort to register as a candidate is rejected, he said, he will launch a campaign of "peaceful resistance" and campaign on the hustings exactly as if he were a recognized candidate.
Guatemalan political leaders and foreign diplomats said that could lead to a destabilizing political impasse, and referred to a recent prediction that General Rios Montt made in response to criticism of his actions.
"Anarchy will prevail if I am not permitted to run," he warned.

Thousands of Guatemalans, most of them Indian peasants, were massacred in the violence that followed, and a million others were forcibly resettled or driven into exile before he was overthrown in another coup. (Associated Press); Gen. Efrain Rios Montt today, as he seeks a term as an elected President of Guatemala. (Nancy McGirr for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Irish Economic Growth Lifts Hopes

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: LIMERICK, Ireland

 BODY:
When Lisa O'Dea's parents finished school 38 years ago, job opportunities were few, and like generations of Irish before them they went abroad to find work, in their case staying 15 years in economic exile in the United States before coming home.
The situation facing Miss O'Dea as a 23-year-old graduate business student at the University of Limerick could hardly be more different. Ireland, long one of Western Europe's worst economic laggards, is in the midst of a major transformation, and is now enjoying rapid growth, booming exports to the rest of Europe and high rates of new jobs in manufacturing and technology industries. 
 "I'm very optimistic about the opportunities we have," Miss O'Dea said, sitting among a group of classmates. "If we want to work in Ireland we will be able to. A lot of us may choose to go abroad, but we don't have to go abroad."
With rising prosperity, residents said, has come a growing sense of confidence among people who have always seen themselves as one of Europe's poor relations. And with Ireland no longer so shackled economically to Britain, they said, the country increasingly wants to assert itself on the European stage, lobbying, for example, to be among the first countries to enter a planned European currency union before the turn of the century.
"One of the biggest transformations of the last 10 or 15 years is that as a country we're increasingly aware that we control our own destiny," said Tom Finucane, a young engineer at Analog Devices, an American-owned computer chip company that is among the many technology companies to set up shop in western Ireland in recent years.
The Irish turnaround has its limits. Unemployment, though expected to continue declining this year, remains above 14 percent, one of the worst rates in Western Europe.
Pockets of poverty still blight cities and the countryside. Economic output per person is still only 73 percent of the average for Western Europe, although up from 62 percent in 1988.
And by many visible standards, Ireland remains far from the affluence enjoyed by most other Western European nations. The Irish, for example, own 228 cars per 1,000 inhabitants, compared with 361 in Britain and 480 in Germany.
Part of the economy's buoyancy comes from a $1.6 billion a year subsidy to Ireland by the European Union that goes to everything from road-building to job training.
But the country has succeeded in luring many foreign companies to Ireland by offering tax breaks, a relatively low-paid and well-educated work force and other incentives.
It has brought Government finances under control since the late 1980's after a decade of profligacy and mismanagement that sent inflation soaring. As a result, this nation of 3.5 million people has posted one of Europe's strongest economic records over the last few years.
While most European nations saw their economies stagnate or shrink starting in 1992 in what was the worst global recession since World War II, Ireland's output increased substantially each of the last several years and grew 6.5 percent last year. Inflation was only 2.5 percent.
Exports grew by more than 10 percent last year. The economy created 34,000 new jobs last year, helping reduce the unemployment rate from 15.8 percent in 1993. Ireland is the only country in Europe to have consistently created new manufacturing jobs since the mid-1980's. With job prospects improving within Ireland relative to other nations, net emigration has fallen from a recent peak of 44,000 in 1989 to 10,000 last year. The prospect of a permanent peace in Northern Ireland has raised hopes for further foreign investment and more tourism in both the Irish Republic and in Ulster.
And where once the economy was built on agriculture, it is increasingly driven by the computer industry, pharmaceuticals and other sophisticated, relatively high-paying manufacturing businesses.
"There's been an absolute transformation," said Terry Baker, an economist at the Economic and Social Research Institute in Dublin.
The changes are easily seen in the region around Limerick, a gritty city 120 miles southwest of Dublin. Until the 1970's the area had little industry except some grain and flour milling and meat processing, and relied heavily on nearby Shannon Airport and on small-scale farming for employment.
Since then, agricultural employment is down sharply, to 22 percent of the local work force from 45 percent, with tourism rapidly catching up with farming in economic importance.
The mills and the meatpacking plants are gone, replaced by technology companies, many from the United States, that have moved in over the last decade, attracted by the country's low taxes, low wages and relatively high educational levels. Analog Devices, for example, employs 920 people in the area and both designs and makes chips here. It is just finishing construction of a state-of-the-art chip manufacturing site.
Just outside of town is the University of Limerick, which has built itself into a center of science, technology and business training and feeds a steady stream of graduates into good jobs.
The rundown city center has been partly redeveloped, with a new shopping mall and office buildings.
"We've created an employment base that just wasn't here before," said Paul Sheane, the chief executive of Shannon Development, the regional development agency. The agency was set up in 1959 by the Government to help replace the jobs lost from Shannon Airport's declining importance as a trans-Atlantic stopover, and has gone on to play a key role in the area's transformation.
But Mr. Sheane said the region and the country still had a long way to go in creating jobs -- a reality that is only too evident in the shuttered store windows and grim housing projects in large swaths of Limerick, where unemployment in some neighborhoods runs to 50 percent. Unemployment is particularly high among older workers who lost jobs in dying industries years ago and lack the education and the skills for the region's new high-tech jobs.
"We now have a university and some very good employers in terms of multinational companies that create well-paid employment for a lot of people, and there is a sense that the economy of Limerick city and midwest region generally is healthy," said John Hannafin, vice chairman of the Paul Partnership, an organization of community groups set up to address poverty and unemployment.
"But alongside that there are a lot of people who aren't particularly benefiting, who say it hasn't really helped them and their families," Mr. Hannafin said. "There is a sense of bewilderment and hopelessness among people who feel the new Ireland is leaving them behind."
