HEADLINE: New York's Quest For More Visitors

BYLINE: By BRUCE WEBER 

 BODY:
Maybe the oldest joke about New York City, Stephen J. Morello said, is the one about the guy who gets off the bus, puts down his bags, looks at the sky and shouts: "Look out, New York! I'm going to conquer you!" Then he looks down and his bags are gone.
It's a wryly appropriate story for Mr. Morello to tell about his job as president of the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau; in a city that sells everything, it's his task to sell the city itself, to expand the flow of strangers coming into town in order to separate them from their money, if not their belongings. 
 Mr. Morello uses the story to illustrate his own welcome to town. It was a year ago, just after Mr. Morello took over the post, that John S. Dyson, the Deputy Mayor for Economic Development, described the bureau as "asleep at the wheel." And in the intervening months, he's found himself in the tangled leadership of the city's tourist industry, where -- surprise! -- any number of people are willing to tell him how to do his job.
"If I'd said I was going to come in and conquer this thing, get everybody on the same page and moving in the same direction," said Mr. Morello, "I'd still be down at the Port Authority looking for my suitcase."
Tomorrow is a landmark in Mr. Morello's stewardship of the bureau, an occasion he has spent the year preparing for. For the next three days, 1,500 tour operators from 69 countries -- people who sent 80 percent of the 44 million foreign visitors to the United States last year -- as well as more than 1,000 American travel suppliers, are to gather at the Javits Center for the Travel Industry Association's annual convention. Known, inexplicably, as Pow Wow, it is the nation's largest convention dedicated to generating international travel to the United States.
At the convention, the delegates will purchase blocks of airline tickets, hotel rooms, rental cars and the like, which they will then divide into smaller packages to be made available to travel agencies and individual travelers in their home countries. The association estimates that more than $2.6 billion in business will be transacted, and that 10 percent of the total is directed at the host city over the following two or three years. Do the math: that's $260 million poured into the New York economy.
"It's kind of like hosting the Super Bowl," said Merrett Stierheim, president of the Greater Miami Convention and Visitors Bureau, which held Pow Wow last year.
No wonder the usually contentious components of the city's tourist industry -- the state and city governments, private business and the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau -- have put aside their squabbles to lick their collective lips.
"In all the years I've been involved I've never seen a better orchestrated, more effective effort to promote tourism," said Martin E. Segal, the chairman emeritus of Lincoln Center, who is heading the committee to involve the arts in Pow Wow.
Only last fall Mr. Segal resigned as chairman of the host committee for Pow Wow, rather than spend the year pleading for sponsorship money after the city government pledged to contribute only $100,000 of the convention's $3.2 million cost.
Now, however, he says the convention represents "a spectacular opportunity for us," a chance to show off what the city's tourist industry has learned in the last three years, since a cooperative venture between city government and business, "NY92," was formed to promote the city during the Democratic National Convention.
"I think it is accepted now, as a consequence of NY92, NY93 and NY94, that we're all in the same boat," Mr. Segal said. "The restaurants won't do well unless there are tourists; the hotels won't have occupants unless people come to town, and people won't come unless there are interesting events."
 
The Market
Signs of Health, And Lurking Perils
 Pow Wow arrives at a curious juncture in New York City's tourism history. There is much good news: Some 24.6 million people visited last year, spending more than $10 billion. Hotel occupancy was 75.2 percent, up nearly 6 percentage points from 1993, even as the average price of a room rose nearly $7, to $144.03. Overseas visitors numbered 4.1 million, a record for any American city, up 8.5 percent from 1993. Broadway attendance was up, as was the number of visitors to the Convention and Visitors Bureau Information Center at Columbus Circle.
But there are causes for concern. Even as the number of visitors increases, the city's share of the travel market, particularly the international market, is dwindling. (It is difficult to measure its share of the domestic market because different cities count visitors in different ways. In any case, though only 20 percent of the city's visitors last year were from abroad, they spent 40 percent of the tourist dollars.)
With the dollar enervated, foreign visitors are flocking to destinations all over the United States in record numbers, but of those from New York City's top international markets -- Canada, the United Kingdom, Germany, Japan, France, Italy, Mexico, Spain, Switzerland, Brazil and Venezuela -- more have been going to places like Orlando, Fla., and Las Vegas since 1990. The worry is that when the dollar regains its vigor and the raw visitor numbers recede, New York will be left with a reduced slice of a smaller pie.
This is particularly troubling because tourism is a growth industry, a potential source for gains in jobs and tax revenue. The Travel Industry Association says tourism was responsible for a $22 billion trade surplus for the nation last year. Tourist spending generated $575 million in New York City tax revenue in 1993, the most recent available figures, after a steady upward climb from $513 million in 1990.
All of which points to an increased need for the city to sell itself. "We don't need capital dollars to be stronger," said Jonathan Tisch, president and chief executive of Loews Hotels. "We need promotional dollars. The demand generators are already in place. The Met is here. The Museum of Natural History is here. Broadway is here. Sixty-two thousand hotel rooms are here. The finest restaurants are here. We just have to continually find ways to let people know we're here."
 
The Financing
All Want Benefits; Few Want to Pay
 How to market New York is the subject of the current tangle in the tourist business, and that puts Mr. Morello, who for nine years was a deputy commissioner in the state's Department of Economic Development and hasn't yet moved from Albany, at the heart of the tangle. He arrived at the beginning of what John Linen, a vice-chairman of the American Express Company and chairman of NY94 called "a breakthrough summer" in the city's tourist industry, when private interests and the city government finally began cooperating. The NY94 campaign had just announced a program that tied theaters, stores, restaurants and hotels in a tour package available with a single phone call.
But the convention and visitors bureau had largely been left out of the planning, which is what led Mr. Dyson to make his disparaging "asleep at the wheel" remark.
Mr. Morello has received high marks from those who have worked with him toward unifying the various constituencies, but that does not mean the bureau's problems have disappeared.
As an indication of how fragmented the constituencies remain, two weeks ago the state unveiled its new I Love New York campaign at a breakfast for industry leaders sponsored by the Association for a Better New York. There, Charles A. Gargano, the State Commissioner for Economic Development, and Libby Pataki introduced a 12-minute promotional film, which, among other lapses, showed only a passing image of one Broadway theater. Governor George E. Pataki was featured speaking on behalf of upstate, but not the city. The audience left incensed.
"It could have been Virginia," said one person who spoke on the condition he not be identified.
The Convention and Visitors Bureau is not a city agency; it is a privately run trade organization with 1,500 dues-paying members, mostly New York hotels and restaurants and other businesses that benefit from tourism. It does, however, get the largest chunk of its $7.2 million budget from the city, which Mr. Morello refers to as the bureau's "megaclient," and it also gets about $400,000 from the state. There is the rub: Whom does the bureau serve?
Businesses want a return on their membership fees. The city wants to be assured that the bureau is working for the city as a whole, including the outer boroughs. At long last, there is agreement that the singular goal of bringing more tourists to the city will help everyone. But how to do that with limited resources?
Many industry people point out that other cities, much smaller ones, spend a lot more money supporting tourism. San Francisco's current budget for its visitors bureau is $11.5 million; in Miami it is $12.5 million. Las Vegas, which was built on tourist money, spends a whopping $86.1 million.
Among other things, the New York budget limitations keep the bureau from promoting the city through consumer advertising. For advertising to high-end tourists, said Alan Stillman, president of the New York Restaurant Group. "New York City spends zero. It is penny wise and pound foolish to the nth degree."
One frustration for Mr. Morello is emblematic. Last fall, after years of lobbying, hotel industry executives succeeded in rolling back the state and city room taxes to 13.25 percent plus $2 on rooms costing more than $100, from a national high of 19.25 percent plus $2. But lost with the rollback was the part of the city tax dedicated to the visitors' bureau, leaving the bureau to compete for city allocations in the budget process. Last year, the dedicated tax brought the bureau $4 million. This year's budget allocated $3 million, and for next year the city has proposed a cut.
"So now, with the tax gone, we have an opportunity to market ourselves more aggressively," Mr. Morello said. "But we don't have the resources."
Everyone understands the political issue -- it's tough to explain giving money to tourism when you're taking it from schools, hospitals and social services -- but the complaint is that such a view is shortsighted. Tourism money is not spent, the argument goes; it's invested.
To demonstrate this, Mr. Morello pointed out convention sales. The visitors' bureau's five sales managers booked an average of 64 conventions last year, whose delegates totaled 366,000. That's 73,000 per sales manager, which projects into $291 million in delegate spending per sales manager.
"The city got $2.9 million in tax revenues from each sales manager," Mr. Morello said, "which is a 42-to-1 return on what it costs to employ them."
 
The Strategy
Balancing Leisure And Conventions
 For now, the wrangle is over how to spend the money that is available. And as a result, one persistent criticism of the visitors bureau is that it spends too much soliciting convention business rather than leisure tourism.
"The convention business is important, and it's a wholesale way of going about bringing people here," Mr. Dyson said. "The issue is, What should the city's contribution be going toward? And to what extent are we only replacing what ought to be the business of hotels to do their own marketing?"
Mr. Dyson's attitude rankles many on the NY95 committee, who believe that he is arrogantly trying to control the tourist business from City Hall. He is seeking bids to conduct a marketing survey among travelers to determine why people come here and why they don't, which can then be used to create a new marketing effort.
For his part, Mr. Morello admits that he has concentrated his effort on conventions. Not only is that not surprising, he said, it isn't wrong. After all, leisure visitors to the city are increasing; convention visitors, because of the well-known problems at the Javits Center, because of the construction of newer convention sites in other cities and because of the hotel tax, have been dwindling for years. Now that the hotel tax is gone and there is more talk of trying to solve problems at the Javits Center, there is an opportunity to increase convention business.
Besides, he added, conventions bring in big numbers of visitors who spend, on average, more money than leisure visitors. And, finally, isn't revenue the point?
"We go after conventions because that's where the fish are," he said. "If someone gave me $4 million to go after the consumer market, we would have an effective and visible leisure market effort."
New York has not been the site of Pow Wow since its inauguration in 1969, one indication that the city's interest in selling itself to leisure travelers was, for many years, lax. The convention, virtually everyone agrees, is a crucial step after years of complacency.
"The mistake people make is that we don't pursue the leisure market," Mr. Morello said. "We're just working it at the trade level. That's what Pow Wow is."

HEADLINE: Celebration Is Disputed In Jerusalem

BYLINE: By JOEL GREENBERG 

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, May 27

 BODY:
In this bitterly contested city, preparations to mark 3,000 years since King David made Jerusalem the capital of the biblical Kingdom of Israel have set off a dispute over rights to the city's heritage and its future.
The festivities, scheduled to start in September and to last for 15 months, are officially promoted as an international cultural celebration of a city holy to three faiths -- Judaism, Christianity and Islam. But the planned observances have become a political bone of contention. 
 Palestinian spokesmen and leftist critics in Israel denounce the celebration as an assertion of Israeli claims to Jerusalem during the year in which Israel and the Palestine Liberation Organization are supposed to start negotiating the city's future. King David and his ancient seat of government, the critics argue, have been marshaled to support arguments that Jerusalem should remain Israel's undivided capital. As it happens, Sunday is Jerusalem Day, when Israelis commemorate the capture of East Jerusalem from Jordan in the 1967 war.
Wary of taking sides before the negotiations, the European Union has advised its members not to take part in the 3,000-year anniversary celebrations. The Palestinian Authority has condemned the festivities as an Israeli hijacking of Jerusalem's historical legacy.
The Senate majority leader, Bob Dole, who introduced a bill this month to move the United States Embassy from Tel Aviv to Jerusalem, cited the anniversary as reason to insist on groundbreaking in 1996.
Tensions over Jerusalem were heightened in recent weeks by Israel's confiscation Arab-owned land in the city, mostly for Jewish housing. Israel suspended the move after international protests, but the dispute illustrated how the 1993 agreement in Oslo between Israel and the P.L.O. -- which says talks on Jerusalem should start no later than next May -- has increased the sensitivities surrounding the city.
The anniversary celebrations are no exception.
Conceived several years ago by Teddy Kollek, who was then Mayor of Jerusalem, they were seen as an opportunity to promote the city around the world and generate tourism, investment and development. Culture, he insisted, was his sole intent, not politics.
"I had hoped that somehow the world could identify with it, to give the world the feeling that this is theirs," he said of the anniversary. "But it became politicized."
Jerusalem's current Mayor, Ehud Olmert, and Tourism Minister Uzi Baram acknowledge that the anniversary observances have political overtones, even though they are designed as a cultural tribute.
"Of course we're interested in highlighting the fact that Jerusalem is the capital of Israel, but we say that only as Israel's capital can it be open to all faiths," Mr. Olmert said.
Planners say the celebrations are intended to highlight the city as a universal spiritual center, but they are also "likely to strengthen Jerusalem's role as the historic and political capital of the Jewish people," according to a statement by Jerusalem 3000, the agency organizing the events.
The planned activities, with a budget of $11 million, include music and theater performances, arts festivals, fairs, and conferences on Jerusalem's history. While many events focus on Jerusalem's place in Jewish history and religion, several also explore the city's role in Christianity and Islam.
But Palestinians, who regard East Jerusalem as the capital of their hoped-for state, are conspicuously absent. They say they were not asked to take part. The Israeli organizers reply that approaches were made to Palestinians, but they are staying away for political reasons.
To the Palestinian leadership, the events are "a crude attempt" to exploit Jerusalem's past to promote Israeli claims to the city's future. King David's rule, the Palestinians argue, was only one period in Jerusalem's varied history, and highlighting it in this way is distorting.
"This alleged celebration is without precedent, an act of theft in broad daylight, before the eyes and ears of the world, of the features and identity of the holiest city in the world," said a statement issued by the Palestinian Authority.
"Israel has no right to appoint itself the inheritor of the human legacy and accumulated achievements of thousands of years, and to seize them on fictitious grounds that have no proof and are refuted by historical facts."
Mr. Olmert, a member of the rightist Likud party, says he makes "no apologies" for Jerusalem 3000.
"Do we have to overlook such a historic date just because there is a debate about Jerusalem?" he said. "We can argue about the future, but why can't we celebrate the past?"

HEADLINE: Conservatives Tug at Endangered Species Act

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 27

 BODY:
As Congress reviews the Endangered Species Act, the most important debate at the moment is not between the law's defenders and its critics, but among conservatives of different stripes quarreling over how much to revise it.
One group, led by Senator Slade Gorton of Washington, has worked closely with representatives of large industries like the timber and paper companies to devise an approach that would make regulations under the law more flexible and ease the way for companies to exploit natural resources. It would leave considerable power in the hands of the Secretary of the Interior, whose agency would continue to control conservation plans for protected species using Government regulations. 
 But in the House, another approach is evolving, one that would practically do away with Federal regulations and rely instead on financial incentives to encourage landowners to preserve and enhance vanishing habitat voluntarily. The faction pressing this approach is appealing to advocates for the rights of small property owners and has gained the support of some prominent conservative natural resource economists.
Supporters of the more ambitious approach said that the law's most vociferous critics were on their side. But Senator Gorton said he believed his approach, which has been strongly condemned by liberals, will gain appeal if a more extreme alternative emerges on the right.
"The national environmental organizations exhausted their entire supply of adjectives in cussing out my bill, and they aren't going to have any left when they see a really radical proposal," he said. "Before the battle is over, mine will look pretty much middle of the road, which is what it is supposed to be."
As the struggle among conservatives plays out, one person to watch for clues to the outcome could be Speaker Newt Gingrich. Although he is likely to let Representative Don Young, an Alaska Republican who is chairman of the Resources Committee, steer the bill forward in the House, Mr. Gingrich was a sponsor of the existing law and professes a deep interest in conservation policies.
"I am very committed to bringing through the House a bill that is economically rational, biologically correct and respects the property rights of individuals," Mr. Gingrich said at a hearing on the bill this week. "That is a very tough, very high standard, and it is going to take a lot of work to get there."
Although he emphasized his sympathy for property owners who object to the law's strictures, he reminded the committee: "On the other hand, we also have to recognize that there are enormous interests that we have as human beings in maintaining biological diversity."
But Representative John Shadegg, an Arizona Republican who is helping to draft a bill that would end mandatory conservation regulations, said he believed species could be protected in new and imaginative ways, including as little regulation as possible.
"There is the typical, think inside the box, regulatory approach," he said, referring to Senator Gorton's bill. "The other school is thinking outside the box, recognizing to a slightly greater degree the interests other than the big interest groups that have already signed on to the Gorton bill."
Mr. Shadegg supports a statement of principles issued earlier this month by a coalition of small property-rights groups.
Among other things, these groups want the new law to include the following changes:
*The primary responsibility for conservation of animals and plants would be reserved to the states.
*Federal conservation efforts would rely entirely on voluntary, incentive-based programs to enlist the cooperation of America's landowners.
*Federal conservation efforts would encourage conservation through commerce, including the private propagation of animals and plants.
The Competitive Enterprise Institute, an influential conservative policy group that favors deregulation, has endorsed this approach. Calling the existing law "fundamentally flawed," Ike C. Sugg, the group's land use policy fellow, testified that "no amount of tinkering around the margins will solve its intrinsic problems."
"I don't agree with that," Senator Gorton said. "There is clearly a place for Government regulation in connection with values that are not easily reduced to dollars."
Representative Richard W. Pombo, a California Republican who is heading a task force on endangered species, said he thought that when the group presented a bill to the House Resources Committee this summer it would probably recommend "more of a fundamental change in the act," along the lines of the voluntary approach that Mr. Shadegg has proposed.
"Say that a rancher enters into a management agreement on his property, to benefit a species," said Mr. Pombo, himself a fourth-generation rancher and an ardent foe of the species protection law. "One of the incentives that could be placed out there is that the inheritance tax on passing the ranch from him to his child could be delayed as long as the property remains in a habitat conservation plan."
The advocates of a nonregulatory approach concede that it might cost a lot of money, but say they hope to pay for it by selling federally owned land to ranchers and the timber industry. And they point out that it would protect the rights of property owners without triggering another form of compensation favored by anti-regulatory conservatives -- the payment for the perceived loss of value, or "takings," of private land.
Of course, both of the two conservative camps are producing proposals that are abhorrent to environmentalists and their allies in the Clinton Administration, which has argued that the Endangered Species Act needs little change at all, beyond administrative changes to improve its use of scientific research and to encourage cooperation from private landowners.
Interior Secretary Bruce Babbitt, said when Senator Gorton introduced his bill that it "can be summed up in seven words: it will repeal the Endangered Species Act."
Mr. Gorton, for his part, says that many of the administrative improvements that the Clinton Administration favors are compatible with the legislation he has drafted.
But with Republicans determined to change the law and with many conservative Democrats joining the effort, the voices of the Administration and the environmental groups are largely drowned out for the time being.

HEADLINE: Budget Package Stalls Over Tax Cuts

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, May 27

 BODY:
With most pieces of the state budget falling into place early this morning, the legislature found itself stymied by the one issue that has haunted Connecticut politics for the last four years: the income tax.
In an all-night session that adjourned shortly after sunrise, both houses of the General Assembly approved a budget for the next two years that would increase spending at a rate lower than inflation. But hopes for a final package today faded as lawmakers split over income tax cuts. 
 The dispute continues a legislative battle that dates from 1991, when the state first enacted an income tax after months of bitter deadlock. The clash has defined the political landscape here, pitting liberal Democrats against their more conservative party members, as well as the Republicans.
Although Speaker Thomas D. Ritter, a Democrat of Hartford, has often been credited with healing the rift in his own party, the wound was reopened with the most recent round of budget deliberations.
Gov. John G. Rowland and his fellow Republicans, with support from some Democrats, have proposed cuts in the income tax that would be phased in over the next five years. In turn, a majority of Democrats have opposed any cuts beyond the next two years.
This has left the Democratic-controlled House and the Republican-controlled Senate at odds, a situation that must be resolved by June 7, the legislature's adjournment date, if the state is to have a budget on schedule.
Leaders of both parties have agreed that there will be an income tax cut of $100 million in the fiscal year starting July 1, 1996, as well as a property tax credit of $100 million to be applied to the income tax.
The disagreement is over proposed cuts in the income tax for each of the next three fiscal years, from July 1, 1997 through June 30, 2000. Under the Republican-supported proposal, income tax revenues could be reduced about $317 million by the final year. The state expects to collect about $2.7 billion in income tax revenue in the 1996-97 fiscal year.
State Representative Annette W. Carter, a Democrat from Hartford who helped lead the fight against the future tax cuts, remarked after the session, "Some of us bled hard to get that tax in place, and to now have to remove that -- well, I just don't like it.
"It was a good idea then, and it's a good idea now."
And State Representative Moira K. Lyons, a Democrat of Stamford and the majority leader, said it was risky to make a commitment to tax cuts beyond 1997 when it was not clear how the state would pay for them. "Both sides of the aisle want to give tax reductions," she said. "The difference is in the reality of doing it. We need to know that we have the money to keep our promises."
But in the debate early this morning, Representative Robert M. Ward of North Branford, the Republican minority leader, rejected such an argument. "The budget is not done well until we further expand income tax reductions over the next four years," Mr. Ward said. "Frankly, I don't think it's deep enough."
The income tax dispute interrupted what had been a surprisingly harmonious session in the House. Although some Democrats had expressed skepticism Friday night about a plan to sell interest in the state lottery to raise money for a tax cut, no one objected to the measure when it was discussed on the floor.
Indeed, speaker after speaker rose in the House to praise the bipartisan spirit behind the budget and to say it represented sacrifice on both sides.
The budget proposes spending $9.8 billion in the 1996 fiscal year, up 2.6 percent from this year, and then $10.1 billion in 1996-97, an increase of 3.7 percent. It would be the smallest budget increase in 25 years, and would represent half the growth of the previous two years.
To do that, the budget includes some tough cuts. For instance, it proposes reducing the rate structure that reimburses nursing homes to $809 million next year, saving more than $71 million. Benefits to families on welfare would be cut by $55 million from this year's projected spending, down to $314 million.
Other areas fared better. A job placement and training program for welfare recipients had its budget doubled to $16 million from about $8.5 million. An array of programs for people who have AIDS, whose financing Mr. Rowland was going to virtually eliminate, had almost all of their money restored.
The House voted 143 to 6 in favor of the budget at 4:55 A.M., drawing support from both parties. The tax vote in the House a little more than an hour later passed 126 to 21, again with bipartisan backing.
The Senate also approved the state budget this morning, in a 19-to-17 vote that split along party lines, with Republicans supporting and Democrats opposing. The Senate has yet to take up the tax package, though the Republican leadership there has said it is committed to five years of income tax cuts.

PUB-DATE: May 28, 1995,

SECTION: Section 4; Page 10; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 698 words

HEADLINE: Budget Test for Mr. Clinton

 BODY:
Except for his tactically inept proposal to balance the budget in 10 years, President Clinton has played the budget game pretty cannily for a person who lacks the votes to impose his will on Congress. Debate driven by the warring ambitions of Bob Dole, Phil Gramm and Newt Gingrich has brought the brutality of the Republican plan into full view.
But voters will eventually demand more of their President than deft tactics or a clever re-election strategy. Mr. Clinton will have to present a principled reason why they should care if he wins re-election. He must answer then, definitively, whether he agrees with Speaker Gingrich et al that the budget must be balanced soon and, if so, at what pain. 
 On this score, Mr. Clinton's best campaign plan might also be the most honest approach for a Democrat. He can assure voters that he is not Republican lite and that he believes deficit reduction, though important, is not the only fiscal policy that matters. Balancing the budget by pounding working people or eviscerating public investment makes no moral or economic sense.
The purpose of eliminating deficits is to reduce Federal borrowing that siphons money from corporate investment. But it accomplishes little to pump up private investment by knocking down public investment -- in infrastructure, training, education and research. Mr. Gingrich may see little outside the military and law enforcement that the Government does well. But most Americans are not so doctrinaire. Nor do they believe the world's richest country must pound its poor to balance its books.
America can have declining deficits, compassion for the poor and public investment. It would take sizable cuts in military hardware and corporate subsidies coupled with reform of the health care industry to achieve these goals. What has been missing is Presidential leadership to show the public that the Gingrich/Gramm highway will not lead to the twin destinations of a fair, healthy economy and an effective government.
Mr. Clinton's budget raises Pentagon spending and shies away from reforming Medicare and Medicaid -- the Federal programs most responsible for escalating deficits. He protected corporate subsidies, even though his Secretary of Labor brought the wasteful giveaways to the nation's attention. Political survival forced Mr. Clinton to meet Republicans part way by proposing to cut taxes by about $70 billion, money better spent on deficit reduction or education and training programs.
The Republicans, to their credit, delivered on their campaign promise to balance the budget in seven years. But their evasions are still stunning. They propose huge cuts in Medicare, but fail to propose specific reforms of the program or of the health care system to achieve the savings. Without reform, the budget cuts would ruin health care for the elderly and saddle everyone else with steeply higher insurance premiums. With reform, the G.O.P. would achieve savings, but much too slowly to balance the budget in seven years. Here's where Mr. Clinton's brief flirtation with a longer horizon makes sense. Done correctly, entitlement reform will rack up large savings, but the deficit impact might be years away.
The Republicans sought to purchase votes by promising huge tax cuts -- $350 billion in the House plan, implicity $170 billion in the Senate plan. They are designed to do little for investment and growth but a lot to trigger consumption that the economy does not need. To pay for these benighted tax cuts, the G.O.P. would hack away at the country's science and technology infrastructure.
Mr. Clinton has identified these Republican missteps, but said nothing about how he would do better. Even with his limited political strength, he can tip the balance toward sanity and humanity. He can write a more honest budget that reflects what he believes the country truly needs. He can veto Republican excesses, especially those that threaten to do irreversible damage, for example, to welfare programs and universities. Mr. Clinton still has the chance to enter the fray on the side of sensible deficit reduction and humane spending of whatever money Congress taxes and borrows.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL NOTEBOOK;
'Small-Market' Angels Enter the Disney Fold

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Baseball officials were delighted when the Walt Disney Company recently added 25 percent of the California Angels to a stable that already included Mickey Mouse and the Mighty Ducks of Anaheim. In a time of low morale and suffering economy, the officials viewed Disney's interest and entry into their game as a highly positive development.
"With all the negative stuff, here's one of the premier companies in the world very aggressively wanting to acquire a major league club," one official said. 
 The Angels themselves reacted positively to the development. No sooner did the club announce that Disney would become one-fourth owner and the managing partner than its players reeled off a seven-game winning streak and soared to an 18-9 record, matching the franchise's best start since 1978.
Questions long have hovered over the Angels, who have never reached the World Series despite constant efforts to do so in the lifetime of their revered owner, Gene Autry. And the biggest question is how did the Angels become a low-revenue, or so-called small-market, club?
The club's economic status was signaled in Kohler, Wis., in August 1993, when owners began their debate over a new revenue-sharing plan. Jackie Autry, the Angels' functioning chief operator, attended a meeting of high-revenue clubs the first day but joined low-revenue clubs for the rest of the meetings.
How could a team in the intensely populated area just south of Los Angeles be linked economically with teams in Pittsburgh, Milwaukee and Seattle? The answer is that classification has nothing to do with the size of the team's market; it's the size of the team's income that counts.
Over the years, the Angels have made questionable decisions on free agents and other personnel matters and have managed to operate the club in a way that has resulted in a steady decline in attendance, from a record 2.8 million in the division-championship season of 1982 to barely over 2 million in each of the last two full seasons.
Still, if this year's team can continue to win, it could mark something of a turnaround in the Angels' decision making. For one thing, they kept Chili Davis instead of letting him become a free agent. For another, they traded their young center fielder, Chad Curtis, for Tony Phillips.
Davis and Phillips are now two of the team's most potent hitters. Entering last night's game, they each had hit six home runs. Davis was leading the team with 26 runs batted in and Phillips was leading with 25 runs scored. Davis was also leading the team with a .386 batting average.
The most startling development, though, has been the Angels' pitching. The staff has knocked nearly two runs off the 5.42 earned run average the team had last year. And contributing to that improvement have been three veteran starters who were available to anyone.
General Manager Bill Bavasi signed Shawn Boskie to a minor league contract during the winter, then plucked Scott Sanderson and Mike Bielecki from the Players Association's free-agent camp in Homestead, Fla., all for a total of $550,000 in base salaries.
"Maybe this is blasphemy, but the union did a great job with that camp in Florida," Bavasi said.
The Angels, however, could develop a serious problem. Boskie, Bielecki and Sanderson are not in the same class as Mark Langston and Chuck Finley. If they suddenly stop being effective, the rotation could sprout large holes.
The bullpen, though, will have no ninth-inning hole as long as Lee Smith is around. Smith was another of Bavasi's sound decisions. The career saves leader, at the age of 37, continues to save victories. He had a league-leading 11 saves heading into the weekend and had not allowed a run in 12 games.
Bavasi, the lone member of a noted baseball family now working in the major leagues, has shown enough ability in 17 months on the job to retain it under the Disney people. On the other hand, Jackie Autry will remain very much involved, an official familiar with the club's plans said, and it is she who has overseen the descent into small-market status.
In gaining a one-fourth interest in the team and the option to purchase the rest of the club six months after Gene Autry's death, Disney beat out Peter Ueberroth, the former commissioner. And Disney did so in part by agreeing to pick up some of the losses this year.
Losses the Angels will have. But the combination of an improved team and an improved operation -- the Mighty Ducks have been a losing team in two years of existence but have basically sold out -- will send the Angels back toward their rightful place on the economic map.

 
 
Stronger in the West

 
 During spring training, Bill Bavasi said, people in the American League West "knew our division would be better than last year, but that's an easy thing to say because we couldn't get any worse."
Only one-fifth of the season has been played, but it has been a revealing segment of the season. Not only is the A.L. West better than last year, but it has been better than the A.L. East, which had been purported to be baseball's strongest division.
Last weekend, for the first time since the majors went to three-division play last season, all four A.L. West teams won on the same day and all four had .500 records or better. They maintained their winning records through the week.
Even more impressively, the Western Four entered the weekend with a collective 19-12 record against the Eastern Five.

 
 
Cuban Should Go Fast in Draft

 
 Players from the Dominican Republic, Venezuela, Nicaragua, Panama, Mexico, Japan and Korea can be signed by any major league club without going through the amateur draft. But Cubans in this country, either as defectors or otherwise, are considered residents of the United States and, in the view of the commissioner's office, which is the one that counts, are subject to the draft, which will take place Thursday.
Ariel Prieto, who left Cuba with that government's blessing, will be in the draft, but the 26-year-old pitcher isn't expected to last long.
"He's the best prospect to come out of Cuba," said Omar Minaya, the Texas Rangers' director of international scouting. "He probably will be a first-round choice. He could go as high as third or fourth."
While awaiting the draft, Prieto is pitching for the Palm Springs, Calif., Suns of the independent Western League. A teammate is reliever Euclides Rojas, who defected from Cuba by raft. Scouts do not consider him as strong a prospect as Prieto.
"Prieto is as interesting as Nomo," one scout said, referring to Hideo Nomo, the Los Angeles Dodgers' corkscrew pitcher from Japan. "He might pitch this year."

 
 
Some Fast Second-Guessing

 
 The Dodgers let Brett Butler, their center fielder for four years, leave as a free agent, saying they had good young players to play the outfield. Last week they traded for Roberto Kelly, put him in center field and returned Raul Mondesi to right.
The Chicago White Sox passed up Mickey Tettleton as a free-agent designated hitter, signing Chris Sabo instead. They explained that Sabo struck out less than Tettleton. They were right. Sabo struck out once every 6 at-bats, Tettleton once every 4. But Tettleton has hit 7 home runs and driven in 18 runs to 1 and 8 for Sabo. The White Sox dropped Sabo from their roster last week when they added John Kruk.
Baseball club owners like to point to the National Football League as one of the leagues that has a salary cap. But they don't talk about the N.F.L.'s revenue-sharing practices. Long before the league had a salary cap, it was sharing a vast majority of its revenues, national and local. Last week it enhanced the sharing program, creating a pool of $18 million a year for the next four years to be shared by teams whose revenues fall below the league average. The baseball owners refuse to enhance their revenue-sharing plan until the players agree to a cap or a luxury tax on payrolls, maintaining the disparity between rich and poor clubs.

HEADLINE: At Indy 500, Parity Forges Way Into Driver's Seat

BYLINE: By JOSEPH SIANO 

DATELINE: INDIANAPOLIS, May 27

 BODY:
The 1995 Indianapolis 500 may end up belonging to the mice that roared. Among them, Scott Brayton speaks the loudest.
Brayton pushed aside the waffles he was eating at his boss's race shop one morning this weekend, and recalled when he realized he was racing in the wrong groove. 
 "I was coming off a sixth-place finish at Indy in 1993," he said, "and I was six seconds behind Emerson Fittipaldi, who won. I could feel it, taste it, sense it. But I was still a long way away. Because I knew I was trying to compete against the superpowers of racing with a little money."
So he came to John Menard's garage, a few miles north of the Indianapolis Motor Speedway, a detour that led to the pole position for the 1995 Indianapolis 500, and with it perhaps his best chance ever for victory.
Brayton, who had spent most of his career in mediocre cars, struggling through season after forgettable season, decided to focus his efforts where they meant the most. "The only parity that exists in racing is money and talent," Brayton said. "I felt I would go with a team that spends a lot of money on a little bit of racing."
Without knowing it, Brayton had pretty much summarized the philosophy of his boss, John Menard, who is spending a lot of money to win just one race: the Indianapolis 500. Menard's strong showing here this month (another of his drivers, Arie Luyendyk, is right next to Brayton in the middle of the front row) is just one of the factors that has turned this Indy 500 upside down.
Thanks to the influx of new teams, a new tire company and a new engine from Honda, it's not just superpowers like Penske Racing that are getting sand kicked in their visors. Even Bobby Rahal, the 1986 race winner, and Danny Sullivan, the 1985 champion, qualified in the bottom half of the lineup.
Roger Penske's team, which dominated the race last year, came back this year with a car that had a handling problem. A few years ago, he might have been able to slip into the race anyway. But the traffic around here has gotten a lot faster this year, and Penske could never catch up.
Instead, Brayton, Luyendyk and the third front-row qualifier, Scott Goodyear, have set the race's theme as well as its pace: Indy for the unemployed.
Like Brayton, both Goodyear and Luyendyk are former full-time race drivers who have only one race on their calendar this season -- the one that pays the most and means the most.
"It really wasn't hard to make a decision to come and drive here," said Luyendyk, who won the 1990 Indy 500 but whose career didn't take off after that. "Last year, I ran all season and was struggling financially, and it makes for a very long year and a very long month of May."
Menard, who owns 105 home-improvement stores in the Midwest, probably has won the race before the green flag ever drops on Sunday. For one whole month, Menard has had pictures of his cars (which, of course, carry his store's logo on them) in newspapers and on television newscasts throughout his market area. And, as he pointed out this week, "Memorial Day is our busiest weekend."
Even Goodyear, who won the only other 500-mile race on the Indy-car schedule last year in an otherwise terrible season, has learned to like working one month a year.
"I don't know if I've minded not being racing the rest of the year because I've been focusing on this event and I'm happy to be here," he said.
Why not, when his Honda engine appears to be the only one capable of running with Brayton and Luyendyk. And most experts give the Honda V-8 a much better chance to finish the race than Menard's home-built V-6 engines, which gulp fuel too quickly and are easy to over-rev and damage.
Honda's new-found muscle is another factor contributing to the rise of the unknowns at Indy. After last year's qualifying debacle, in which Rahal had to abandon the Honda as too puny, almost no team wanted to touch the engine this season.
Steve Horne, however, was restarting an Indy team after the one that he and Rahal won the 1986 race with broke up. Horne saw the Honda as an opportunity.
"The key thing in any form of racing is making the right decisions long before you go to the race track," said Horne, who owns the cars driven by Goodyear and Andre Ribeiro, a Brazilian rookie on the outside of the fourth row. "This year we made some very smart decisions and they all fell our way. They could have fallen other people's way, but they didn't see the potential."
Among those who didn't see it is Rahal, who is starting behind both Honda-powered cars after switching to a different engine this year.
Horne is also confident in his choice of Scott Goodyear to drive this race.
"He knows how to win 500-mile races," Horne said, referring to Goodyear's two victories in the 500-mile race in Brooklyn, Mich. "And he's had a great deal of success at Indy. There's no question he has a burning desire to do well and get back with a full-time ride."
When Penske's two drivers, Al Unser Jr. and Emerson Fittipaldi, failed to make the race, it meant that the winners of four of the last six Indy races would be missing from the lineup. And, for the first time since 1962, the Unser family, which has nine Indianapolis 500 victories to its credit, will not be represented here.
That, along with Mario Andretti's retirement and Nigel Mansell's short-lived return to Formula One, has left a very unfamiliar-looking starting lineup this year.
If a new star comes out of this most-unsettled Indianapolis 500, he is more likely than not to be a non-American. Only 15 of the 33 drivers are American citizens; there are six rookies (all foreigners), and of the seven drivers making only their second Indy 500 start, only two -- Bryan Herta and Scott Sharp -- are Americans.
And if Penske -- or any other member of the Indy establishment -- thinks 1996 will be any easier, he should consider this: Dan Gurney, who built the cars that won the Indianapolis 500 in 1968, 1973 and 1975, will be back next year with his own cars, powered by yet another new corporate player at the Indianapolis Motor Speedway, Toyota.
The traffic will keep getting tougher on the roadway built to handle only 33 cars.
 
PIT STOPS
 Although the race Sunday is expected to be a wide-open affair, the weather may spoil the show. The forecast for Sunday called for mostly cloudy conditions with a 50 percent chance of rain, with temperatures in the 80's. The forecast for Monday, the rain date, is not much better, with scattered showers predicted.

HEADLINE: The How of the Dow (and the When and the What)

BYLINE: By DYLAN LOEB MCCLAIN 

 BODY:
In the abstract, the Dow Jones industrial average doesn't seem like a promising barometer of the stock market. It contains just 30 stocks out of the on the New York Stock Exchange, and this small sample is biased, too: The 30 Dow stocks are uniformly large, established enterprises, the bluest of the blue chips, accounting for 25 percent of the exchange's value despite their small numbers.
Nevertheless, the Dow has succeeded where it counts -- in real life. The nation's oldest continuously published stock index, the Dow has become synonymous with movements in the markets. When the radio says stocks fell 10 points, it means the Dow. 
 Ironically, part of the Dow's prestige comes from its very focus on a small, elite group of companies. Part is historical happenstance. And part is performance. Although the Dow has plenty of critics, it has proved to be a fairly accurate barometer of market direction.
How much do you know about this Wall Street fixture? Take this quiz and find out. DYLAN LOEB MCCLAIN
 
Who is this man?
 
None other than Charles H. Dow, one-half of the team that gave their names to the industrial average. The other is Edward D. Jones, and together they founded Dow Jones, the publishing company that runs the index.
 
What's odd about the Dow?
 
It is a "price weighted" index. The higher a share's price, the more it counts in the calculation. Many other indexes, such as the S.& P. 500, are "capital weighted," in which both share price and share number are taken into account. In the latter kind of index, changes in the price of a $10 stock with 6 million shares will count double that of changes in a $10 stock with just 3 million shares.
 
The Dow is at least 100 years old. True or false?
False -- by a hair. Ignoring a predecessor index composed mostly of railroads, the Dow debuted in 1896. It contained 12 companies, most of which sold commodities. The Dow was expanded to 20 stocks in 1916 and, in 1928, it reached its current complement of 30. But while these numbers are constant, the companies are not. They can be added or removed.
 
Who is the Dow's gatekeeper?
Journalists! The editors of the Wall Street Journal determine which stocks to let in or kick out. The factors include a company's size and its economic importance, but there are no ironclad criteria.
 
Which is the largest company not included in the Dow?
None other than the fifth largest company in the United States -- Wal-Mart, which has a market value of nearly $59 billion. Here are the next largest companies that haven't made the cut:
 
Are there any mutual funds that invest only in the 30 Dow stocks?
 
Just one. The tiny ASM Fund, a no-load with just $5.7 million in assets, is 4 years old and has a $1000 minimum for initial purchases (800-445-2763). Index funds themselves are fairly common -- Morningstar counts 111 equity funds that invest in the S.& P. 500, the Russell 2000 and other indexes -- but the Dow's small size probably limits its attractiveness as an index investment.
 
Riddle: How is the Dow like a mirror?
It reflects changes in the United States. Scan the Dow rosters below, and follow the country's move from a commodity, to an industrial, to a more diversified economy.
 
The Dow falls 90 points. Should an investor panic?
 
Trick question. The missing element is when. The Dow fell almost that much in the week ended May 19, but with its level at more than 4,400 these days, that drop represented just a 2 percent decline. But when the market lost just 70 points in the crash of October 1929, panic sensibly ensued. The reason: the Dow's pre-crash level was just -- so a 70-point fall meant the Dow lost more than 20 percent.
 
Are there better indexes than the Dow?
 
It depends on the user's needs. Given the Dow's sole focus on large companies, some investors may be more interested in the Russell 2000, an index of 2,000 smaller stocks, or the S.& P. 500, a mix of 500 companies of diverse size.
The Dow has fewer stocks than any of the world's major stock indexes. True or false?
 
False. Germany's DAX index also has just 30 stocks. But many other indexes have more. Japan's Nikkei has 225 stocks, for instance, and Britain's FTSE-100 has 100.
 
Why is the Dow's daily high not a high?
The Dow's daily high is calculated by using the highest price of each of its component stocks during the day. But stocks do not reach their daily highs at the same time. So the Dow never actually reaches its published high.
 
Does anyone earn money from the Dow?
 
You bet. Dow Jones charges some media organization, investment professionals and others a fee to use the Dow. The fees vary according to whether the index is supplied in real time, delayed time or at the end of the day.
 
What are the most recent additions and deletions to the index?
 
In 1991, the Dow's high priests removed USX, Avistar and Primerica from the index, and replaced them with Disney, Caterpillar and J.P. Morgan.
 
What's the significance of 0.36143882?
 
It's the number that enables you to calculate the Dow yourself. Just add up the closing prices of the 30 Dow stocks - listed above - and divide them by 0.36143882. Easy! (How the mathematicians arrived at 0.36143882 is not easy. At first, the price totals were simply divided by the number of stocks in the index. But, through the years, as companies were added and removed and stocks split, the need to maintain the index's integrity led to its keepers to adjust the divisor.)

Graph shows monthly closing prices of the Dow Jones Industrial average from 1915 to 1995 (Sources: Datastream, Birinyl Associates, Fortune Magazine) $14.375 on Friday, the lowest share price in the Dow, and I.B.M. closed at $97.25, the highest price. The indifferent state is Vermont, where the government is not allowed to buy any stocks that cost more than $11.50 a share.

HEADLINE: SOAPBOX;
When Home Is a Housing Project: Two Former Residents Remember

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Going Home" captured the mixed feelings of shame, pride and hope that I experienced growing up in Brooklyn's Red Hook projects. 
 But I did find it troubling that the writer, Tony Marcano, linked the demise of the James Monroe project to an influx of welfare recipients. They brought in a "defeatist attitude," he said, and were not understood by the original tenants.
As I remember, the lines between the working poor and dependent poor were never so clearly defined. Working-class residents understood all too well that any misfortune, like a fire or a medical emergency, could force them to apply for welfare.
In fact, my father received welfare for nearly two years during the 1970's recession, after his employer left the city.
The difference between my family and those families that lived from generation to generation on welfare was this: My Dad, the son of two generations of Southern Baptist preachers and laborers, knew welfare was something to sustain the family until he found a new job. He knew there was a light at the end of the tunnel, while the welfare families had learned to live in the tunnel.
My family didn't move from Red Hook until 1989 -- a year after I graduated from college -- so I witnessed the beginning of the crack wars. Shootings and muggings became commonplace and led to the general sense that the community was being held hostage. At least two generations of working poor and welfare families co-existed, sharing the values of survival, before crack invaded the projects.
 
CAROLYN R. BUTTS
Brooklyn

HEADLINE: Don't Let Sharp Operators Near Social Security;
Scrap the System

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I applaud Senators Bob Kerrey and Alan K. Simpson for trying to focus attention on the Social Security problem (Op-Ed, May 23). Unfortunately, their solutions don't go far enough, and they aren't being completely honest about the problem in the first place. 
 Social Security is an income transfer program, not a consumption program or a savings program. Each generation transfers a portion of its income to the previous generation. Benefits are determined by what each generation decides it will provide. Payroll taxes are levied on all workers to pay for these benefits.
The funds received by Social Security are invested in United States Government debt. United States debt instruments provide an insufficient rate of return to compensate for the demographic changes that worry the Senators. Generational warfare looms behind the demographics. Older Americans wrongly assume that Social Security is both an entitlement and a retirement-pension plan, and younger Americans believe that they will have huge future tax burdens and no benefits. It's a no-win situtation.
Even more troubling, Social Security surpluses, which resulted from the last effort to "save" the program, are being counted against the Federal deficit, thus understating the deficit dramatically.
If Senators Kerrey and Simpson worry about Social Security firestorms, they haven't seen anything like what will happen when constituents realize that the cuts being suggested by Congress are a drop in the bucket. This is the big truth about Social Security and the Federal deficit that no one in Congress seems to want to face.
As the Senators suggest, we should encourage retirement planning and savings. The best way to do so is to establish individual retirement accounts, fully invested in long-term growth stocks, and fund them with the current employee-employer contributions to Social Security.
To create a new system, we must pick a date, say, 2000. Contributions must remain mandatory. The Government should budget a percentage contribution equal to half the unemployment rate (assume investment compounding will make up the difference) to cover those who are unemployed.
The system's funds should be placed in a passively managed index fund. The accounts can be administered by the private sector (mutual fund companies and the like) through a competitive bidding process.
Finally, the Social Security system should be ended altogether in 30 years.
This new system could provide all Americans with a much better level of retirement security than Social Security does, dramatically increase the United States savings rate and eliminate an unnecessary Government bureaucracy.
If the Senators want to make Social Security a "savings" program, perhaps they could begin saving the time wasted on reforming an income transfer program and devote that time to a frank and honest debate about how to reduce the Federal deficit and our national debt. This would truly be a savings for all.
 
PAUL W. MEEK
Arlington, Va., May 23, 1995

HEADLINE: Brother's Tax Problem Puts Home in Jeopardy

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
AT first glance, Maria Merlino's fight to keep her Philadelphia house seems like just another case of faulty tax and trust planning.
In a finding filed this month, Judge Edna G. Parker of Federal Tax Court recounts how the home was bought and transferred among Merlino family members, and what the flaw was in the transactions. But in truth, if Maria Merlino's brother, Joseph, had just paid taxes on his proceeds from an armored car robbery, she wouldn't be battling the Internal Revenue Service now. 
 And unmentioned anywhere by the Judge is that Joseph Merlino is the son of Salvatore (Chuckie) Merlino, the former underboss of the Scarfo crime family who is now in the witness protection program, according to the Philadelphia police. The younger Merlino is described by Federal prosecutors as engaged in an especially bloody war for control of rackets in Philadelphia and Atlantic City, a war they said included a wild rush-hour shooting on a freeway two years ago. The intended target, according to the Federal Bureau of Investigation, was John Stanfa, who is about to stand trial for murder and racketeering and was identified in an indictment last year as successor to Nicodemo (Little Nicky) Scarfo in the Philadelphia mob.
For all the people who think their family financial matters are messy, try following the Merlinos' tale. In 1986, Ms. Merlino and her brother agreed to buy a family home for themselves and their mother, Rita. Ms. Merlino did not qualify for a mortgage because she was unemployed and had a poor credit history, according to the judge's finding. The family paid $100,000 in cash, and arranged a $25,000 mortgage to cover the balance of the $125,000 purchase price.
The home was purchased in the name of Joseph Merlino. On the advice of a lawyer, Fred Levin, title was transferred to the mother and then to a trust with Joseph as trustee for Maria and another sister. In December 1988, Joseph Merlino gave his one-third share of the trust to his sister, Maria. The fair market value of the house was $130,000, and the title was conveyed for $1.
Here's the rub. Mr. Merlino and two associates stole $352,150 in an armored car robbery in 1987. In 1990, he was convicted, which made him liable for income tax on his share of the loot. Over the years, the amount of tax he owes has swelled because of penalties and interest to $139,964.
Judge Parker found that the title to the house was transferred fraudulently to keep the house beyond the reach of Federal authorities. Because Joseph Merlino never filed a tax return for 1987, his tax liability arose on April 15, 1988 -- before the house was transferred to his sister. And because she did not pay her brother for the house, Judge Parker concluded that the transfer was fraudulent.
In a partial victory for Ms. Merlino, however, the judge held that she was not liable for the full $139,964. Her liability is $43,000, equivalent to the value of Mr. Merlino's one-third stake in the house at the time of the transfer, plus interest.
Mark E. Cedrone, Ms. Merlino's lawyer, said he was pleased that the judge limited his client's liability but would still seek a reversal. "Her brother didn't even know he was a suspect in the armored car robbery when he transferred his interest in the house," Mr. Cedrone said, arguing that the transfer could not have been fraudulent. "And my client is no Mafia princess. She's goes to work every morning as a legal secretary at one of the most prominent law firms in Philadelphia. The finding is really unfair."

CORRECTION-DATE: June 4, 1995, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article last Sunday about the Internal Revenue Service's efforts to hold a Philadelphia woman liable for her brother's unpaid Federal income tax on the proceeds from a robbery misstated the whereabouts of their father, Salvatore Merlino, whom the authorities have identified as a member of organized crime. He is not in a witness protection program; he is in a state prison in Pennsylvania.

LENGTH: 304 words

HEADLINE: Business Group Backs 3d Harlem Line Track

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER 

 BODY:
A PROPOSAL by the Metropolitan Transportation Authority to construct 3.6 miles of new track between the Mount Vernon West train station and the Crestwood station has been endorsed by the board of directors of the Westchester Business Partnership.
The Westchester Business Partnership is a nonprofit joint venture of the public and private sectors with a goal of fostering a positive business climate and enhancing the county's economy. 
 "A fine mass-transit system is crucial to Westchester's economic stability and growth," said George J. Delaney, president of the Westchester Business Partnership and a vice president for Con Edison in Westchester. "A third track on a section of the Metro-North Harlem line would make train service more efficient. Without this third track, Metro-North cannot meet its projected increase in peak-hour ridership."
The plan to build a third track on the Harlem Line is the main element in a $41 million, four-year project proposed by Metro-North, the M.T.A. division that runs Westchester's commuter trains. The authority already owns the right of way for the tracks.
The 76.6-mile Harlem line extends from Grand Central Terminal in Manhattan through the Bronx and Westchester and Putnam Counties and ends in Dover Plains in Dutchess County. The line is the only Metro-North line that narrows to two tracks and eventually one track from four tracks.
According to Metro-North, the section between Mount Vernon West and Crestwood is the most densely traveled section on the system. The inbound track, for example, carries 21,070 riders on 35 trains at peak hours in the morning.
The third-track project will allow Metro-North to add trains and change its mix of local and express trains, both for commuters to Manhattan and reverse commuters to Westchester. PENNY SINGER
