LENGTH: 4520 words

HEADLINE: New Jersey: Exploring the Shore

BYLINE: By BILL KENT 

 BODY:
The reviving economy, a weak dollar abroad, and millions of dollars spent on storm-ravaged coastline, beach replenishment and other improvements promise a banner year for the Jersey shore, where, despite crowds, congestion, tedious tolls and beach fees, happiness is still a bit of sand, a splash of surf, a place to park and a meal in any place that's air conditioned. 
 The traditional strengths of some better known shore towns, have been rated on a scale of 1 to 3. If your favorite place isn't listed, or if you don't agree with the rating, don't fret. Think of all the people who won't be going.
 
Long Branch
 Family Friendly 2
Thanks to benign neglect and an impressive effort by locals, the former capital of Victorian sin and degradation is shaping up into a relatively inexpensive, kid-friendly resort. The Ramada Inn and McIntosh, in West Long Branch, offer motel type accommodations--the beach is a short drive away. A night in the ocean front Hilton costs about as much as an Atlantic City casino hotel, but it's quieter. Storytelling hours in library. Great family style "meedballs and spaghet" at Tuzzio's, Westwood and Morris Ave. or Tony's, right across the street.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
As they say in the Branch, it ain't much, but it ain't bad. A good deal of the shore north of the Branch has been wiped out in storms, and the boardwalk amusements were wiped out in a fire. In place of the honky tonk is a flat, fireproof "promenade." The somewhat wider West End Beach is close to typical boardwalk junk food snackeries. Tag fees: seasonal $25 adults, $20 ages 12-17; daily $3 for adults, $2 12-17. 908-571-6545.
Sports 2
Breezy in-line skating on the promenade. Easy proximity to boating and fishing centers around Monmouth Beach and further south. Miniature golf near the Hilton. Rollerblading on promenade. Edgy bicycling on narrow Route 36 north. Turn west at Sea Bright for spectacular low-gear-only rock scarp scenery around Shrewsberry, and Rumson's "mansions of glory," one of which is owned by Bruce Springsteen.
The Essentials
One of the oldest resort towns, Long Branch blossomed in the Belle Epoque, when its racetrack and gambling casino were a brief ferry and train ride away from New York. After a long, graceless slide, the Branch, home to a large population of Italian retirees, is on the comeback trail, thanks to visible beachblock rennovations and a new generation of conservative family visitors that want everything to be just like Momma and Poppa did it. A twenty minute drive to Doris & Ed's in Highlands, or the Fromagerie in Rumson, two of the shore's best restaurants. Long Branch Chamber of Commerce: 908-222-0400.
 
Beach Haven
 Beach 2
Narrow, sloping, virtually identical to Island Beach, but dominated by large motels built right up to the crest of the dunes. Crowded but user friendly, with a barefoot bar conveniently located on the pool deck of the Engleside Inn. Beach tags: seasonal $9 to June 15, $15 thereafter; $5 weekly, $2 daily. Beach is free for senior citizens and children under 12. 609-492-0111.
Sports 3
Bicycling on astonishly crowded Long Beach Boulevard. The Barnegat Bay is the best single place on the shore for day sailing. Also, jet-skis rentals, as well as party boat, fishing and scuba charters, and an adults-only round-trip casino cruise to Atlantic City on the Black Whale. Yup--you get coins when you arrive.
Family Friendly 3
Thoroughly walkable. Summer stock and children's theater at Surflight Dinner Theater, pizza everywhere, souvenir and toy shops, VCR rentals, food markets, the Haven's small historical museum, and, most significant for families on a rainy day: a movie theater and fully stocked library with story hours. A good, but frustrating field trip is a drive north, to the Barnegat Light State Park, where the lighthouse is the most scenic on the shore.
Honky Tonk 2
Stroll to Fantasy Island, a boardwalk-style amusement area that isn't on an island. Too many dance clubs and meet-market bars for just-turned-21'ers, both in the Haven and a short drive up the Boulevard.
The Essentials
What was once a strong, blue-collar seasonal town covers just about every base imaginable. A dozen bed-and-breakfasts, such as Pierrot by the Sea and the Bayberry Barque (both on Centre Street) are less elaborate, and less expensive than Cape May's and Spring Lakes'. The motelish Engleside Inn, on Engleside Avenue is family-friendly. Expect congestion and and busy-ness. Long Beach Island Chamber of Commerce: 609-494-7211.
Events in the Bicentenial Park after June 17. House Tour, Aug. 9.
 
 Spring Lake
Quaint/Cozy 2
Short Hills meets Cape May: some of the best Victorian architecture on the shore (that fabulous stone pile on the boardwalk is the closed Essex-and-Sussex Hotel) clash with garishly modern suburban mansions on perfectly manicured lawns. Just enough bed-and-breakfasts and gingerbread hotels, not enough interesting restaurants within walking distance, but no one seems to care. Among the more expensive resort towns on the shore, it tends to give you your money's worth.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
Minimal in every sense of the word. Absolutely no honky-tonk or commercial development between two pleasant Victorian-style pavillions. The use of a plastic-wood blend to replace portions of the storm-damaged boardwalk is dubious at best. Magnificent, century-old beachfront hotels are more fun to look at than the beach. Tag fees: seasonal $55; $4.50 daily, children under 12 free. 908-449-8005.
Family Friendly 2
The rambling Chateau, across from Spring Lake city's park, is kid-friendly, very close to the train station, and across the street from the city park, which has an excellent kiddie playground, bring-your-own-canoe on the lake itself. Feed the kids at the Spring Lake Pizzaria, or Who's On Third, both on Third avenue. Eggimann's, in Spring Lake Heights, is a former stagecoach stop, for dinner time.
Sports
Walking, if only to admire the architecture. Also, bicycling and tennis in the park.
The Essentials
As narcissistic as Cape May, but not as crowded or commercial, Spring Lake feels like a dignified, affluent commuter suburb that happens to have an ocean nearby. Quiet, defiantly dull, with a pleasant Third Avenue main street shopping area and easy access to the New Jersey Transit commuter line into New York, the town can become cloying if you don't have a car. The top bed-and-breakfasts are the Normandy Inn and Sea Crest, both on Tuttle Avenue. Whispers, an eclectic restaurant in the small but tres luxe Hewitt-Wellington Hotel, is the burg's best eaterie. You're a short drive from the Old Mill Inn, in Spring Lake Heights, a dress-up surf-and-turfer. The Spring Lake Trolley links the major streets to the park and beach areas, weekends in June, daily in July and August, for $1 per person, per ride. Spring Lake Chamber of Commerce. 908-449-0577.
Events
Summer stock musical by Spring Lake Theater company, at Community House. House Tour, June 8.
 
 Seaside Heights
Honky-tonk 2
The best beachside rides north of Wildwood, and a towering water theme park at Grant Ave. and Ocean Terrace, bring an endless rush of teens and just-turned-21's. Rites of passage galore in loud dance clubs.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
Comfortably wide beach, great for body-watching, though the odor of coconut tanning oil overwhelms the salt brine. Wonderfully greasy boardwalk junkfood augmented by the transformation of boardwalk-fronting JR's into an upscale steakhouse. Fast pedaling bicycle cops keep order over, and under the boardwalk. Beach tags: seasonal, $35; daily $2 weekday, $3 weekend day. Children under 12 free. Limited number of beach wheelchairs for handicapped visitors available free with beach admission. Advance reservations for chairs required. 908-793- 9100.
Sports 2
Recently refurbished bayside tennis and basketball courts round out surfing, swimming, kayaking bicycling, jet-skii rentals and party boat and scuba charter. Close to boating and fishing in the Barnegat Bay.
The Essentials
Ocean County's premier teen party town is slowly going adult contemporary, but don't hold your breath. The dense pack of kids, and the rides and clubs that cater to them, so dominate the beach front that Seaside Heights visitors tend to ignore its quieter residential bayside, on the eastern edge of busy Route 35. The town is pushing to have motel owners upgrade their rooms, especially those around the ten block "French quarter" redevelopment area on the Boulevard, where a Cajun restaurant, Temptations, recently opened. If the town becomes too much, you can always flee to Island Beach State Park. Seaside Heights Public Relations: 908-793-9100.
Events
Seaside Heights holds a bayfront seafood festival and sports competition. June 17, 18. Clownfest--convention for professional clowns, Sept. 23,24.
 
 Point Pleasant Beach
Honky-Tonk 1
The only town on the shore with the guts to call itself The Beach is a satisfying, if overbuilt, balance of honky-tonk, junkfood emporia, obligatory seafood restaurants, kiddie arcades, and an aquarium. On Tuesdays, $10 buys unlimited rides.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
After bodywatching, the most common beachside recreation is viewing the big cabin cruisers and sportfishers chugging out from the blue-blazer-and-white-pants marinas around Brielle (prounced Breel by the natives). Healthy pig-out at the Natural Buffet, at Ocean Avenue. Tags--$5 weekend days, $4 weekdays; seasonal $60 adults, $40 children 5 to 11 and senior citizens; half seasonal $40 adults, $30 seniors and children 5 to 11. 908-892-3274.
Sports 3
The annual offshore power boat race has made The Beach the capital of the state's acquatic speed freaks. Barroom conversations are most often about RPMs, propshafts and hull designs. Great surf, charter and party-boat fishing nearby. Numerous fishing tournaments at local marinas. Professional and amateur volleyball competitions on the beach every weekend.
The Essentials
A tolerable antidote to the enforced, WASPy stuffiness of Bayhead directly to the south. The dominant blue collar culture has been edged out over the last few years, as members of the Nautica set (who want to be close to Bay Head but can't afford it), buy up, rennovate and price up what had been Point Pleasant's affordable rental housing. The newest motel, Point Pleasant Manor, on Sea Avenue, isn't beach front, but is affordable. The Ivory Seahorse is one of four new bed & breakfasts to open this year, signalling a challenge to the Cape May/Spring Lake crowd. The Beach's loud, but relatively tame pursuit of summer hedonism is still what the shore has always been about. Greater Point Pleasant Chamber of Commerce. 908-899- 2424.
Events
New Jersey Offshore Power Boat race, July 19. Seafood Festival, Sept. 16. Outdoor concerts every Wednesday night on the beach near Jenkinson's; boardwalk fireworks every Thursday night; Boardwalk laser light show every Sunday night.
 
Atlantic City
Sports 3
Sure, you can find party-boats, surf fishing and boardwalk bicycling, racquet sports (inside Merv Griffin's Resorts) and bowling (inside Showboat) but there's only one activity anyone cares about, and that's casino gambling. The casinos have fully equipped resort spas, with indoor swimming pools, saunas and other luxuries for rainy days.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
This is the world's first Boardwalk (named for Alexander Boardman, it's visionary inventor) but that isn't saying much. Some portions need a facelift. Others, near the casinos, are no fun at all, with the exception of the delightfully cheesy facade of the Taj Mahal. Atlantic City's is also one of the last free beaches on the shore now boasts dunes that are supposed to protect the city from storms. Take a load off your feet in a rolling chairs, another local invention. Mall-type shopping and food court at Ocean One pier, opposite Caesars at Arkansas Avenue. Few visit the beach, though there's surfing around States Avenue. Beyond the grim public bathrooms, there's no place to change and shower off unless you get a room in hotel or motel.
Honky-Tonk 2
The casino's gaudy interiors are adult honky-tonk at its most lavish. This is the only town in New Jersey where the bars stay open 24 hours. New York Avenue is the city's traditional party-club street, though most party-heartiers go to the clubs on the mainland in Somers Point, or to Margate clubs along Washington and Amherst Avenues, a five minute drive south.
Family Friendly 1
You CAN bring the kids here. TropWorld has an indoor theme park. Steel Pier (opposite the Trump Taj Mahal) and Central Pier (between the Sands and Resorts) have outdoor amusements. These pale compared to Wildwood, but at least there's something better than the video arcades. Casinos will, for a charge, provide babysitting. On rainy days, the Atlantic City Historical Museum on Garden Pier offers glimpses of the city's past. The city's public library is the best on the shore.
The Essentials
Legalized gambling has turned what was once the shore's premier convention resort and entertainment capital into a gambling town that also has conventions. Though casinos have reduced the number of headline entertainers and revue shows, this is still the only place at the shore to see everyone from Engelbert to Penn & Teller. Bally's Grand, at Boston and Pacific Avenues, is erecting an outdoor amphitheater for major headlining acts. There and elsewhere, the best seats are reserved for gamblers (buy tickets as far in advance as possible from Ticketmaster). At casino restaurants--gamblers are seated before paying customers. Restaurants at Trump Plaza and Merv Griffin's Resorts have earned consistently high reviews, and the new, all-you-can-guzzle Bacchanal at Caesars, where actors impersonate Caesar and Cleopatra, is a hoot. Gambling has given the city the highest crime rate along the shore, so park in casino garages, be careful of personal belongings and stay away from forbidding areas. Jitneys ($1.50 per person) provide fast transportation along Pacific Avenue, and link the Boardwalk casinos with those in the marina district. Visitors Information: 1-800-BOARDWK.
Events
Seafood Festival June 10-11; Beach Fest, June 16-18, July Fourth (fireworks on Boardwalk and in marina district); Kentucky Avenue Rennaissance, July 8-9; Harborfest, Aug. 5-6; Latin American Festival, Sept. 9, 10; Miss America Scholarship Pageant, Sept. 16.
 
 Wildwood
Honky-tonk 3
The best. Well over 250 outdoor amusement rides and a water theme park on six amusement piers (more than Disney World!). Carnival "barker" games and party-hearty bars like Cheers have given the island a reputation for teenage hellraising and rites of passage.
Beach and Boardwalk 3
The biggest beach on the shore--a rolling stretch of powdery white sand that is fully a half a mile wide. Admission is still free. The boardwalk is a odd, meandering combination of wood and concrete that supports trams. The new Great Nor'easter Roller Coaster, on Morey's Pier, aims to be the town's top lunch-looser: you ride it suspended over the ground. Bicycling and jogging is possible only in the early morning. Incredible crowds on weekends last well into the night. Nightly concerts at boardwalk bandshell at Montgomery Avenue.
Sports 2
Surfing, fishing (on piers, inlets and party boats. Thermals off the beach are great for kiteflying. Tennis in Wildwood Crest at Wisteria and Atlantic Aves. Baseball, basketball and tennis at fox park athletic field, Montgomery and Atlantic. Volley ball on the beach at Lincoln Avenue. Serious miniature golf at Shipwreck Island, 5000 Park Blvd.
The Essentials
Wildwood gets a bum rap for its teenage, rites-of-passage crowd and, in the frenetic areas around the party bars, the reputation is deserved. Elsewhere on the island, North Wildwood is lower keyed, family oriented, and Wildwood Crest, to the south, has row after row of moderately priced resort motels, also catering to a family crowd. A large Italian population blessed the island with superb mom-and-pop spaghetti joints, like the Ravioli House, on Bennett Avenue. Each year another old guesthouse becomes a bed and breakfast. Grown-ups can pretend they're kids, only to become adults again, at the Candlelight Inn in North Wildwood. Concerts and artsy activities throughout the season at the Hereford Inlet Lighthouse in North Wildwood. Wildwood Information Center: 609-522-1407.
Events
72nd Annual National Marbles Tournament June 19-22, Ultimate Frisbee Tournament and First-Ever National Pro Roller Hockey Tournament July 22-23. Aug. 31, Boardwalk Baby Parade.
 
Cape May
Quaint/Cozy 3
An occasionally unsettling, but generally pleasing mix of restored, gaily painted bed & breakfast inns and monotonous motels that have donned "Victorian" trim. Some among a dozen bistro-style restaurants are the best in the state. Expect odd fogs and other peculiar weather at the southernmost city on the shore.
Beach and Boardwalk 1
A flat, narrow, somewhat dull stretch of sand around the lower portion of Beach Drive, crowned by a barren, stone "Promenade" in the process of being repaved. Wonderful pounding surf, but no swimming permitted, just a few blocks north along the Drive, or south in Cape May Point State Park. Tolerable honkytonk near Convention Hall, made pleasing by the addition of an old-fashioned solarium, with retail shops and a new eclectic-style restaurant, Tish's Fine Dining. Beach tags: $15 seasonal, weekly $9, daily $4. Children 11 and under free 609-884-9525.
Outdoors 3
Fanatical birdwatching in Cape May Point State Park; good nature walks, too. 609-884-2159. Horseback riding a brief car trip (or bicycle ride, Hidden Valley Ranch in West Cape May, 609- 884-8205.
Sports 3
Fabulous angling--sign on the small partyboats departing from Fisherman's Wharf, or get a charter out of the nearby marinas. Great bicycling down incomparably quaint streets and country roads in West Cape May. New this year: jet skis around Fisherman's Wharf.
The Essentials
Only in Cape May is narcissim a major passtime: Mid-Atlantic Center for the Arts guided tours of the shady streest and their restored inns and mansions, either on foot or by trolley, are among the primary diversions. Some inns have apartment type dwellings for families; best to stay in a motel. Make sure, wherever you stay, that parking is included. Breezy, restaurants such as 410 Bank Street, Frescoes and the Water's Edge, are worth the long waits for tables. Nightly theater at the Cape May Stage (above the Welcome Center on Lafayette Street), and dinner theater at Elaine's, just down the road. Jazz nights at Shire Tavern, on Washington Mall. Cape May Chamber of Commerce: 609-884-5508. Mid-Atlantic Center for the Arts: 609-884-5404.
Events
Music Festival now through June 25. International Clam Shell Pitching Tournament Aug. 25
 
 Ocean City
Family Friendly 3
Plentiful scheduled and on-going activities for kids, both on the boardwalk and elsewhere. You're never far from a playground, though the "Miracle on 34th Street" Sand Castle Park, at 34th and West Avenue, is worth a trip. The city's library, arts center (where kids can take summer courses) and historical museum are in one building at 17th and Simpson Street. Sailing lessons for kids are available at bayside boat rentals. The boardwalk itself has a collection of tame, but enjoyable outdoor amusements, and that all-important water theme park, at 9th Street, and daily, free 5 p.m. children's concerts in the boardwalk Music Pier might just wean the kids off the television set.
Beach and Boardwalk 2
Surprisingly narrow as you head south, and attempts at widening it have failed. When magazines need a picture to dramatize a crowded beach, they take one here. You'll can depend on a place in the sun around the volleyball courts at 6th Street, and in the lumpy dunes at the south end. Go-cart tracks have sprouted on the boardwalk, at 9th street and at Plymouth Street. Every Thursday in July and August is family night on the boardwalk, with parades, performances and other activities planned. Tags--seasonal $12 until June 1, $15 thereafter, $6 weekly, $3 daily. Children 12 and under free. 609-399-6111
Sports 3
Tennis, surfing, golf (both miniature and full size, at the municipal course), shuffleboard, fishing by charter, pier or party boat, bayside boardsailing and day sailing, bicycling on and off the boardwalk.
The Essentials
Nobody does families better than at this former Methodist retreat, which, despite its preposterously overbuilt neighborhoods, is only becoming more popular among its mix of Irish, Italian and Jewish seasonal renters. For them, and a growing number of year 'round residents employed in Atlantic City's casinos, Ocean City's ban on the sale and public consumption of alcoholic beverages promises peace and safety. Rentals, starting at $1,000 a week, are the way to go--the grand Flanders Hotel, once the island's most luxurious accommodation, closed. Many motels have gone condo. Ten bed-and- breakfasts add variety to the mix, with the Northwood, at 401 Wesleyan, and the Scarborough, at 720 Ocean Avenue, among the newer properties. Breakfast on pancakes at the Chatterbox, fill up on the vaguely Italian surf-and-turf entrees at Cousin's or the Culinary Garden. Jitneys and trolleys ply the major streets. If the wholesomeness becomes too intense, you're only fifteen minutes from Atlantic City. Ocean City Public Relations: 609-525-9300.
Events
Night in Venice Boat Parade (bayside inlets), July 22, Hermit Crab Day and Miss Crustacean Pageant, Aug 2, Baby Parade, Aug. 10.
 
 Ocean Grove
Quaint/Cozy 2
Who could have thought that this crowded, stuffy little reserve of middle American wholesomeness could become chic and trendy? The small, old-time shore town mood still prevades as, each year more wood frame Victorians along Main Street sprout gingerbread flourishes and bright, multi-colored trim, as what was once a sleepy, neglected warren of bargain basement housing and monotonous eateries blossoms into a plainer, quieter version of Cape May.
Beach and Boardwalk 1
In order that the faithful may attend services, ocean swimming is forbidden on Sunday mornings until 12:30 p.m. The beach is a thin, unremarkable stretch of sand, ruled by a boardwalk that is a narrower extension of Asbury Park's great wooden way. What gives it a special appeal is the lack of motels, bloated summer homes and other seashore tack-ola. If life gets dull, you're a short walk from struggling nightclubs and the somber ruins of Asbury Park. Tags: daily $4.50, weekend special $8, weekly $22.50, seasonal adult $55, senior citizen $49.50, youth (ages 12-17) $30. Children 11 and under, free. 908-531- WAVE.
Family Friendly 3
The mix of Irish retirees and true believers and the Grove's fussy grid of claustrophobic streets, promise the shore's safest, and quietest get-away, except for Sunday morning, when the equivalent of a religious air raid siren calls the faithful to services in the Great Auditorium. Methodist oriented youth activities daily. Morning aerobics, planned to begin in July in Auditorium Park, should be sufficiently non-sectarian. No coin operated arcades, no junky souvenir stands, no junkfood. ground, there isn't much for kids to spend their allowance on. If you
Sports
Boardwalk bicycling is forbidden at all times, though a bicycling rental concession may open later in the season for city pedaling. An ocean kayak rental may also come along eventually, but never on Sunday mornings.
The Essentials
The oldest Methodist retreat town on the shore, Ocean Grove is a short taxi ride from the Asbury Park train station. Over the last decade, the Grove has been cautiously welcoming Yup-scale family visitors while struggling to maintain its appeal to the sincerely religious who see this a sacred retreat. For those who don't need alcohol, have little taste for honky tonk, this can be more an advantage than not. The town is tiny and easily walkable. Chose from a handful of clean, plain older hotels, like the moderately priced Quaker Inn, or the newly rennovated Ocean Plaza. Coyly quaint bed-and-breakfasts like The Carol-inn, the Pine Tree and the Mustard Seed or Pine Tree inns aim for the couples crowd. Obdurately ordinary, but well-prepared repasts at the Ocean View on the boardwalk, or on Main Avenue's Sampler Cafeteria, one of the last put-it-on-your-tray places anywhere. Great waffles at the Raspberry Cafe, whose owners are now serving dinner at the dreamy Moonstruck restaurant. The Four Trees, on Ocean Pathway (inside the Manchester Hotel), serves eclectic, bistro-style stuff. Not one, but two coffee houses are open to midnight on Saturday. One of them, the Daily Grind, plans folksy entertainment, a signal, perhaps, that the Grove is growing up. Ocean Grove Chamber of Commerce: 908-774-1391.
Main Events
Attend a Saturday night concerts in the Great Auditorium, a vast wooden assembly hall unlike any you've ever seen. Also, flea market, June 3, Sept. 9. Summer house tour, July 14. Choir festival (1,500 voices) July 9, Lobster bake, Aug. 20.
 
 Island Beach State Park
Outdoors 3
As good as it gets: a state park whose relatively pristine condition, bramble-choked dunes, ample parking, convenient changing facilities and uninterrupted views will make you proud to be a taxpayer.
Beach 3
Dramatic waves roll into narrow, steep stretches of sand, sculpted by tides and storms into densely planted grottos and hollows where you can find the rarest of beach pleasures: the illusion of privacy. And you can have a picnic anywhere on the beach, though include insect repellent, especially if a greenhead fly warning is posted at the gate. Sadly, what appear to be seasonal tides of illegally dumped flotsam have marred the Island Beach's incomparable natural splendor. Park rangers and volunteers do what they can to pick up what the tides won't wash away. You'll want to do the same.
Family Friendly 3
Daily guided nature walks and educational displays at nature center. Bring your kids here so they know what the shore used to look like.
Sports 3
Anything you can do in the water is better here. Surf fishing is permitted away from bathing beaches. No horseback riding in summer. No boat ramps, but a small access area will allow a kayak or canoe on the bayside.
The Essentials
Island Beach's main gate is supposed to be open from 8 a.m. to 8 p.m. for bicyclers and foot traffic, but it closes to automobiles when the parking areas fill up. Current capacity is about 2300 cars. If you're driving, arrive before 10 a.m. on weekends; 11 on weekdays. Admission is $6 per car weekdays, $7 weekends and holidays; free all day Tuesday. Immaculate changing facilities, with showers and lockers. Snack bars sell burgers, cold drinks and other seashore grease. Consumption of alcoholic beverages prohibited at all times. No overnight camping. 908-793- 0506

HEADLINE: Other Countries Thrive On Unbalanced Budgets

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The drive to balance the budget seems driven not by economics but by a radical social philosophy. Budgets are not balanced in any countries comparable to ours. As a percentage of gross domestic product, the 1994 imbalance in the United States was 2.7 percent. 
 The comparable percentages in other countries are: Japan, 2; Britain, 4.6; Germany, 2.7; France, 5.8; Italy, 9.4.; Netherlands, 3.1; Canada, 6.2, and Switzerland, 3.7 (data from Union Bank of Switzerland).
Except for Japan, none of these countries have been in balance in five years. All these countries provide more than we do in social services to their citizens, and in all of them there is less disparity between the rich and the poor.
Budget imbalance is not the major problem facing any of these countries, nor should it be for the United States, although it is important that savings be found wherever possible without damage to our society.
 
WALTER J. MOORE
Bloomington, Ind., May 23, 1995

HEADLINE: Rummage Sale of Land: One Way G.O.P. Will Try to Balance the Budget?

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 26

 BODY:
The Republican budget proposals moving through Congress include a subtle change in accounting rules that for the first time in a decade would invite Congressional committees to pay for their spending by selling land and other publicly owned assets.
Nobody is seriously considering selling any major parks. But because of the change, committees seeking ways to balance parts of the budget that come under their jurisdiction may well be tempted to go for a kind of Federal rummage sale. 
 "Asset sales are short-term and short-sighted," said Senator Bill Bradley, a New Jersey Democrat. "I doubt that any business accountant or economist would agree with the underlying budgetary premise that liquidating public assets adds to public wealth. If I sell my stock portfolio and put the returns in my checking account, do I become wealthier?"
The change in accounting rules to allow asset sales to raise cash will take effect only after House and Senate versions of the budget are reconciled and the bill is signed by President Clinton.
The Clinton Administration does not oppose counting asset sales as budget savings, although it differs with Congressional Republicans on many of the assets to be sold.
While nothing has prohibited the Government from selling its property to the private sector, Federal budget law has minimized the financial incentive for Congress by prohibiting the counting of these payments as a contribution toward a balanced budget.
But now, as Congress directs the committees that write spending bills to meet budgetary targets aimed at eliminating the deficit, the rule change could be especially important for committees that are reluctant to cut spending or raise taxes. For the first time since the 1986 Gramm-Rudman-Hollings budget law tried to infuse more discipline into the budget process, the committees will be free to use income from selling assets to offset spending.
For example, the budget outline that passed the Senate on Thursday assumes that from 2000 through 2002 the Government will receive $550 million by selling the Presidio, a spectacular section of waterfront next to the Golden Gate Bridge in San Francisco. Once a military post, the Presidio is now owned by the National Park Service.
"It is one of the most remarkable pieces of property left in the United States, and certainly the most remarkable piece of property left in an urban area," said Senator Dale Bumpers, an Arkansas Democrat, who fought the notion of counting revenue from asset sales in arriving at a balanced budget. "Here we have already put it up for sale. Who knows where we go after that?"
Environmental advocacy groups predicted that the pressure year after year to come up with new sources of revenue could lead to extensive sales of public lands.
"Under the guise of deficit reduction, industry allies in the Senate are using budget gimmickry to set up a fire sale of our national parks, forests, refuges and other public lands," said Robbie Cox, the president of the Sierra Club. "Whenever a senator's pet pork-barrel project is threatened they will rush to sell off another piece of our children's heritage."
But Senator Ted Stevens, a Republican of Alaska, called such suggestions "stupid."
"Have we suggested selling Mount Rushmore?" he said during the debate. "My God, I really cannot believe the depth of this argument, when it comes down to just say anything to scare people throughout the country."
The environmental groups were especially upset about the rule change because the budget resolutions specifically recommend raising cash by leasing the coastal plain of the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge for oil exploration and drilling. Protecting the area, a breeding ground for wildlife, has long been a priority of the environmental groups.
Although the budget is only an outline of where the Congress will actually spend and raise money, the votes on how income is accounted for are important because they provide a clear sense of sentiment and because procedural rules will make it hard later this year to stop initiatives like opening the Arctic refuge to oil drilling.
Under budget rules, committees that write spending bills must meet dollar targets included in the budget resolution, but they are not bound to raise or spend money on specific projects.
The change in accounting rules could lead committees to offer for sale assets under their jurisdiction to pay for spending programs that they are unwilling to cut or that they want to expand.
Already, a House committee has approved legislation to create a commission for recommending which parks ought to be sold or transferred to the states. The commission would operate in the same way as one that has identified military bases for closing, subject to Congressional approval. The bill would exempt 54 major parks.
And a House task force studying the deficit has endorsed a proposal by the Heritage Foundation, a conservative research organization, to help balance the budget by selling Federal land that is now used for grazing, wildlife refuges and parks or preserved as wilderness.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

U.S. Is Isolated, Both East and West
 The Clinton Administration is finding itself isolated and outmaneuvered around the world on its threatened auto-trade sanctions against Japan, with Asian governments joining their counterparts in Europe in condemning Washington's move to impose $6 billion in punitive tariffs against Japanese luxury cars. 
 Japanese trade officials have been warning their Asian neighbors that their economies will be the next targets if Tokyo is forced to set aside a part of its market for American goods. The message is sinking in: Asian leaders have told the Clinton Administration that they share its frustration in opening Japan's markets, but cannot abide its tactics. [Page 1.]
 The United States will announce a major effort to strengthen ties with Europe, a move that would start with more cooperation on helping poor nations and removing trade barriers, and could ultimately lead to a free-trade area spanning North America and Europe. [3.]
 
In Asia, the Internet Could Cut Two Ways
 The cyberspace revolution's impact will be felt most intensely in authoritarian nations of Asia, the continent that is home to the world's fastest-growing economies. The Internet holds the promise of bolstering regional economies by connecting them to the information highway, but also means the countries will be flooded with the sort of information that some of their governments have been trying to restrict. [1.]
 
New Round of Britain vs. Murdoch
 Britain plans to revise its rules for control of newspapers, television and radio. One effect will be to block Rupert Murdoch, a towering figure in British publishing and broadcasting, from expanding in television unless he scales back his newspaper holdings. Mr. Murdoch has made it clear that he will fight the proposals. [41.]
 
A Glut of Computer Screens
 Japan's manufacturers of advanced screens for notebook computers, a bright spot for the country's electronics industry, have become victims of their own success. With far more small screens available than computer makers need, prices for notebook computers are dropping, pulling profit for their manufacturers down with them. [39.]
 Small, underfinanced American companies are struggling to make their mark in the display-panel business, but they are finding the going tough. [39.]
 
International Paper Revises Bid
 International Paper, which has offered to pay $320 million for Holvis Holzstoff, a Swiss fibers and paper concern, said it was willing to top a competing $372 million bid. [38.]
 
New Packages for Network News
 Confident of finding new ways to turn news into cash, executives at the three major broadcast networks say network news has a vibrant future despite slips in the ratings and in revenue. Bill Carter: Television. [41.]
 
More Women on the Net
 A demographic survey of the Internet has confirmed that the global computer network is mostly a men's club, with male users outnumbering women 2 to 1. But the numbers are still surprising; earlier surveys had put the margin closer to 9 to 1. Peter H. Lewis: On the Net. [39.]
 
New Ground for a Ghost
 "Jurassic Park" wowed audiences with its dinosaurs, though, it turns out, only six minutes of the film involved computer animation. So what impressions will moviegoers take away from "Casper the Ghost," which has 43 minutes, more time than any film has devoted to computer animation? [41.]
 
20's Classics Up for Grabs
 Many literary classics of the 1920's are entering the public domain -- bad news for publishers like Scribner, Harcourt Brace and Houghton Mifflin, which have enjoyed the prestige and, often, the substantial profit that has come with owning publishing rights to much of the rich literature of the 1920's. [41.]

HEADLINE: In Search of Nielsens for the Internet

BYLINE: By LAURIE FLYNN 

 BODY:
Like many companies still unsure whether the Internet is a business fad or the face of the future, the Chrysler Corporation recently took a leap of faith into on-line advertising.
The auto maker purchased ads at several locations on the World Wide Web, the Internet's multimedia service. Chrysler has also created a kind of electronic brochure on the Web, including a virtual showroom of hot-concept cars, a look at safety systems and the standard lineup of Dodges and Jeeps. 
 The challenge now is to figure out if anyone cares out there in Modem Land.
Chrysler's predicament is shared by the tens of thousands of businesses that have begun running interactive ads on the Web, including Coors Brewing, MCA Records, a unit of the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company and MCI Communications. Right now, there is no reliable way of knowing how many people are interacting with these ads.
Television has its A. C. Nielsen ratings, which tell advertisers how many sets are tuned in when their ads run. Radio has the Arbitron ratings. And magazines and newspapers have independent auditing agencies that verify circulation.
Such audience measurements are the basis for the ad rates set by broadcasters and publishers. And the same data, in turn, enable marketers to gauge how well their ad dollars are being spent.
For advertisers pumping their money into cyberspace, however, there are no Nielsens -- not yet, at least. That may soon change.
In the last few months, a handful of ambitious software start-up companies have announced techniques for measuring the audience for Internet ads. Earlier this month, A. C. Nielsen, a subsidiary of Dun & Bradstreet, said it planned to enter the game, probably in a venture with one of these software start-up concerns. Arbitron, a unit of the Ceridian Corporation, has also expressed interest.
Some of the start-ups, including Digital Planet and the Internet Profiles Corporation -- I/Pro, for short -- recently started performing their monitoring services for a few Internet advertisers. And while it is too early to predict whether either of these, or any other services, might become the industry standard, advertisers are eager for some sort of standard to emerge. Otherwise they must continue to rely on guesswork and trust.
"Today, you come up with a number that seems reasonable and then keep raising the price as long as people are willing to pay it," said David Carlick, senior vice president of Poppy Tyson Advertising, one of two agencies enlisted by Chrysler to create its on-line campaign.
More advertisers appear ready to start spending at least part of their budgets on interactive ads. Jupiter Communications, a market-research company in New York, said the target audience was the nearly 4.6 million people with the Internet access and software allowing them to visit the World Wide Web, an area of the Internet where text can be mingled with photographs, film clips and sound. The Web allows browsers to move easily between sites by selecting highlighted elements. Jupiter forecasts that the number of Web users will more than double by 1997.
John A. Dimling, president of Nielsen Media Research, predicts that the Web will be generating "hundreds of millions" a year in advertising sales by the end of the decade -- still tiny compared with the $125 billion a year now being spent for all media in the United States, but money worth keeping tabs on.
Chrysler, which so far is restricting its Web site to pictures and text (the address is http://www.chryslercorp.com), is also paying undisclosed amounts for ads in two other electronic sites on the Web. One is Time Warner's Pathfinder site (http://www.pathfinder.com); the other is Wired magazine's HotWired (http:www.hotwired.com).
Internet advertisers are eager for data that would show the most effective sites on the Web.
What is ultimately needed, said J. R. Julow, director of marketing operations at Chrysler, is a system that measures not only the effectiveness of one Internet ad over another, but one that can compare the effectiveness of Internet advertising with that of ads placed on television or in newspapers.
Besides I/Pro, which is based in San Francisco, and Digital Data of Los Angeles, analysts say the companies that may provide the answer include Logical Design Solutions of Murray Hill, N.J., and a joint venture of the Delahaye Group of Portsmouth, N.H., and Internet Media Services of Palo Alto, Calif.
Monitoring overall use of the World Wide Web is easy, since the network servers automatically create a log of network activity. But in most cases, this data is limited to tracking the number of times a single file on a Web site is called up.
The big task for the new Web-monitoring services is analyzing the raw traffic statistics and turning them into useful marketing data, like determining how many times the same people viewed an ad, how long they lingered and what route they took to get there. Advertisers might also want to know what Web site they visited next, to determine where else it might pay to advertise to reach similar types of people.
Initially, these emerging systems will provide only basic data, most often just the name of network servers, the universities, companies or commercial Internet providers who connect an individual computer to the Internet.
Even in cases where more individual information can be ascertained through users' electronic registration forms, Internet publishers say their concern for consumer privacy would prevent them from disclosing the users' identities or other personal information. But as people routinely make purchases over the network, more specific consumer information is likely to be collected -- just as credit-card companies compile huge data bases on the spending habits of those who use their cards.
That might make the I/Pro's and Digital Planets serve more as data-base builders than census takers, Adam Schoenfeld, a vice president at Jupiter Communications, says. "There's better tracking of new media than traditional media already, because everybody leaves fingerprints, showing where they were and where they went next," he said.

HEADLINE: U.S. to Seek Stronger Trade and Political Ties With Europe

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, MAY 28

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration will announce a major effort this week to strengthen ties with Europe, a move that would start with more cooperation on helping poor nations and removing trade barriers and could ultimately lead to a free trade area encompassing North America and Europe.
Secretary of State Warren Christopher is scheduled to unveil this effort in Madrid on Friday in response to fears, voiced on both sides of the Atlantic, that the once-seamless alliance between European nations and the United States has deteriorated because of the end of the cold war, trade disputes and differences over Bosnia, Iran and Japan. 
 According to aides to Mr. Christopher, he will say that the trans-Atlantic relationship focuses too much on security and must be broadened by setting up new channels for political and economic cooperation. In his Madrid speech, he will endorse the goal -- still 10 to 20 years away -- of establishing a trans-Atlantic free-trade area of 700 million people.
Mr. Christopher will also propose numerous steps to strengthen links in the near future between the United States and the 15-nation European Union, including coordinating the fight against crime and drugs and opening talks on reducing restrictions on bidding for public contracts.
These steps will be the beginning of a long-term effort to breathe new life into the European-American relationship, American officials say. Some officials acknowledge that these efforts are as much public relations as substance, but they say that some highly visible steps are needed to reaffirm that the United States and Europe have a shared heritage and that they can lead the world on crucial issues.
The Administration's new effort comes after American and European leaders have worried aloud that the other side was looking too much to the east. American officials say Western Europe is preoccupied with such issues as admitting Poland, Hungary and other Eastern European countries into NATO and into the European Union and on improving ties with Russia and Turkey.
European leaders -- most recently Foreign Minister Klaus Kinkel of Germany in a speech here last week -- said they were concerned that America's attention was drifting toward the fast-growing markets of Asia and Latin America.
"There is definitely a sense in Europe that a vacuum has developed between Europe and the U.S.," Jeffrey E. Garten, Under Secretary of Commerce for International Trade, said in an interview. "This is partly a result of a European sense that we have spent most of our time and energy on other areas. And with the end of the cold war, the reduction of American forces in Europe and no widespread security threat, there is a fear that the essential glue that held the relationship together seems to be coming apart."
Mr. Christopher will be in the Netherlands Monday through Wednesday for NATO meetings focusing on the crisis in Bosnia. On Friday, he will give his policy address in Madrid and meet with Spanish officials before they assume the European Union's rotating six-month presidency in July.
"His speech will be very reassuring to Europeans as to U.S. intentions," said a senior State Department official.
To strengthen political ties, Mr. Christopher will propose stepped-up cooperation on halting the spread of nuclear weapons, seeking peace in the Middle East, helping political reform in the former Soviet bloc and providing aid to the third world, Eastern Europe and Russia. While trans-Atlantic coordination is often done haphazardly through occasional meetings, Mr. Christopher will call for setting up more formal mechanisms, like regular meetings of top American and European antinarcotics officials.
The way British Foreign Secretary Douglas Hurd described it in a speech on May 22 in Chicago, increased cooperation is vital so that the United States and Europe can be "pathfinders" that lead world efforts toward peace, stability, free trade and prosperity.
As for economic cooperation, American and European diplomats say that while setting up a trans-Atlantic free trade area is a decade or two off, opening talks to establish such an area can inject new energy into the relationship.
During a visit last Thursday to Washington, Mr. Kinkel, who is a leading advocate of a free-trade area, said, "Now that economic and financial issues have come more to the foreground, next to NATO another pillar ought to be built -- an economic bridge -- although it won't be built overnight."
Mr. Kinkel said that in a meeting on Capitol Hill, this idea was backed enthusiastically by Representative Newt Gingrich, the Speaker of the House, who has said trans-Atlantic ties cannot be sustained by "nostalgia" alone.
American officials acknowledge that Mr. Christopher's talk of a free-trade area may help alleviate European anger about Washington's high-pressure tactics to pry open Japan's automobile market.
Among the topics on the economic agenda are developing joint product standards, harmonizing environmental regulations and eliminating barriers that make it hard for American companies to bid for public procurement contracts in Europe and vice versa. Commerce Secretary Ron Brown and top European Union officials are organizing talks between European and American business executives to discuss these topics and others, especially how to avoid destructive competition, through, for instance, bribes or heavy government subsidies, to win contracts in huge Asian markets like India or China.
"We need a new economic architecture across the Atlantic," said Mr. Garten. "Unless there is some idea that holds us together in the absence of a common, overriding security threat, natural economic juices may force us much further apart than anyone conceives of right now."
Some Administration officials are said to be pushing for a go-slow approach to free trade with Europe because they remember how an important Democratic constituency, labor unions, soured on President Clinton when he pushed for the North American Free Trade Agreement.
But some policy makers predict that labor will hardly object to pursuing a free-trade accord since many European nations, unlike Mexico, have a higher minimum wage and tougher safety and environmental standards than the United States.
European and American officials say movement toward a free trade zone will be hampered by the three politically charged issues that the two sides wrestled over in the so-called Uruguay Round of trade talks: subsidies to farmers, subsidies to aircraft manufacturers, and European quotas on imported movies and television programs.
The idea of a trans-Atlantic free trade area has caused some Asian leaders to warn that the United States is turning its attention away from them because of frustrations with Asian trade restrictions.

HEADLINE: FRANCE DEMANDS REINFORCEMENTS

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, May 28

 BODY:
Prime Minister Alain Juppe said tonight that France would press for withdrawal of the United Nations peacekeeping forces from Bosnia unless NATO and the United Nations agreed in the next few days to send them reinforcements.
He also repeated a call by President Jacques Chirac on Saturday for the United Nations to revise its mandate and intensify its peacemaking efforts if France is to keep its 3,800 soldiers in Bosnia, which both men say is what they really want. 
 As Bosnian Serb forces took more peacekeepers hostage today, including 33 British troops, Mr. Juppe said both the mission of the United Nations force and its deployment at scattered and poorly defensible sites around the country urgently needed to be changed.
The British Government issued a statement saying that it "utterly condemned" the seizure of its soldiers and said it was increasing its deployment of troops to Bosnia as soon as possible.
"The Ministry of Defense will be sending two artillery batteries and an armored engineer squadron to Bosnia as soon as possible," the Government said in a statement after an emergency Cabinet meeting.
In Washington, Clinton Administration officials said the United States is prepared to support a French plan to redeploy United Nations troops into more secure positions, and emphasized the need to bolster the peacekeeping mission in Bosnia. [Page 5.]
In France, Mr. Juppe said in an interview on national television, "We have to end the taking of hostages, restart the diplomatic process, and if all that fails, then agree to withdraw."
"I can't tell you whether that will be today or the day after," he said, addressing a public he knew was gravely concerned about the fate of French hostages, whose treatment he described tonight as "barbarity."
"We cannot live with the situation as it is," he said.
He spoke after a weekend of intensive telephone contacts on the Bosnian crisis between Mr. Chirac and President Clinton, President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia, Prime Minister John Major of Britain, President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia and other leaders. Most of the European allies that have troops in Bosnia appear to support France's calls for pulling back from exposed positions. Diplomats said Canada is also deeply concerned about the possible additional dangers that its troops could face as a result of NATO threats to repel attacks with air strikes.
Mr. Juppe said that at meetings set to be held in the Netherlands on Monday night and Tuesday, France would ask its allies to agree to send a NATO air-mobile reaction force to Bosnia equipped with helicopters and gunships that peacekeepers could call on for protection.
"These decisions should be taken within the next few days," he said.
Secretary of State Warren Christopher and Foreign Ministers of the so-called contact group seeking a diplomatic settlement in Bosnia -- the United States, Britain, France, Germany and Russia -- are to meet in the Hague on Monday night to consider ways of restarting diplomatic efforts, French officials said.
They are to go to the coastal resort of Nordwijk on Tuesday and Wednesday for a regular NATO ministerial meeting and for consultations between the allies and former Warsaw Pact countries.
The French proposal for a NATO reaction force would require agreement by both the United Nations and NATO -- an arrangement that United States officials say has proved highly unsatisfactory in launching air strikes so far.
The French Prime Minister was also critical of the United Nations decision to call in NATO bombers against the Serbs last week, though France supported the decision at the time to force the Serbs to stop bombarding Sarajevo and to return heavy weapons they had illegally seized from United Nations weapons-collection depots.
"That ultimatum and those air strikes were not carefully planned," Mr. Juppe said tonight. "They submitted our troops to ill-considered risks. We have no right to expose our soldiers to ill-considered risks."
Mr. Juppe, who was Foreign Minister until Mr. Chirac, France's newly elected conservative President, put him at the head of a new Government 11 days ago, said that France would ask the contact group to name a single negotiator to try more effectively for a settlement. So far, its officials have usually traveled together in groups, but the Bosnian Serbs have sometimes refused to let all of them pass its checkpoints.
The Prime Minister did not say how he expected diplomacy suddenly to work after years of futility, but he did say that President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia could use his influence on the Bosnian Serbs more effectively to pressure them into accepting a settlement.
"Milosevic could do something tomorrow," Mr. Juppe said. "He could recognize Bosnia and Herzegovina within its present frontiers." The contact group tried unsuccessfully this spring to get Mr. Milosevic to agree to that as a condition for lifting United Nations economic sanctions against Serbia, but Mr. Milosevic insisted that the the sanctions be lifted unconditionally first.
"The moment that is accomplished, I believe the international community should recognize the situation and lift the sanctions -- with a clause permitting reimposition if necessary," Mr. Juppe said.
Mr. Chirac on Saturday gave French troops in Bosnia full authority to resist attack with force and despatched a French air and naval force, including the aircraft carrier Foch and a helicopter carrier, to the Adriatic. The force is to arrive there on Tuesday and join NATO naval operations that already include an American aircraft carrier, the Theodore Roosevelt.
The NATO forces could be used to aid peacekeepers and, if necessary, to take them to safety if France, Britain, Spain and the other countries involved decide that their mission cannot be carried out and that their position is untenable.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The flight operations room of the American aircraft carrier Theodore Roosevelt, stationed in the Adriatic, preparing to provide air support in case of further NATO air strikes against the Bosnian Serbs. (Associated Press) (pg. 5)

HEADLINE: U.S. FINDS ITSELF VIRTUALLY ALONE ON JAPAN TRADE

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: SINGAPORE, May 27

 BODY:
Japanese trade officials have spread through Asia in recent days, warning government officials that their booming economies will be the next targets if Japan is forced by the United States to set aside a certain portion of its market for American goods.
The message is sinking in. Nearly two weeks after declaring that it would impose harsh trade sanctions on Japan, the Clinton Administration is finding itself isolated and outmaneuvered around the world, with Asian governments joining their counterparts in Europe in condemning Washington's move to impose $6 billion in punitive tariffs against Japanese-made luxury cars. 
 In a stream of newspaper columns, public speeches and private meetings with American officials, Asian leaders have told the Clinton Administration that they share its frustration in opening Japanese markets, but cannot abide its tactics.
"We are quite sympathetic with America's goals," Sarasin Viraphol, director general for American affairs in the Foreign Ministry of Thailand, said here today during a break in a meeting of senior American and Asian business leaders, government officials and academics that was dominated by the trade confrontation. "We understand why America feels it must go eyeball to eyeball. But if it is successful using this weapon on Japan, soon it will use it on all of us."
The sharp rejection of President Clinton's initiative in both Asia and Europe has come as a pleasant surprise to Japanese officials, and it seems to be emboldening them to face down the United States. In private, several senior Japanese officials say they believe that Mr. Clinton's top trade advisers are already having second thoughts and will seek a face-saving compromise rather than let the newly formed World Trade Organization issue a ruling on the legality of the American sanctions. The Clinton Administration, however, appears dug in.
"Both countries have walked themselves into positions that are hard to get out of," said Gerald Curtis, a professor of political science at Columbia University and a longtime expert on Japanese politics. "That is why everyone is so nervous that this time there could be a real collision."
[On Sunday, the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, continued weeklong sparring with the Japanese over when to resume trade talks. Washington proposed a meeting on June 20, which Tokyo said was too close to the sanctions deadline. It asked instead for a meeting early in June. But Mr. Kantor, appearing on the ABC television program "This Week," said June 20 was the "correct date."]
Most trade experts say they expect that Japan would win a legal challenge to the 100 percent tariffs that are to be imposed on 13 Japanese-made luxury car models if an agreement is not reached by June 28. Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown seemed to acknowledge that possibility earlier this week in Paris, when he said that American Government lawyers had mixed opinions on whether the punitive tariffs violated the world trade agreement approved last year.
"He was putting the best possible face on things," a senior American official said of Mr. Brown's comments. "I think there is a clear apprehension that we are going to court with a losing case."
Indeed, American officials are making little effort to defend the legality of the sanctions. Instead, they have begun to send direct and indirect messages to Tokyo that something much more important than auto parts is at stake.
Japanese leaders, they now argue, have an essential decision to make: whether it is worth winning points in front of the World Trade Organization if the long-term cost is harm to their overall relationship with the United States. Winning the case in front of the trade organization, they have warned, would be an empty victory if it jeopardized American public support for the security alliance that Japan depends upon.
The warning seems at odds with the Administration's public position -- stated several times in the last two weeks by White House officials -- that they would never use American security guarantees for Japan as a weapon in trade disputes. But in subtle ways, the two are already linked.
This morning, for example, The Straits Times, Singapore's Government-influenced newspaper, carried an interview with Winston Lord, the American Assistant Secretary of State for East Asia and the Pacific, that used unusually blunt language in warning Japan of the risks of its stance.
Mr. Lord said the United States was not linking its security presence with trade agreements. But he added that the public might make that linkage.
"Over time," he said, "if the U.S. domestic perception is that the U.S. was being shut out of Asian markets, they might well begin to question the maintenance of forces in the region." There are nearly 100,000 American troops in Japan, South Korea and other parts of East Asia.
Yet in a two-week tour of Asia, Mr. Lord was unable to whip up much support for Washington's hardball approach. He received his strongest backing from Singapore's patriarch and now senior minister, Lee Kuan Yew, who delivered a fiery speech in Tokyo last week urging the Japanese to deregulate and open their economy.
Mr. Lee warned Japan that it "risks serious deterioration in bilateral relations with the U.S. if it persists with its current practices." He added that Japan "must weigh its trading interests against its needs for an indispensable U.S. counterweight to a China growing in weight and influence, and a Russia which is still well armed but unstable."
But Mr. Lee reserved some strong criticism for the Administration, ending his speech by declaring that American demands that Japan enter a "voluntary plan" to purchase a minimum number of autos and auto parts "amount to managed trade" that would "damage the interests of other countries."
The reasons for Asian governments' refusal to back the United States are complex, Asian officials say -- as intertwined as the trading relationships that link some of the world's fastest-growing economies.
The first fear here is that Asian countries without Japan's wealth or political influence would not stand a chance against similar American demands that they, too, quickly open their markets or face strong sanctions. Thus, countries from Taiwan to Indonesia have a strong interest in forcing the United States to go to the World Trade Organization in the hope that big and small economies might be treated as equals.
Japan is also calling in its chips after a decade-long spending spree around the region. It has provided billions of dollars in foreign aid to Indonesia, China and other Asian nations -- compared with a tiny trickle of American spending -- and has built a huge network of Japanese-owned factories around the region. That network has made many Asian countries part of the Japanese economic family. Thus, air-conditioners and refrigerators produced by the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company's subsidiary in Kuala Lumpur have no problem getting onto Japanese store shelves. And Japan is a huge employer. David D. Hale, an economist for the Kemper Financial Companies, estimated recently that one in seven manufacturing workers in Thailand is now employed by a Japanese concern.
Thirdly, there is a fear that any concessions won by the United States could come at the expense of other Asian nations. In South Korea, a newspaper cartoon recently showed the United States shooting an arrow at Japan, and the Japanese deftly ducking out of the way. South Korea was standing just behind, right in the arrow's path.
Under this theory, Japanese companies reserve a certain part of their purchases for foreigners, and Asians and Europeans are left with whatever the United States does not claim. Similar fears of being cut out led European countries to warn Japan and the United States last week that they would sue both countries if Japan agreed to numerical targets for increased sales of American cars and car parts. American officials say that worry is misplaced, and that if the United States succeeds in opening the Japanese market, all of Japan's trading partners will benefit.
Thus, many Asian officials say they want to make sure that the United States and Japan are neither too adversarial or too close, both conditions that could pinch Japan's neighbors.
"When the elephants fight, the grass is trampled," Mr. Sarasin said, quoting an oft-heard saying in Asia. "But when they make love, it's even worse."

LENGTH: 1331 words

HEADLINE: In the Hamptons, the Gridlock Season Begins

BYLINE: By NEIL MacFARQUHAR 

DATELINE: EAST HAMPTON, L.I., May 28

 BODY:
Twelve hours before this year's bistros-of-the-moment served their first calamari appetizers, in fact well before many of their clients had even lifted an eyelid, Danny King was on the bay, straining under nets laden with just plain squid.
Even as he lifted, Mr. King, his face framed by a white-flecked, rounded beard of the type his ancestors might have worn 200 years ago, kept watch for the cigarette boats stinging across Gardiners Bay. 
 "You used to go fishing and you didn't see half a dozen boats all day," he said, his long iron hook bringing up a trap net swarming with squid, skate, rockfish, striped bass, fluke, blowfish, sea robin and horseshoe crabs -- a living, frothing bouillabaisse. Now on foggy days, he said, he hesitates to venture even the 100 yards offshore to check his nets. "I keep my head going around in circles," he said. "A lot of these people don't know where they are and just keep going full throttle."
Memorial Day weekend marks summer moving to full throttle in the Hamptons, the annual onslaught of the chic, both welcomed and dreaded by the locals. The transformation from ordinary to constantly crowded and vaguely exotic makes the natives feel like strangers in their own land, even though it is almost the sole support of their economy. Each summer they feel a little more besieged in this string of quaint, highly groomed villages that thrive on the annual tide of summer trade, a tide that never recedes as far as the year before.
"This is going to be the summer when we forget who the locals are," said Helen S. Rattray, the editor and publisher of The East Hampton Star, who some natives barely consider local, even though she has lived here for more than three decades. "They are disappearing."
It has been years since those rooted on eastern Long Island got a September break from the crowds. Faxes, computers and multimillion-dollar houses have made the Hamptons a virtual extension of Manhattan. A recent state study estimated that $10 billion has been invested in second homes, and there is no great urge to shutter them come Labor Day.
"In October we now have the Hamptons Film Festival, just to extend the misery," said Bill Henderson, a publisher who took refuge from New York 15 years ago and now escapes from East Hampton to Maine until Thanksgiving. Not even that date may be safe; a just-opened Hamptons branch of a SoHo eatery is planning a New Year's Eve party.
Still, the crowds reach a crescendo in the summer, when traffic along much of the 30 miles between Southampton and Montauk all but stops, and the only positive thing anybody can say about the gridlock and the difficulty making a left turn across the Montauk Highway is that it keeps accidents to a minimum.
It is little wonder, then, that the vehicle of choice for the standing still has become the British Range Rover, which is somewhat akin to driving a turbo-charged living room couch. David Yurman, a jewelry designer, and his wife, Sybil, paused on the way from Manhattan to plunk down $55,000 for a silvery blue version.
"Before we got to the house, we stopped here to buy," Mrs. Yurman said. "It's a very cushy ride. It's a status car out here." The couple appeared a little unsettled when someone mentioned that dealers in Brooklyn or New Jersey might sell it $4,000 cheaper, but protested the idea of venturing into Brooklyn to buy a car.
Since most customers barely get to drive their cars on the road, much less off, Bob Dauman, a salesman on the lot, said that the dealership rents out the Bridgehampton race track every Labor Day, filling it with mud puddles, gravel slides and hillocks so the vehicle owners can splash around in four-wheel drive. "It's a fantasy," Mr. Dauman said. "They love it."
Although more and more people stay put in eastern Long Island come September, Tony Bullock, the supervisor in the town of East Hampton, estimates the area population surges to 225,000 from 100,000 over the summer. "I know from our garbage flow," he said. "It's 18 to 20 tons a day in winter, while peak weekends generate over 120 tons a day."
That 50-day influx generates between 60 and 70 percent of the year's economic activity, he said, a number likely to grow as major employers like Drummond Aircraft leave and are not replaced. The business of building or buying second homes keeps an army of lawyers, architects, carpenters, house painters and gardeners busy almost year-round. Local shops have to pay more than minimum wage to keep workers. And about 80 percent of the $260,000 collected for parking tickets last year in East Hampton came over the summer.
This year the price of tickets went to $40 from $20, setting the scene for tempers erupting like overheated radiators. "I am delighted that you are such a genius," sneered a man in a blue shirt and yellow silk bow tie, flinging onto the pavement a ticket that Officer Greg J. Brown wrote him for parking in a bus stop.
The natives develop summer survival skills. They stop going to restaurants. They boycott the three-hour lines for movies. They rarely stop by the post office and scurry into supermarkets on prime beach days. With shop rents on some streets now at $4,500 a month, most of the other stores they once frequented have been driven out by boutiques, antique dealers and real estate agents.
"How many people are going to buy a $600 or $700 sweater, no matter how nice it looks on the mannequin?" said Jonathon Dayton, a farmer who said he soured on second-home owners after one moved next door and began a protracted legal battle to shut down his produce wagon. "There is no place to go anymore that hasn't been taken over. There really are no local people left."
Snatches of sidewalk conversations are rather distant from fisherman or farmers discussing the rarity of the east wind this year. A sampling:
"The trendiest person I know? I'm like the trendiest person I know."
"My therapist told me just to look at my job like working for a bunch of monkeys."
"Don't go to the estate auctions. You should fly over to England to buy the antiques and bring back a container. It will be cheaper in the long run."
The accents are often French, German and Italian, as the weak dollar attracts Europeans, who real estate agents say like the low crime rate.
"People who have rented in someplace like Cap-Ferrat in the south of France have been robbed at gunpoint," said Harald Grant, a transplanted Norwegian working at one of the gold-plated real estate agencies. "Here you don't have that."
But from the prices, it's sometimes hard to tell. A studio within driving distance of the beach goes for $5,000 for the summer. For ocean-front property with a pool, tennis court (the local shortage is viewed as a major scourge) and a five-car garage, expect to pay more than $200,000. A basic three-bedroom house within walking distance of the beach is $25,000 to $40,000.
The days when summer meant a slower pace and getting away from it all seem lost forever. The East Hampton Star is probably one of the few small-town newspapers in America with its own 14-page arts supplement and an editor who worries that chasing Steven Spielberg, an area resident, for a profile might duplicate an article in Vanity Fair.
Dan's Papers, the grandfather of the local giveaway weeklies, splashed things like U.F.O. sightings across its pages in the 1960's. Now Catherine J. Squires, the editor's assistant, calls the artsy front covers "collectible," and hands out a list of three fetes for its 35th anniversary. "Our gala party on June 17 has an A-list that would just knock your socks off, people like Donald Trump and Mort Zuckerman," she said.
Irene Silverman, a Manhattan refugee, shakes her head at what started as a few writers and painters mixed in among the potato farmers. "Now's the time that life gets closest to the life in New York City," she said. "We tried to get away from each other, but we just followed each other. We are all here now."

HEADLINE: What Goes On?;
Call of the Modem, to Some, Is a Sweet Nothing

 BODY:
It is the white noise of the electronic age, the signature tune of computers, faxes and just about all things that interconnect over phone lines. It is to the office what the blue jay's screech is to the garden.
It is the call of the modem in search of a mate. 
 But what, exactly, are they saying?
As in all intital encounters in the animal kingdom, two strangers in the digital kingdom need to check each other out. Nicholas Negroponte, professor of media technology at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, wrote in his book, "Being Digital" (Alfred A. Knopf, 1995), that "these mating calls are the negotiations to find the highest terrain from which they can trade bits, with the greatest common denominator of all variables."
Roughly translated, the introductions go like this:
Modem A: Hi, I'm a modem.
Modem B: So am I.
Modem A: Wanttotalkfast?
Modem B: I o n l y t a l k v e r y s l o w l y.
Modem A: F i n e.
And why, in this age of technological advancement, does all this still sound like Donald Duck gargling? Given the harshness of the sound and the effort many software and hardware companies devote to esthetics, why have no manufacturers masked the guttural cacophony with a few bars of Beethoven or the blues?
Because there's a limit to what modems can do. Some software companies offer options to turn the sound off. Those who are comfortable with computer technology sometimes do so. But the commands are too cumbersome and intricate for most novice computer users, involving such non-household acronyms as ATDT and X-MODEM, Y-MODEM and Z-MODEM.
The alternative -- making pleasing sounds -- is not yet economically feasible. Modem manufacturers note their products have a finite amount of computing power and cannot add bells and whistles without sacrificing basic communications performance. To add the bells and whistles would probably create a product priced completely out of a very competitive market.
Moreover, those who say they understand the technology find meaning, even beauty, in the sounds. An engineer said he liked to go to sleep listening to the sound.
"Some people place great importance on the tones," said Dale Walsh, vice president for advanced development for U.S. Robotics Inc. in Skokie, Ill. "They can identify the signals, recognizing each sound combination.
"Actually," he added, "I find the sound extremely pleasing."
