HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Both Japan and U.S. Get Some Support in Dispute on Trade

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: GENEVA, May 29

 BODY:
Japan picked up support in the World Trade Organization today for its contention that Washington's trade sanctions against it violated the rules of the organization.
But trade diplomats said there was also support for the American counterclaim that Japanese regulations and the sales practices of Japanese car makers made it difficult for foreign manufacturers to compete on equal terms in the Japanese market. 
 The two nations indicated today that they were still far apart on how and when to move toward resolving the dispute over American demands that Japan open its market for American cars and automotive parts. Two weeks ago, Washington declared it would impose 100 percent tariffs on imported Japanese luxury cars unless the two sides could reach agreement by June 28.
Today, the Japanese rejected an American proposal for negotiations in Washington late next month, urging instead that negotiations be held sooner, preferably in Geneva, where the World Trade Organization is based.
At a meeting today of the trade organization's Council on Trade in Goods, the European Union's Ambassador, Jean-Pierre Leng, said that even an advance announcement of sanctions in a dispute could not be accepted under the organization's rules if it affected trade.
Trade officials said envoys from Australia, India and Indonesia also condemned "unilateral actions" -- a reference to the American imposition of the tariffs.
Mr. Leng said that while the European Union disapproved of the methods used by the United States to try to resolve the dispute, "we approve of the aim in view." Despite some changes, he said, Japan had not yet done enough to change its domestic practices.
The council's session was the first time the dispute, widely seen as a major test of the big powers' readiness to work through the new rules, had come before a W.T.O. gathering.
"If they can bully the Japanese and still claim they are going through the W.T.O., how much easier will it be for them to lean on us?" an Asian trade diplomat asked.
But Andrew Stoler, deputy chief of the United States mission to the W.T.O., told the council that since the sanctions had not yet gone into effect, there was no violation of the group's rules. Imposition of the tariffs "can be avoided if our two Governments can reach a mutually satisfactory solution of the matter," he said, according to an official text.
On Friday, Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, proposed that the two countries hold talks in Washington on June 20 and 21. Hisashi Hosokawa, the Director General of the Japanese Government's International Trade Policy Bureau, said after the council meeting today that Japan could not accept Mr. Kantor's dates since they fell outside the timetable of the trade organization's dispute-settlement process. Japan began that process on May 17 by asking the United States for urgent consultations on the legality of sanctions.
Mr. Kantor refused to accept urgent treatment of the dispute while sanctions were pending. Under the normal W.T.O. procedure, consultations would begin by June 15.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Subic Bay Rises as an Industrial Hotbed

BYLINE: By EDWARD A. GARGAN 

DATELINE: SUBIC BAY, the Philippines

 BODY:
When the United States left its largest military base in Asia in 1992 -- with the pointed encouragement of the Philippine Government -- some here thought the future of the area was departing, too.
With the memory still fresh of the looting that followed the American abandonment in 1991 of nearby Clark Air Base, there seemed little hope that much would be made of Subic Bay Naval Station. A total of 42,000 Filipinos lost their jobs at the base with the American departure, and the region's surrounding economy was deprived of hundreds of millions of dollars in spending by American sailors and marines. 
 But Richard J. Gordon, the mayor of the neighboring town of Olongapo, was determined to make use of the substantial resources left behind: thousands of acres of dockyards, housing, an airfield, beaches and jungle. He wanted to create a zone for economic growth and development that could serve as a model for the rest of the country.
And, apparently, he has succeeded. Today, investors are pouring into Subic Bay, lured by the infrastructure of roads, buildings and other facilities left behind by the Americans; by the skilled English-speaking work force and by the absence of duties. Taxes, while not the lowest in Southeast Asia, are a flat 5 percent of gross revenues. So far, nearly 19,000 jobs have been created in the free-trade zone that Mr. Gordon oversees as chairman of the Subic Bay Metropolitan Authority.
Duty- or trade-free zones exist elsewhere in Asia, most commonly in China, where the country's overall economy is substantially regulated. Some of the Chinese zones, like the one in Shenzhen across the border from Hong Kong, have proved wildly successful locales for foreign and domestic investment, while the zone in Xiamen in Fujian province has floundered.
Vietnam, too, has created several economic zones, including an export-processing zone in the north, but has not yet attracted much foreign investment. Sri Lanka, by contrast, has seen its export-processing zone near the airport in Colombo thrive with substantial investment from Asian garment manufacturers.
But, as Annabel Betz, an economist at Baring Securities (Hong Kong) Ltd., noted, "Nothing is as high-profile as Subic."
The Federal Express Corporation will begin using Subic as its Asian hub in July, and the Enron Corporation, an American energy company, has built a power plant here that sells electricity to Manila. The Coastal Corporation has chosen Subic as an Asian transshipment point and has taken a 35-year lease on the oil storage tanks here.
Asian companies, though, are making up the bulk of investors here, from Sheng Guard Company Ltd., a small Taiwan cookware company, to Acer, Taiwan's largest manufacturer of computers. A Malaysian hotel group has established a hotel and casino complex and a young Australian has started a bungee-jumping business from an old naval crane.
Wong Wen Chieh oversees the manufacture of telephones for Thomson Audio Philippines, a division of the French multinational Thomson S.A. "We started operations in November," he said. "Initially we had 150 people, now we have 640. We hope to get up to 3,000."
Mr. Wong said Thomson was drawn by the supply of highly trained technicians and engineers in the Philippines but came with a memory of the country's past instability. Skepticism was quickly overcome, though, he said, and "now, we're talking about phase 2, to build a new high-end facility here."
One measure of the economic zone's appeal is the speed with which tenants gobbled up space in its first of three light industrial parks.
"We opened to the public last October," said Cheng Hsien-jung, president of the local development office that manages the three parks. "By the end of March we were fullly booked, 52 companies. The first few investors we gave some incentives, but after that no.
"I would say there's no comparison with any other industrial park in Asia," added Mr. Cheng, a Taiwanese investment banker. "Here there is a first-quality airport, a first-quality port. You have roads, power. In mainland China, you have to start from scratch. Here you have everything."
Ultimately, Mr. Cheng said, $181 million in investments should pour into the park, creating an estimated 20,000 jobs on space that American servicemen once used as a skeet-shooting range.
One of the largest investors here is Federal Express, which will use Subic's airfield at Cubi Point as its Asian hub beginning in July.
"Subic offers us a number of things," said Cliff Deeds, the managing director for market development and communications. "Geographically, it's centrally located within Asia, which allows us to hook up North America to our Asia network. It has a substantial infrastructure. It has not only a deep water bay, it has 24-hour airport capability. It has its own power generator plant, which insures an uninterrupted power supply. It has a free-trade zone. The operating costs there are competitive with other ports in the region."
Subic also draws local start-ups attracted by the benefits or simply fleeing the crime and poor business conditions prevalent elsewhere in the country. "We haven't had a mugging, certainly no rapes, no shootings," said Leo H. Larkin, who opened a leather-goods factory with his wife, Leila. "We just don't have that here.
"We were experiencing 4-, 6-, 8-hour brownouts in Manila," he added. "We decided this was the place -- a good source of labor, power, everything. We came with 38 people and in 19 months we have 276 people."
Mr. Gordon, the Mayor of Olongapo, is neither shy about his accomplishments nor his ambitions. He was appointed by the Philippine President, Fidel V. Ramos, to a six-year term as chairman of the Subic authority. But Mr. Gordon, a member of an opposition party, has never denied his intention to use Subic's success as a platform for a higher political office, even a run for the presidency.
In an office filled with the products of Subic's factories, Mr. Gordon spoke of his plans for Subic Bay. "Basically, what people want in terms of this country is speed," he said. "I'm going to improve the ports, build a new terminal at the airport and we're going to build a new road to Manila.
"There are a lot of competitive countries in Asia," he said. "But in a fair contest, the Philippines will beat anyone."

SECTION: Section D; Page 9; Column 6; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 480 words

HEADLINE: Drug Prices Overstated, G.A.O. Says

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
The Labor Department overstated inflation rates in prescription drugs in some parts of the market by 23 percent to 36 percent from 1984 to 1991, the General Accounting Office said in a report released today.
The G.A.O. said the Bureau of Labor Statistics had used a "market basket" of drug prices that did not give enough weight to new and recently introduced drugs during that period. Government statistics showed drug prices rising, on average, at triple the rate of general inflation during the 1980's and early 1990's. 
 But Senator William S. Cohen, Republican of Maine, who requested the G.A.O. study, said Congress should still be concerned about "high drug prices, which still represent one of the highest out-of-pocket health care costs to consumers."
Some corrective changes have already been made in the bureau's Producer Price Index, the study noted. Early last year, the Government's statisticians began more frequent and comprehensive samplings of drugs.
But the drug index still does not account for the savings or the higher costs that consumers face when they switch to lower-priced generic copies of brand-name drugs or to new higher-priced drugs, the G.A.O. said. It said the bureau disagreed with researchers' proposals for identifying low-priced generic drug substitutes.
The price index also does not reflect improved products like drugs with fewer side effects. On that point, the Government statisticians and outside researchers agreed that they lacked sufficiently refined methods to adjust the index to reflect the improvements.
Senator Cohen said that although competition has reduced prices for big employers and health maintenance organizations that can get discounts from drug makers, this has hardly helped people who have to pay for their drugs. "Tragically, it is often senior citizens and low-income families who must pay the highest prices for their drugs," he said.
Mr. Cohen, chairman of the Senate Committee on Aging, said that a new treatment for multiple sclerosis, for example, was "priced at more than $10,000 a year."
During the 1980's and early 1990's, there were loud complaints about drug prices from the elderly, who are the main buyers of drugs, and President Clinton singled out drug pricing for criticism in an unsuccessful campaign to change the nation's health care system. Most big drug companies promised to hold down price increases and keep them within overall inflation rates. And some economists attacked the Government statistics, saying they were faulty.
Stephen W. Shondelmeyer, a professor of pharmaceutical management at the University of Minnesota, said that "a number of pharmaceutical companies had slowed their price increases, although prices have rarely decreased."
"Two or three of the companies are still raising prices by 6 to 8 percent," well above the current inflation rate, he said.

HEADLINE: Steel Mini-Mills Could Bring Boon or Blood Bath

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
The American steel industry, transformed by the mini-mill movement in the 1980's, is again undergoing a wrenching change.
This time two forces are at work. A second generation of mini-mills -- small, highly efficient factories that use scrap instead of iron ore -- is springing up to produce sheet steel, one of the last bastions of traditional steelmaking. And companies of all sizes are experimenting with new technologies to simplify the manufacturing process. 
 The result is likely to be good news for consumers as cheaper steel reins in price increases on products from automobiles to home appliances. The American trade balance should benefit, too, as imports become less competitive.
But the industry faces a potential blood bath. The rush of competition might force not only the shutdown of aging mills but also threaten some of the more thinly capitalized new entrants.
"Some of these guys will be coming in with limited product lines and a raw materials shortage," said Richard Wardrop, president of AK Steel, a traditional company. "Within a few years, some of them are going belly up."
The mini-mills, pioneered by the Nucor Corporation of Charlotte, N.C., drove established companies like the USX Corporation's subsidiary, U.S. Steel, and Bethlehem Steel mostly out of the business of making construction bars and beams in the 1980's.
Then, in 1989, Nucor burst into sheet steel with a new mill in Crawfordsville, Ind. The company, which has grown into America's fourth-biggest steel producer, has since expanded production at that site, built a second sheet steel mill and announced plans for a third.
Its success has prompted a wave of imitators. Eight companies or groups, including start-ups like Steel Dynamics and established giants like LTV, British Steel and BHP of Australia, have announced plans for sheet steel mini-mills in the South and Midwest. Industry analysts say most of the new mills will be nonunion, allowing them to hire cheaper labor and keep production costs low.
As new competitors enter the market, some mini-mills are experimenting with steelmaking processes that could revolutionize the $30 billion American steel industry. Fearing a shortage of high-quality scrap, they are developing substitutes made from iron ore, but without the hugely expensive blast furnaces, coke ovens and treatment furnaces used at traditional mills.
Nucor, for example, has built a pilot plant in Trinidad to convert iron ore, which is iron oxide, into iron carbide. The iron carbide can be added to an electric furnace as a substitute for scrap. Once the first plant -- designed to produce 1,200 tons of iron carbide a day -- is in operation, Nucor plans to build four more of equal size to supply its own plants and to sell to other companies.
The company has also formed a venture with U.S. Steel and Praxair Inc. to convert the iron carbide directly into steel, saving large amounts of energy and bypassing intermediate steps. F. Kenneth Iverson, the chairman of Nucor, estimates the process could cut costs by $30 to $50 a ton for a product that sells for $270 a ton in economic downturns.
Other companies are experimenting with casting steel directly from liquid steel into thin strips, which would eliminate the need for the huge rolling machines that squeeze and elongate slabs of steel into coils of thin sheet steel.
Steel analysts say all the recent activity is building to an inevitable outcome: overproduction, leading to an industry shakeout. If all the new mills announced are built, they would add at least 15 million tons of steel by the year 2000 to a market that consumes about 60 million tons of sheet steel a year in good times, but which shrinks to about 50 million tons in downturns.
"It seems as though a light bulb turned on over everybody's head at once," said Keith E. Busse, president of Steel Dynamics Inc. in Butler, Ind., which is set to start operation later this year. He said supply is likely to overwhelm demand in a few years, with the result that some projects would be scrapped and some older mills closed.
Mr. Busse was the manager of Nucor's Crawfordsville plant during its start-up. The success of that operation gave him the standing to raise money for a mill of his own.
According to Michael Gambardella, an analyst with J. P. Morgan, the Crawfordsville plant "redefined" the sheet steel business, cutting the capital costs of building and equipping a mill by 85 percent, to $250 a ton of annual production from $1,700 a ton in a traditional mill.
The end of this century could be a rerun of the late 1970's and the 1980's, when competition from mini-mills and imports forced the traditional steelmakers to close aging mills with a capacity to make 65 million tons of steel, or 35 percent of total American capacity. Employment in the industry had plummeted to 171,000 last year from 512,000 in 1980.
The rush to build more sheet steel mills has already discouraged some companies from taking the plunge. A year ago, officials of Birmingham Steel were talking eagerly about expanding into sheet steel to broaden their existing bar and wire businesses. Now Birmingham says it will delay making any investment until the situation clears.
"We read about something like 18 new mills in the United States and decided to stay in long products," James A. Todd Jr., Birmingham's chairman, said in a recent meeting with analysts.
But not all the news is bleak for American producers, who have made a remarkable comeback in the last decade. That is because the new low-cost mills, aided by the weak dollar, should help displace imports. Peter F. Marcus, a metals analyst at Paine Webber Inc., estimated that the price of a metric ton (about 2,200 pounds) of a basic grade of steel is about $500 in Germany and $600 in Japan, compared with $400 in the United States. He estimated that imports would drop to 24 million short tons (of 2,000 pounds) this year and to 17 million tons by 1997 from 30 million last year.
The mini-mills, however, are not likely to escape unscathed. As Nucor continues to expand from a base of economic strength, analysts say, some thinly financed mini-mills might be squeezed between low prices and higher raw materials costs.
"I use 10 percent scrap; the mini-mills use 100 percent scrap," said Paul J. Wilhelm, president of the U.S. Steel Group of USX. "Who is going to be more affected by the price of scrap, which looks like it is going to stay very high?"
Others point out that steel made from scrap tends to have more impurities than sheet made from iron ore. They say that Nucor has not yet demonstrated that it can supply the big markets in automobiles and appliances, which demand the highest quality steels with an unmarred surface.
But the dollar could grow stronger and the mini-mills could hone their skills to produce the kind of sheet steel suitable for the hood of a luxury car. If demand slackens, the integrated mills would be stuck with their high fixed costs while the variable costs of the mini-mills, notably scrap, would tend to decline.
Mr. Iverson of Nucor, who made his reputation and built a $3 billion company on taking markets away from traditional integrated steelmakers, sees them declining further. "There is going to be more shrinkage," he said. "There are still inefficient facilities left."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Nucor Steel mini-mills, like this one in Crawfordsville, Ind., are among the highly efficient plants using scrap instead of iron ore and producing sheet steel. (Todd Buchanan for The New York Times)(pg. D1)
 
Graph: "Mini-mill Newcomers" shows scheduled start-up dates for mini-mill newcomers set to challenge Nucor from 1989 to 1997. (Source: J.P. Morgan)(pg. D8)

HEADLINE: Primark to Buy VNU Unit In Financial Information

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: HAARLEM, the Netherlands, May 29

 BODY:
The Dutch media company VNU N.V. said today that it would withdraw from the United States financial data business by selling the financial information company Disclosure to the Primark Corporation for $200 million.
The sale includes VNU's entire financial information services division. The Dutch company will retain its American trade magazines and nonfinancial data bases. 
 The transaction "means we're pulling out of the financial data business," said Lout Schoelvinck, a VNU director.
Primark will become owner of Institutional Brokers Estimate System Inc. and get a 50 percent stake in Worldscope/Disclosure International Partners.
Primark, based in Waltham, Mass., is already active in the financial information business with its Datastream and Vestek units. Data-stream offers on-line historical economic and financial information in 40 countries worldwide.
"Disclosure is a profitable company, but when you look at the entire field, it only plays a small role, next to bigger players like Reuters, Bloomberg and Datastream," Mr. Schoelvinck said.
"If VNU wants to be a serious player in financial data, it means substantial investments are needed," he said. "We have chosen not to do this, also because we were able to get a nice price for Disclosure."
Disclosure, which is based in Bethesda, Md., has annual sales of about $88 million and employs about 950 people. VNU added I.B.E.S. to the Disclosure group last June, when it bought the earnings estimates service from Citicorp.
The sale will result in a "significant" one-time gain, VNU said. Those proceeds, estimated by analysts at about $100 million, will be used to expand other United States publishing activities concentrated on nonfinancial services.
The three other United States divisions of VNU are VNU Marketing Information Services, Bill Communications and BPI Communications. BPI publishes leading music and film industry trade magazines. The Dutch company bought BPI last year for $220 million. VNU bought Bill Communications, which publishes 11 magazines, last October.
In the Netherlands, VNU is the largest publisher of consumer magazines. It also publishes newspapers and trade magazines and is involved in commercial television.
The Disclosure unit provides financial data to analysts, researchers and law firms. Its data base holds information on more than 11,000 listed American companies.

HEADLINE: Orders for U.S. Machine Tools Fell 42% in April

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Orders for American-built machine tools tumbled 42 percent in April from a record showing in March, but year-to-date demand remained strong and is deriving strength from healthier markets abroad, a trade group reported yesterday.
Machine tool industry orders fell to $335.05 million in April, down from a revised record total of $577.45 million in March and $413.50 million in April 1994, according to the Association for Manufacturing Technology. 
 Nonetheless, for the first four months of 1995, orders totaled $1.67 billion, 17.6 percent ahead of the $1.42 billion in the corresponding 1994 period. Export orders received through the end of April totaled $198.95 million, more than triple the $60.25 million in the period last year.
"This month's order data clearly signal that our overseas markets are recovering," said Albert W. Moore, president of the association, which is based in McLean, Va..
The overseas strength is partly a result of the lower value of the dollar, which has made a broad range of American exports more affordable to foreign buyers. Economic improvements in Europe and Asia also have generated more demand for machine tools and other manufacturing equipment.
Machine tools are used in industries ranging from consumer electronics to airplane assembly. They are an important gauge of future economic activity because they reflect projections for sales by manufacturers.
Higher orders suggest factory managers are optimistic and want to refurbish their equipment to satisfy growing demand. Lower orders suggest factory managers see little need to expand or improve their capabilities.
Orders can fluctuate sharply from month to month, making it more useful to look at a trend over several months. Still, the April decline occurred against a backdrop of growing economic weakness within the United States.
Last week, for example, unexpected sharp drops were reported in domestic factory orders for long-lasting goods and sales of existing homes, while weekly claims for unemployment compensation shot up.

HEADLINE: Virgin Air to Raise Agents' Commissions

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Virgin Atlantic Airways said last week that it would give travel agents 15 percent commissions on economy-priced flights from Boston, Milwaukee and Newark to London and on connecting flights to Athens.
The move by Virgin, the British carrier that is part of Richard Branson's Voyager Group, is good through Sept. 30 and undermines the efforts of big airlines in the United States to cap commissions. 
 On Feb. 9, Delta Air Lines said it would do away with the standard commission of 10 percent by capping payments at $50 for a domestic round-trip ticket and $25 on a one-way ticket. The other big carriers followed.
Earlier this month, Trans World Airlines moved to grab some market share from its rivals by discarding the cap on its commissions to travel agents.
Travel agencies contend that the reduced commissions may force some of them out of business. Some agencies have vowed to direct their business away from carriers imposing the caps.

HEADLINE: Democrats Hid Their Lobbyists

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "Authors of the Law" (Op-Ed, May 24):
Representative George Miller takes umbrage with the way Congress is going about its business. He is upset about the involvement of industry in writing legislation and maintains this is not what the last election was all about. It is exactly what the last election was all about. 
 For 40 years, Democrats held sway in Congress and ruled consistently with their party philosophy. The "lobbyists" used to write legislation were put on the Government payroll and called Congressional staff. When individuals and corporations would not act as they would have them act, laws were passed and regulations promulgated to make the country conform to their view of the world order. Thus, in the cloistered environment of Congress, developed the concept of Truth.
Republicans, without opportunity to oppose the Democratic steamroller, sought personal gratification in the private sector. In self-defense, some were even employed to interact with the Government. They are derisively referred to by Representative Miller as "lobbyists."
Now that the Republicans exercise power, they are consistent with their party's philosophy. Congressional staff is reduced and government power de-emphasized. Private-sector expertise is sought, rather than opinions of Government bureaucrats.
The private sector is concerned with efficiency and global competition: keeping the wheels of industry turning, employing people and generating wealth for the nation. Regulations in this spirit will be forthcoming. Representative Miller, on the other hand, would regulate industry with little respect to economic impact.
 
TIMOTHY J. FREDRICKSON
Brooklyn, May 25, 1995

HEADLINE: Don't Cut Scant Foreign Aid More

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In your May 23 report on Secretary of State Warren Christopher's statement that he was urging President Clinton to veto a House bill that cuts foreign aid, you say polls show this aid to be the least popular item in the Federal budget, and that's why Congress has targeted it for cuts. 
Polls can, of course, be used to "show" anything. If you take Jesse Helms's approach and ask people if they want to pour money down foreign ratholes, they say no. If you ask them if they want to help feed starving children in Africa, they say yes.
One issue that comes through on several polls is that Americans think over 15 percent of the Federal budget is spent on foreign aid. When they discover that the true figure is less than 1 percent and that we are the most miserly nation in the industrialized world, they may change their tune and say aid should be increased. The Republican attempt to trim the minuscule foreign aid budget even further is unconscionable.
Also unconscionable, the cuts are being made to the poorest nations. The proposals would cut aid to Africa by more than 30 percent, while the relatively well-off countries of Israel and Egypt would still get a massive $5.2 billion (far more than all 48 countries of sub-Saharan Africa combined).
What about the Republican proposal to merge the Agency for International Development into the State Department? General Accounting Office studies found no evidence that it would bring significant savings.
Furthermore, such a merger would only exacerbate the problem that has plagued A.I.D. since its inception: that of being too closely tied to United States geopolitical concerns. During the cold war, A.I.D. was infamous for supporting dictators who were United States allies.
Billions of dollars were spent shoring up some of this century's most shameful rulers: Ferdinand E. Marcos in the Philippines, Mobutu Sese Seko in Zaire, Anastasio Somoza in Nicaragua.
A.I.D., under its present director, J. Brian Atwood, is finally getting depoliticized and focusing more on community development. Merging it with the State Department, which emphasizes government-to-government ties and short-term crises, would be a step backward.
Contrary to Republican neo-isolationist views, we live in a truly global world, The fall in the Mexican peso wipes out tens of thousands of United States jobs. Destruction of the rain forest in Brazil affects the air we breathe. The AIDS epidemic does not respect borders.
Turning our backs on our neighbors through this penurious costcutting imperils our own future as well. If the Republican bill passes, President Clinton should take Warren Christopher's advice and veto it.
 
KEVIN DANAHER
Senior Analyst, Global Exchange
San Francisco, May 23, 1995

HEADLINE: BBC's World Service: A Cultural Lifeline To Educated People

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES 

DATELINE: CAIRO

 BODY:
The streets in Algiers had cleared two hours before the nightly curfew. The guests at the El Djazair Hotel, who would not go out until morning, settled into their rooms, and many -- out of either boredom or devotion -- switched on their short-wave radios to the BBC World Service. Minutes after an unsettling ripple of automatic fire echoed from one of the side streets, the service began to broadcast William Shakespeare's poem "Venus and Adonis."
Even as the sun with purple-color'd face
Had ta'en his last leave of the weeping morn,
Rose-cheek'd Adonis hied him to the chase;
Hunting he lov'd, but love he laugh'd to scorn. . . . 
 For many people, the World Service is a lifeline, especially in remote parts of the world, offering a variety of cultural programming that ranges from last year's three-month series of Shakespeare plays to programs on Homer or 20th-century books, to "Composer of the Month," a regular feature on the life and work of such artists as Emmanuel Chabrier and Frederick Delius. There are short-story readings and documentary series, including one that studied the daily lives of ancient Egyptian villagers. One special program celebrated the 300th birthday of Voltaire, who called liberty of thought "the life of the soul."
Every month the World Service broadcasts short stories written by listeners from around the world. There is an annual play-writing competition, which last year drew 3,000 aspiring writers living outside the British Isles, as well as a program called "Poems by Post," where listeners can write in to request their favorite pieces of poetry.
"Culture can inform all sorts of debates, certainly political debates," said Alastair Lack, the head of English programs at the British Broadcasting Corporation, in a telephone interview from London. "Who can understand Bosnia unless you understand the history and culture of that area? You can't understand it just by looking at a daily news bulletin. When you dig beneath the surface of how people live, you find that people are an amalgam of all sorts of memories, desires, interests, all of which go into the making of culture."
Among the World Service's listeners are government officials, intellectuals, artists, students, diplomats and foreign correspondents in the Middle East, Asia, Africa and Latin America. The BBC says it has an audience of 37 million for the English-language service and 130 million over all. (The service is broadcast in 41 languages.) The typical World Service listener is also someone who has gone out of his or her way to find it. (In the United States, it can be found at 7325 KHz on a shortwave or worldband radio.)
"He or she is usually of an inquiring mind and has an interest in a society beyond his or her own, and obviously has a certain level of education," Mr. Lack said. "When you think of the enormously high standards of education in a country like India, we are not talking about a narrow elite but a well-educated group of people who increasingly speak better English."
The service has also become one of Britain's most effective instruments for maintaining both its prestige and reach as its political and economic power have diminished. "It is important for any country to do this," said Mr. Lack. "There is an enormous desire to learn more about life, to explore life and to contribute to it culturally. It is something we ignore at our peril, if we want a fair and interesting society."
"If we forget our history we lose perspective," he said. "We lose the belief that individuals have something to contribute, that the great painters, musicians and writers of the past are continuing into the present and provide riches."
Extensive as it is, the World Service's cultural programming does not take up as much air time as its news programming, which is the most comprehensive in the English language. It includes the "Newshour" three times a day and a variety of current affairs programs, among them Alistair Cooke's "Letter From America." News and current affairs constitute about 55 percent of the BBC World Service's programming, with 35 to 40 percent devoted to the arts. The rest of the programming is turned over to sports, religion, education, science and other topics.
But its emphasis on the arts is what makes the World Service special. "One of the reasons for doing this is that there is a market for it," said Penny Tuerk, the service's commissioning editor for English programs, in a telephone interview from London. "Other people aren't doing it."
Terry Anderson, the former chief Middle East correspondent for The Associated Press, who spent nearly seven years in captivity in Beirut, was a regular listener to the World Service before his kidnapping and during his last year and a half of captivity. Now living outside New York City, he said in a telephone interview that he still listens to the World Service.
"There is nothing like it offered in the U.S.," he said. "Around the world the BBC has enormous influence because it reaches an audience that is important, an educated elite that often does not have access to the kind of things the BBC brings. It is unique."

HEADLINE: CONFLICT IN THE BALKANS: THE OVERVIEW;
ALLIES RESOLVE TO BOLSTER U.N PEACEKEEPING IN BOSNIA; U.S. WEIGHS A GROUND ROLE

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: THE HAGUE, Tuesday, May 30

 BODY:
The United States, Britain, France, Germany and Russia resolved this morning to expand the size of the United Nations force in Bosnia and give it heavier equipment to deter any repetition of raids that have left more than 325 peacekeepers as hostages of the Bosnian Serbs.
And for the first time, a senior Clinton Administration official said on Monday that the United States was not ruling out contributing American troops in a combat role, specifically a commando action to free the hostages. 
 Asserting that they would not let the hostage-taking blackmail the United Nations into withdrawing its 22,500 peacekeepers, the United States, Russia and the European powers endorsed a plan drafted by France that would regroup the United Nations force into larger, easier-to-defend contingents and give it more aggressive rules of engagement to ward off attacks.
To that end, Britain announced that it would send at least another 5,000 troops to Bosnia. France said it was sending the aircraft carrier Foch and helicopter gunships to support its contingent. The United States, which has sent the aircraft carrier Theodore Roosevelt to the Adriatic Sea, is also sending 2,000 marines there.
Anthony Lake, President Clinton's national security adviser, said in Washington that the deployment of the marines, who are trained in commando operations, was "a precautionary measure," adding, "No decision has been made yet to send them into combat."
But another senior Administration official insisted that a commando role could not be "excluded."
The foreign ministers of United States, Britain, France and Germany made their call for strengthening the United Nations Protection Force at an emergency four-hour meeting held here late Monday night with Russia's Foreign Minister, Andrei V. Kozyrev. The five countries form the so-called contact group delegated to lead international efforts to end the fighting in the Balkans.
The United States and the European nations went into the meeting hoping to win Russia's endorsement of the plan to strengthen and reconfigure the United Nations force.
The officials called on the Bosnian Serbs to free the peacekeepers immediately but gave few clues about how they would persuade the Serbs to release the hostages.
Peacekeepers from Britain, France, Canada and other countries were seized after the United Nations, with strong American urging, called in NATO planes to bomb a Serb ammunition depot on Thursday and Friday.
In a communique the foreign ministers issued at 2:30 this morning, they gave a stern warning to the Bosnian Serbs, saying they would "hold Pale leaders personally accountable for the security of U.N. personnel taken hostage."
Referring to the United Nations force, a senior United States official said, "The taking of hostages underscores the need for Unprofor to be beefed up and for measures to be taken to reduce the vulnerability of the troops so the risk of hostage-taking and retaliatory actions is reduced, if not eliminated."
The official, who is traveling with Secretary of State Warren Christopher, said the Administration might also lend unspecified logistical support to reconfigure the United Nations force. Such a move is expected to involve reducing the functions of the peacekeepers, while concentrating them into larger, more defensible groupings that can better carry out their remaining functions.
"We believe the U.N. needs to be regrouped so it's less vulnerable," said Britain's Foreign Secretary, Douglas Hurd.
France's new Prime Minister, Alain Juppe, has threatened to withdraw France's peacekeepers unless the United Nations force is strengthened and its mission redefined so it can be more effective.
While focusing on military options, the foreign ministers also said they hoped to pursue diplomatic avenues to end the conflict, by redoubling efforts to persuade President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia to recognize Bosnia's territorial integrity. Such an action would further isolate the Bosnian Serbs, who control about 70 percent of Bosnia's territory.
The ministers convened their emergency meeting here to discuss the hostage crisis and the future of the United Nations mission in Bosnia. Those talks will continue today and on Wednesday in a different forum: a NATO foreign ministers meeting in Noordwijk, a Dutch seaside resort just north of The Hague.
In Brussels Monday, the 15 nations of the European Union backed France's and Britain's call to strengthen the United Nations force.
The ministers said there was a need to secure the peacekeepers' right of freedom of movement and especially their right of access to the six zones declared "safe areas" by the United Nations. The ministers also said there was a need to provide a rapid reaction force to protect peacekeepers in emergencies.
A senior American official said that because the United States has no troops in Bosnia, the Administration was deferring to Britain and France, which have the largest contingents of peacekeepers, about what should be done with the United Nations force and how the hostage crisis should be resolved.
"I don't think you are going to a see a change in our position that American ground forces are not going to be introduced into the conflict," the official said. "So that, as a result, means we have to work with our partners rather than tell them what to do."
An American official said there seemed to be little alternative but to negotiate with the Bosnian Serbs since Britain, France and other countries with troops being held hostage fear that new air strikes or other tough action could lead to the death of those hostages. Some American officials say they want to make sure that further air strikes are not ruled out as an option, adding that the Bosnian Serbs must not be allowed to get away with what the Administration has called "terrorist" behavior.
At a news conference this morning, Mr. Christopher said, "The use of air power must remain an option." Administration officials acknowledged that this was left out of the communique because of Russian objections.
American diplomats said the contact group was leaning against having the peacekeepers pull out of hard-to-defend Muslim safe-areas in eastern Bosnia, including Srebrenica and Gorazde. These officials said France opposed it because those safe areas were already so vulnerable to a Serbian takeover.
But the United Nations Secretary General, Boutros Boutros-Ghali, has suggested withdrawing from those enclaves because the United Nations forces there could be overwhelmed by Serbian troops and because he wants to the peacekeepers to do fewer jobs, but better.
Officials traveling with Mr. Christopher said they were dismayed that United Nations leaders did not do a better job of insuring that the peacekeepers were in defensible positions before last week's air strikes.
At their meeting, the United States, Russia and the European powers discussed what should be done next to persuade Mr. Milosevic of Serbia to recognize Bosnia.
An American diplomat said a deal with Mr. Milosovic "was probably 80 to 85 percent complete" when talks between the Serbian President and Deputy Assistant Secretary of State Robert Frasure fell apart last week. The diplomat said the United States and Mr. Milosevic had agreed to the terms of recognition of Bosnia, the step-by-step process of lifting economic sanctions and the expansion of patrols along Serbia's border to stop smuggling to the Bosnian Serbs.
But the talks fell apart over Mr. Milosevic's insistence that he would recognize Bosnia only in return for an irreversible lifting of the sanctions against his country. The United States insists that Mr. Milosevic should be offered no more than suspension of the sanctions so they could be reimposed if Serbia stopped contributing to peace efforts.
 
Balkan Update
 At THE HAGUE, Secretary of State Warren Christopher and the foreign ministers of Britain, France, Germany and Russia resolved to bolster the United Nations force in Bosnia to deter the Bosnian Serbs from making new raids on peacekeepers and taking hostages.
In BRUSSELS, the 15 nations of the European Union backed France's and Britain's call to strengthen the United Nations force.
Britain dispatched two gun batteries to the Croatian port of SPLIT, from where they were to move into Bosnia. These British units would be under United Nations command and provide the peacekeeping force with artillery for the first time.
The Bosnian Army said Serbs unleashed heavy artillery attacks near MOSTAR, on the main road connecting Split with Sarajevo.
British officials said they would send at least 5,000 more ground troops to Bosnia, while France dispatched the aircraft carrier Foch to the ADRIATIC SEA, where the American aircraft carrier Theodore Roosevelt and 2,000 marines were also headed.

HEADLINE: A Conservative Sure His Time Has Come

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: TAMPA, Fla., May 27

 BODY:
When Patrick J. Buchanan appeared here at the Hillsborough County Republican Party dinner three years ago, several audience members shouted him down and stormed out of the ballroom to protest his drive to deny President George Bush his party's nomination.
But when Mr. Buchanan stood before the same group this week, there were shouts of "Go, Pat, Go!" and a foot-stomping standing ovation. "The time and the man have come together," Mr. Buchanan said triumphantly in an interview. 
 This was to be the year Mr. Buchanan disappeared in a crowded field of conservative look-alikes. The man who embarrassed Mr. Bush in 1992 -- winning 37 percent of the New Hampshire primary vote as the sole voice on the party's right -- was given little chance of making similar waves when he entered the 1996 race this March. After all, candidates with five times his $1.5 million war chest, bigger organizations and far more endorsements, like Senators Bob Dole of Kansas and Phil Gramm of Texas, were already scrambling for the turf he had staked out.
Yet the 56-year-old Mr. Buchanan is hardly disappearing. While he is still viewed as an unlikely nominee, the former television commentator and conservative columnist is a lot closer to driving the race than to being driven out. Unhindered by the need to moderate his positions with an eye to the general electorate, he is pulling the primary campaign farther to the right with a blend of full-throated social conservatism and economic nationalism in which he advocates "a New World Order to restore our sovereignty."
While Mr. Buchanan's beliefs are too harsh for some Republicans, those who dominate the party's primaries are the hard-core conservatives who may not flinch at his message.
Although his influence could be dismissed nine months before the first primary election, Mr. Buchanan has also frustrated his better-financed rivals by placing second to Mr. Dole in some national polls. And he is no novice to the national stage: He and Mr. Dole are the only candidates who have sought the White House before.
Some Republicans contend that Mr. Buchanan is not running because of any realistic hope that he can win, but to pull the party to the right.
"When Republicans talk about the possible threat of a Buchanan candidacy," said former Representative Vin Weber of Minnesota, who is backing Mr. Dole, "the question that comes up again and again and again is: Does he mind if it does great damage to the Republican Party? Some people think he'd like to punish the party for ideological transgressions."
Mr. Buchanan rejected the notion that he was on a crusade to bring the party around to his point of view. "I've won that battle," he said. "Jack Kemp's talking about shutting down HUD. Richard Lugar is talking about abolishing the I.R.S. Pete Wilson is talking about illegal immigration. And Bob Dole is talking about a cultural war for the soul of America. Pat Buchanan has won the battle for the heart and soul of the Republican Party. It is not a Bush party any more; it is a Buchanan party."
Still, Mr. Buchanan sounds like a man ready to abandon his party should he not become its nominee. He warned that he might establish a third party if spurned by the Republicans, particularly if an anti-abortion plank is not in the Republican platform. "To turn your back and walk away from right to life," he said, "I think you would bring into being an instant third party which would gradually become a socially conservative party."
Mr. Buchanan is a natural campaigner, and he is drawing crowds who seem entertained, if not won over, by his punchy delivery that was honed over the years on programs like CNN's "Crossfire" and from writing speeches for Presidents Ronald Reagan and Richard M. Nixon.
As the others try to finesse their stands against abortion, the party's most internally divisive issue, Mr. Buchanan is demanding that it be front and center in the battle for the nomination. He vows that his first act in office will be to rescind executive orders by President Clinton that ease abortions. In the last week or so, Mr. Buchanan further set himself apart by calling on Congress to defy the Supreme Court's ruling denying states the right to impose term limits on Federal legislators. And he was the only candidate who asserted that the Christian Coalition's Contract With the American Family was too timid and should have called for a Constitutional amendment on abortion and total elimination of money for fetal tissue research.
More than anyone else, Mr. Buchanan has disrupted the long-plotted effort of Senator Gramm of Texas to emerge as the leading conservative alternative to Mr. Dole. Mr. Buchanan's relentless attacks on Mr. Gramm have helped cost the Senator the crucial support of prominent religious conservatives. Last month, Mr. Gramm had another setback after he and Mr. Buchanan addressed a conservative group in Tucson, Ariz., and Mr. Buchanan came away with 76 percent support in a popularity poll.
"I'm Phil Gramm's problem and Phil Gramm is my problem," Mr. Buchanan said. "But I think I've run a far better campaign in the last two months than my old friend Phil Gramm has. It's too much emphasis on money and endorsements. Too much old politics."
Then he recited a list of reasons Mr. Gramm is a "leap-year conservative," from the Senator's vote to confirm Justice Ruth Bader Ginsburg to his support of the Americans With Disabilities Act to his support for foreign aid.
Such talk rankles the Gramm camp, though the Senator's loyalists dismiss Mr. Buchanan as an extremist given to the kind of inflammatory remarks that can destroy a campaign. For example, he has attacked the Israeli Defense Ministry's "amen corner" in the Senate; declared that AIDS is nature's "awful retribution" against homosexuals, and said that women were "less equipped psychologically" to perform in some professions.
"I don't view Buchanan as a threat," said Senator John McCain of Arizona, chairman of the Gramm campaign. "He doesn't have the kind of credibility that you need. And he preaches a dangerous isolationism. He even opposed the Persian Gulf war, vociferously, but he doesn't mention it."
Beyond the sniping between Mr. Buchanan and Mr. Gramm, the lingering question among hard-line conservatives is not whether Mr. Buchanan is to the right of Mr. Gramm but whether he is electable.
"He says everything I want to hear," John Williams, 65, a retired telephone company official said after Mr. Buchanan's dinner speech here. "But I don't think he has a chance of winning. I think Phil Gramm will be elected because he has the organization, the money."
Mr. Buchanan, who traveled to Florida mainly to raise cash, faces such doubters again and again, not just from rank-and-file conservatives, but also from prominent figures like Ralph Reed, executive director of the Christian Coalition.
"They want to go with a winner," Mr. Reed said. "The concern about Buchanan is: Can he win?"
By concentrating his limited resources on states with early contests like New Hampshire, Iowa and Arizona, Mr. Buchanan believes a strong early showing can catapult him to become the leading alternative to Mr. Dole. He lays out a game plan of how he can ride to victory with a coalition of religious conservatives and populist-minded Perot voters who applaud his opposition to free trade and feel threatened by immigrants.
Mr. Buchanan lacks the usual resume of a Presidential candidate. He has neither held elective office nor run a Government agency. Indeed, his Government experience had more to do with politics than policy; he was a communications director in the Reagan White House and before that an aide to Presidents Nixon and Gerald R. Ford.
That does not stop Mr. Buchanan from emphasizing that he has far more White House experience than the other candidates. In addition, he said his role as a commentator forced him to form opinions on numerous issues. He stretches to say that as a native of Washington, D.C., he understands bureaucrats: "They're nice people, but they aren't that bright."
Perhaps it is because he has had a platform to speak out on -- without the responsibilities that come with elective office -- that Mr. Buchanan has earned a reputation as a provocateur, and a polarizing one at that. Some leading Republicans will never forgive him for his speech at the Republican National Convention in 1992 about "a religious war" for the cultural soul of America.
On the stump, Mr. Buchanan's positions are anything but muddled.
*On abortion: "I will be the most pro-life President in the history of the United States of America."
*On schools: "We don't need some guy in the Department of Education in sandals and beads telling you how to educate your children."
*On his plan for a five-year moratorium on legal immigration and a fence along 200 miles of the United-States-Mexico border: "If we can send an Army halfway around the world to defend the borders of Kuwait, can't we defend the national borders of the United States of America?"
*On the Clinton White House: "It's a day care center -- children's hour. It's a chaotic White House that's probably in part reflective of the mind of the President of the United States."
*On trade: "If we conducted our arms negotiations as we're conducting our trade negotiations, we'd all be speaking Russian now."
Lyn Nofziger, who first met Mr. Buchanan when the two worked in the Nixon White House, said his old friend's brand of conservatism might be authentic, but might also be uncomfortable for most voters.
"The problem for Pat is that he's too far out in front," Mr. Nofziger said. "Obviously, Pat believes. But he is more of a true believer than most people out there."

The comment -- "They want to go with a winner. The concern about Buchanan is: Can he win?" -- was made by Mr. Buchanan, not Mr. Reed.

HEADLINE: Invader From Asia Increases Gypsy Moth Threat

BYLINE: By JANE E. BRODY 

 BODY:
WHERE have all the gypsy moths gone? Six years ago, millions of trees throughout the Northeast were eerily leafless in midsummer, thanks to the voracious appetite of this European import. Foresters frantically tried to contain the damage by spraying huge tracts with bacteria that infect the leaf-gobbling larvae, while homeowners girdled trees with sticky tape to impede their leaf-bound trek. Hundreds of hairy caterpillars spoiled summer outings by crawling or falling onto walks, decks and picnic tables.
The good news is that probably for the next two or three summers, the oaks, alders, aspens, birches and many other popular shade and forest trees in the Northeast will continue to, as Joyce Kilmer put it, lift their leafy arms to pray and wear nests of robins in their hair. After that, however, swarms of gypsy moths will almost certainly be back with a vengeance, and this time they may be joined by a new and potentially far more aggressive relative: the Asian gypsy moth. 
 The North American gypsy moth, introduced from France to Medford, Mass, in 1869, has spread slowly but inexorably northward, southward and westward, advancing into Quebec, Michigan, Wisconsin, Ohio, Indiana, Virginia, West Virginia and North Carolina. Progress has been slow, partly because of multibillion-dollar containment efforts and partly because the females of the North American gypsy moth cannot fly. Neither do they feed on leaves. About all they do is mate in mid- to late summer, perhaps walk a few inches and then lay their eggs. The eggs remain dormant all winter, hatching into larvae with huge appetites for about 250 different species of trees and shrubs just as they leaf out in spring.
The Asian gypsy moth represents a speedier threat, experts say. It was transported by freighters from Russia, probably in 1991, to the coasts of Oregon, Washington and British Columbia, and it arrived a few years ago with troop shipments from Germany to the Carolinas.
The Asian gypsy moth female, unlike her European relative, does fly, which is how pregnant females got to the ships headed this way. Females may travel up to 62 miles before laying their eggs. Furthermore, their larvae, which are believed to have blown from ships to American shores, have even more eclectic appetites than their North American relatives.
While the latter attack conifers only after eating themselves out of deciduous trees and shrubs, the Asian gypsy moth has a penchant for Douglas firs, pines and spruces, as well as deciduous hardwoods like apples, oaks, alders, willows and poplars -- some 600 species of trees. And after stripping the trees bare, Asian gypsy moths have been known to attack nearby field crops. Two years ago, $25 million was spent to wipe Asian gypsy moths out of the Northwest and another $9 million was spent last year to eradicate them from the Carolinas.
The Asian and native gypsy moths readily interbreed and, as many entomologists feared, it is now clear that some of their female descendants can fly. Although first-generation hybrids can only glide a few yards, Dr. Melody Keena, a research entomologist, has shown that half the females born of a mating between these hybrids and the Asian moth are capable of full flight.
"We don't want flighted females," said Dr. Keena, who works at the United States Forest Service research center in Hamden, Conn. "They will spread the gypsy moth faster."
Her colleague, Dr. William E. Wallner, envisions a further disaster in the making with the relocation of American military personnel now in European countries. "By the end of 1995, 150,000 troops, 190,000 family members, 55,000 automobiles, 45,000 pets and 600,000 tons of personal property will be shipped back to the U.S., not to mention military equipment and munitions," he said. "Even with the accelerated inspection and detection efforts now under way, the sheer amount of material makes the risk of inadvertent introduction of Asian gypsy moths distressingly high."
Dr. Wallner has recently concluded studies in the Russian Far East that suggest a rather simple way to keep ships free of flying female gypsy moths, not only the Asian variety but two destructive relatives, the nun moth and pink gypsy moth.
Dr. Wallner demonstrated that the female Asian moths were highly attracted to the mercury vapor and incandescent lights now used on docks and ships, but if ultraviolet and blue filters were added, few if any moths would fly toward the lights.
To better monitor the arrival and spread of Asian gypsy moths, Dr. Richard G. Harrison, an ecologist at Cornell University in Ithaca, N.Y., has been studying DNA sequences to isolate reliable genetic markers for the moth. At the Hamden laboratory, Dr. Wallner has devised a technique that he says is faster and simpler than DNA analysis. He says he can tell where a moth is from by using an automated and computerized color analysis of the striped larval head capsule.
Dr. Andrew Liebhold, a Forest Service entomologist in Morgantown, W.Va., said DNA tests suggest that Asian genes may already have been introduced into some populations of the North American gypsy moth. Gypsy moths that may have Asian ancestry have so far been found in California, Connecticut, Delaware, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts, Michigan, New Jersey, New York, Ohio, Rhode Island, South Carolina, Virginia and West Virginia.
Dr. Liebhold has tracked the progress of the North American variety, showing that from 1900 to 1912 the moth extended its range by about five miles a year; from 1912 to 1965, by just a little more than a mile a year, but then from 1965 to the present, by 12 miles a year, practically a gallop for an insect that barely moves.
In the 1950's, he noted, millions of acres were sprayed with DDT. This suppressed but did not stop the gypsy moth, and inspired Rachel Carson's landmark work, "Silent Spring," damning the environmental damage caused by persistent pesticides. By the mid-1960's, having failed to stop the moth, Dr. Liebhold said foresters "essentially gave up" and the moth stepped up its spread. Recently, he said, the Forest Service began a new effort -- the Slow Spread Project -- to delay the inevitable with tools such as slow-release pheromones to disrupt mating and bacterial sprays that are relatively environmentally benign.
Other researchers, meanwhile, are investigating viral and fungal weapons that attack the larvae. Dr. Ann E. Hajek, an insect pathologist at the New York State College of Agriculture and Life Sciences at Cornell University, isolated and cultured a fungus, Entomophaga maimaiga, which was responsible for the collapse of the last major gypsy moth outbreak in 1989. Though this fungus was first introduced here from Japan in 1911 to fight the moth, it was thought to have failed in field tests and had not been seen since. It reappeared in 1989 in the carcasses of dead gypsy moth larvae in Connecticut, and Dr. Hajek later found it throughout Massachusetts and in other Northeastern states.
Entomophaga (insect eater) maimaiga (the Japanese name for gypsy moth) secretes enzymes that digest the skin of the gypsy moth caterpillar. The fungus then invades the larval body and eats it from the inside out. This fungus, which preferentially attacks gypsy moths, has produced major epidemics in the caterpillars and caused the large-scale destruction gypsy moth populations. It may be largely responsible for the current lull in gypsy moth damage.
Thanks to this fungus, Dr. Hajek says, there may never be another season like 1981, when gypsy moths defoliated a record 13 million acres in the Northeast. Still, the effectiveness of the fungus in suppressing the gypsy moth may depend on something beyond human control: the local weather. A wet, warm spring favors fungal growth; dry, cool weather does not.
Also promising as a biological control weapon is a nuclear polyhedrosis virus, or N.P.V., that is specific for gypsy moths. Dr. Martin Shapiro, a research entomologist at the Agricultural Research Service's Insect Biocontrol Laboratory in Beltsville, Md., is trying to produce the deadliest possible virus with the longest staying power. To this end, he has devised a sunscreen to protect it; this virus, as well as other biological- control agents, is inactivated by sunlight. Dr. Shapiro has shown that stilbenes, chemicals that are used as whiteners, or "optical brighteners," in laundry detergents, can shield the virus from ultraviolet destruction and at the same time make it more virulent. After a bath in stilbenes, the virus lives longer and kills gypsy moth larvae faster, before they have a chance to eat many leaves. Normally, by the end of its eight-week life, one three-inch-long caterpillar has eaten more than a square yard of foliage.
"This approach potentially has an enormous payoff," Dr. Shapiro said. "Many economically important insects are attacked by viruses that could be enhanced by brighteners. Even viruses that would not naturally kill gypsy moths will do so after a treatment by brighteners."
While DDT, Sevin and other potent insecticides have been banned, diflubenzuron (sold as Dimilin by the Uniroyal Chemical Corp.) is still being sprayed on hundreds of acres each year to kill gypsy moths. According to entomologists at the Nature Conservancy, a private environmental group based in Arlington, Va., this persistent chemical not only kills immature insects that feed on trees, it also migrates into the leaf litter on the forest floor and into streams where other insects and nontarget animals feed.
A general wariness toward sprays among conservationists is not surprising. Dr. Dale Schweitzer, an entomologist with the conservancy, maintains that in northern New Jersey, spraying for gypsy moths was responsible for the disappearance of about two dozen species of moths and butterflies in the late 1950's and early 1960's.
Many kinds of trees do not succumb to insect invasions without fighting back. One major weapon is the production of tannins, which in some species can rise to toxic levels when a tree is besieged by leaf eaters like gypsy moth larvae. The noxious tannins suppress the growth of the larvae and, as a result, adult females lay fewer and smaller eggs. But Dr. Jack C. Schultz, an entomologist at Pennsylvania State University, said this natural defensive action by the tree may ultimately be self-defeating. He reports that high levels of tannins also act as antiviral agents and thus may protect the gypsy moth larvae from the nucleopolyhedrosis virus.
Dr. Schultz is now in the midst of a decadelong study of a 120-acre wood that is highly susceptible to gypsy moth devastation to see what role the interaction of tannins and viruses may play in determining the size of the gypsy moth population.
Dr. Joseph Elkinton, an entomologist at the University of Massachusetts in Amherst, meanwhile, has linked the rise and fall of gypsy moths to fluctuations in populations of white-footed mice, which dine heavily on gypsy moth larvae and pupae. Why do the mice populations fluctuate? "That may have to do with the acorn crop," another favorite mouse food, Dr. Elkinton said. And why do acorn crops fluctuate? "No one knows," he said. "They may vary with the weather."
And so one mystery leads to another, and the saga of the gypsy moth continues, perhaps for another century. After all, in 126 years, Dr. Elkinton said, "no one yet has got a good handle on the causes of gypsy moth outbreaks or why they remain suppressed for long periods of time."

CORRECTION-DATE: June 1, 1995, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Science Times on Tuesday about gypsy moths misstated the commercial status of the insecticide Sevin. It is sold for a number of uses, not including the control of gypsy moths; it is not banned.

No Sign of Compromise In Japan Over Trade
 The senior politician managing Japan's contentious trade dispute with the United States said that he had already made "the maximum possible concession" to Washington and that American sanctions against luxury-car imports would worsen job losses in Japan.
The official, Trade Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto, also accused Washington of reneging on understandings about how the two countries would handle the negotiations over automobiles and auto parts. Mr. Hashimoto now appears resigned to seeing the Administration's punitive trade tariffs go into effect on June 28. [Page A1.] 
 Japan's unemployment rate rose to 3.2 percent in April, its highest level since 1953. [D6.]
 
Technology Stocks Fall Sharply
After leading the market's 1995 rally, technology stocks were shaken by the prospect of slowing sales and by profit taking. The Nasdaq composite index fell 13.17 points, to 858.70. The broader market was mixed. [D8.]
 
Treasury Yields Hit 15-Month Low
 The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond fell to 6.66 percent, down from 6.75 percent on Friday, the lowest in 15 months, on signs that the economy is continuing to slow. [D18.]
 
Trump Is Sued Over Empire State
 A suit filed by two of Harry Helmsley's companies accuses Donald J. Trump of conspiring with a jailed Japanese businessman to extort money from the companies through which Mr. Helmsley operates the Empire State Building. The suit seeks $100 million in damages, but its real purpose seems to be to cause Mr. Trump trouble with his casinos and his plan to sell casino securities. [B3.]

 The on-again, off-again acquisition talks between Electronic Data Systems and A. T. Kearney are on again. A deal could be reached next week. [D5.]
 
Affirmative Action Review
 A White House review concluded that affirmative action is justified in employment and education, but it found problems with programs that reserve Federal contracts for companies owned by blacks or women. [A1.]
 
Group Calls for End of F.C.C.
 A conservative research group tied to Newt Gingrich called for abolishing the Federal Communications Commission, arguing that the agency hurts consumers by blocking competition. [D4.]

 Baring Holding sold half its 40 percent stake in Dillon, Read back to the investment firm for an estimated $30 million. [D2.]

 Shares of Philip Morris began recovering after the company said it was moving quickly on its $100 million recall of more than eight billion cigarettes. [D2.]

 Since Inc. magazine was founded in 1979, its readers have become the recipients of more than half the corporate revenue in the United States. And Inc. has benefited by providing a forum for advertisers seeking to sell to this market. For the first six months of 1995, its advertising pages increased 23 percent. Advertising. [D7.]

 Tens of thousands of public employees in France held a one-day strike to protest the European Union's plans to end some state monopolies. [D5.]

HEADLINE: CONFLICT IN THE BALKANS: IN EUROPE;
U.S. and NATO Demand Quick Release of the Hostages

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: NOORDWIJK, The Netherlands, May 30

 BODY:
The United States and NATO, in their strongest terms yet, warned the Bosnian Serbs to release more than 320 detained United Nations peacekeepers immediately or suffer serious consequences.
The warnings were issued a day after the United States, Britain, France, Germany and Russia called for the 22,500-member peacekeeping force in Bosnia to be strengthened, partly to show the Bosnian Serbs that taking hostages would not intimidate the United Nations into withdrawing. 
 At a meeting of NATO foreign ministers, Secretary of State Warren Christopher addressed himself to the Bosnian Serbs in harsh words.
"We've made known to the leadership of the Bosnian Serbs that we hold them responsible for the welfare and well-being of those who have been taken and detained," he told reporters. "It's a barbaric act. It's uncivilized. It's really not part of any reasonable struggle that might be going on there."
Willy Claes, the NATO Secretary General, was equally tough. "We condemn in the very strongest terms the utterly despicable behavior of the Bosnian Serbs," he said. "We will not be intimidated. We demand that the Bosnian Serbs release unconditionally the U.N. peacekeepers."
Mr. Claes threatened serious consequences if the hostages were not released unharmed, but said it would be unwise to discuss in public what actions NATO might take.
Several American diplomats said that in the interest of protecting the hostages, diplomacy should be given a chance and they sought to avoid discussing any heroic military measures to free the hostages. The hostages were taken after NATO air strikes on a Bosnian Serb ammunition depot last week.
Mr. Christopher announced today that he was sending a senior diplomat to Belgrade on Friday to urge President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia to use whatever sway he has over the Bosnian Serbs to free the peacekeepers.
American officials said that Lieut. Gen. Rupert Smith, the United Nations commander in Bosnia, has been holding talks with Gen. Ratko Mladic, the Bosnian Serb commander, in an effort to win the hostages' release.
At the meeting today in this Dutch resort town on the North Sea, Mr. Christopher and the foreign ministers of 15 other NATO nations held lengthy talks on two subjects other than Bosnia: the expansion of NATO and the alliance's future relationship with Russia.
At a news conference this morning in The Hague, Russia's Foreign Minister, Andrei V. Kozyrev, announced that his country would reverse its stance on the so-called Partnership for Peace, a group of 26 former Soviet bloc countries that cooperates with NATO on security matters. Last December, Mr. Kozyrev suspended Russia's membership in the partnership plan to protest American-led moves to expand NATO.
Deputy Assistant Secretary of State Robert Frasure is to travel to Belgrade to resume efforts, broken off last week, to persuade Mr. Milosevic to recognize Bosnia. The United States and the European powers say such a move will reduce tensions in the Balkans and isolate Radovan Karadzic, the Bosnian Serb leader who is seen as responsible for taking the peacekeepers hostage.
"What we want to do here is divide Milosevic from Karadzic," Mr. Christopher said. "We want to further isolate Karadzic. It perhaps will occur to him that it might be a rather lonely world out there."
Mr. Milosevic wants the economic sanctions against Serbia lifted irreversibly if he recognizes Bosnia, but the United States insists that the sanctions be suspended only temporarily, reserving the right to reimpose them if Serbia interferes with progress toward peace.
American officials said Mr. Milosevic and Mr. Frasure have agreed on stages for easing sanctions and on increasing patrols to prevent smuggling from Serbia to Bosnia's Serbs.
The NATO ministers discussed ideas for strengthening the role of the United Nations force, among them reconfiguring the peacekeepers into larger groups that would be easier to defend; withdrawing tiny teams of military observers in far-flung parts of Bosnia, and providing the peacekeepers with a rapid reaction force to deal with emergencies.
On Wednesday, NATO is to open, for the first time, an official dialogue with Russia in which the foreign ministers will share their views on European security and cooperation with Russia with Mr. Kozyrev.
NATO officials said they were delighted and relieved that Russia had decided to proceed with full membership in the partnership plan because that might signal less Russian resistance to expanding NATO.
"Russia is crossing a new threshold in its relationship with NATO," Mr. Christopher said. "Russia will be moving toward integration with Europe and away from isolation. That's a choice that I feel sure will greatly benefit Russia and Europe and the world as a whole."
NATO official say they will spend much of Wednesday seeking to assure Mr. Kozyrev that NATO is no longer an anti-Russian alliance. They fear, nonetheless, that Moscow remains as firmly opposed as ever to extending NATO eastward.
"The new order of European security, founded on cooperation, must be built with Russia, and not against it," Mr. Claes said.
