HEADLINE: REPORT TO CLINTON HAS A MIXED VIEW ON MINORITY PLANS

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 30

 BODY:
In a sweeping review of the Government's affirmative-action programs, Clinton Administration officials have concluded that most hiring and other preferences based on race or sex are justified in employment and education. But they found problems with some purchasing programs that reserve Federal contracts for companies owned by blacks or women.
A confidential report prepared for the President by Administration officials catalogues numerous forms of affirmative action and analyzes the reasons for "white male resentment" of those practices. 
 The report, prompted by Republican criticism of such programs, lays out standards for acceptable affirmative action: race or sex can be considered as "one among several factors." If numbers are used, they should be "genuine goals rather than straitjackets or quotas." Affirmative-action plans should be reviewed periodically to see if they are still needed. And the rights of "bystanders," including white men, must be respected.
Administration officials said Mr. Clinton would probably announce his conclusions in a broad thematic speech not yet scheduled, leaving agencies to disclose details of any changes in their programs. If Mr. Clinton proposes changes, they are likely to become a starting point for Republicans in Congress who want to go much further, possibly repealing programs that they say discriminate against white men.
When Mr. Clinton ordered the review in February, he evidently hoped to assuage the concerns of white men. But he and his advisers quickly concluded that they would alienate many blacks, Hispanic Americans and women if they pulled back too far in their support for affirmative action.
A senior Administration official involved in the review said, "We concluded early on that the politics were a wash" for the Administration because the political costs would offset any political gains from curtailing affirmative action.
But the official denied that the review was part of a Presidential re-election strategy. "From the beginning," he said, "we have viewed this as a moral and policy exercise unmoored from political calculations -- an attempt by the President to discover and define what he believes. We spend more time talking about constitutional doctrine than the politics of affirmative action."
George Stephanopoulos, senior adviser to the President, said tonight that the review was not complete and had taken somewhat longer than expected because "it's complicated." He refused to discuss any preliminary conclusions. The President's ultimate decision on this issue, he said, "is not a political decision, but will be based on policy and principle."
A third Administration official said that Mr. Clinton would offer "sympathy and understanding" to white men who resent affirmative action. Mr. Clinton indicated his strategy in a speech to the California Democratic Party in April, declaring, "This is psychologically a difficult time for a lot of white males, the so-called angry white males," who feel they have been treated unfairly.
The report upholds most types of affirmative action, saying they increase productivity by reducing discrimination and finding the best candidates for particular jobs. But it expresses concern about programs that establish "hard set-asides," or rigid quotas, for blacks, Hispanic Americans and women.
As an example, the report cites Commerce Department rules for certain local public works programs financed by the Federal Government. The rules say that no grant may be made for any project unless "at least 10 percent of the amount of the grant will be expended for contracts with or supplies from minority business enterprises."
A similar preference is found in the Diplomatic Security Act of 1986. At least 10 percent of the money for construction to improve security at American embassies is earmarked for minority contractors.
Administration officials said such preferences, while legally defensible, might be seen as unfair because they suggested that for certain Government contracts, whites need not apply. President Clinton will be on firm ground if he can say he opposes anything that looks like a hard quota, the officials explained.
In March, Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader, denounced "Government-sanctioned quotas, timetables, set-asides and other racial preferences." He and several other Republican candidates for President, including Senator Phil Gramm of Texas, have vowed to repeal or restrict such preferences. On Thursday, Gov. Pete Wilson of California, a possible candidate for the Republican Presidential nomination, plans to sign an executive order eliminating some preferences based on race or sex that the state adopted.
Many Republicans say such preferences discriminate against white men and undermine the goal of a color-blind society. But the report to President Clinton says, "The Supreme Court has consistently rejected the argument that the Constitution is color-blind."
The report says "consideration of race or sex is permissible" sometimes, if it is narrowly tailored to overcome the effects of discrimination, to cure "a manifest imbalance" in the workplace or to promote diversity at a college.
The report for Mr. Clinton says white male resentment of affirmative action "seems to be rising" because of "a static or shrinking economy," corporate layoffs and increasing nonwhite immigration, which "may fuel ugly racial sentiments, particularly in tight or transitional economic times."
The Administration says there may be a statistical basis for some white male resentment, because set-asides can "account for more than half of all procurements" in some industries and areas.
Numerical goals, preferences and set-asides have been "very successful in expanding procurement opportunities" for businesses owned by blacks, Hispanic Americans and women, the report says. But "given the shrinking size of the procurement pie, such increases may mean real decreases" for other companies, including small businesses owned by white men, it says.
In addition, the Administration found "a tendency for agencies to concentrate their minority contracting in certain fields such as construction, where there are a significant number of minority firms."
Thus, it said, an agency may set an overall goal of buying 10 percent of its goods and services from companies owned by blacks or women. But, it said, the "goals, set-asides and preferences in some fields can be several times the overall goal," and "it seems likely that resentment of these programs is particularly high" in those industries.
In Congressional elections last fall, 60 percent of white men voted for Republican candidates, according to a national survey of voters leaving the polls conducted by Mitofsky International. Some Republicans say that by challenging affirmative action, they can drive a wedge into the traditional Democratic coalition next year.
But the report quotes President Clinton as saying that affirmative action must not be a "cheap political wedge issue to divide the American electorate."
About two dozen officials took part in the first phase of the affirmative-action review. They examined dozens of preferences based on race or sex, asking, "What works and what doesn't?" A smaller group, composed mainly of White House aides, is trying to define a policy and vision for the President. This group includes Mr. Stephanopoulos and Christopher F. Edley Jr., a Harvard Law School professor who worked at the Office of Management and Budget for the last two years.
Black business executives have lobbied the White House to preserve set-asides and other forms of affirmative action. But an Administration official said there might be some political benefit if such executives criticized the Administration's eventual proposals.
"We want black businessmen to scream enough to let angry white males understand that we've done something for them," said the official, who spoke on the condition that he not be named.
The official said Mr. Clinton, while supporting most forms of affirmative action, had concluded that "making distinctions or judgments using race or sex as a factor has a moral cost." In this, he said, Mr. Clinton differs from some liberals. "The strongest traditional proponents of affirmative action say we shouldn't be worried about the white male bystander or innocent victim," the official said.
But Administration officials said Mr. Clinton agreed with liberals who assert that affirmative action is not meant just to remedy the effects of discrimination. It is also a legitimate tool to promote diversity, they said, and for this reason, colleges may consider race along with other factors in awarding financial aid.

HEADLINE: Harvard Officials Stress the Positive Despite the Most Recent Events in a Year of Trials

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN 

DATELINE: CAMBRIDGE, Mass., May 30

 BODY:
When Neil L. Rudenstine, the president of Harvard University, took an unscheduled leave of absence last winter, suffering from the stresses of his job, he received 1,500 get-well cards from faculty and students.
"A lot of them said, 'I need a break too,' " Mr. Rudenstine recalled during an interview in his office today. " 'Can I join you in the Caribbean?' " 
 Mr. Rudenstine could be jovial until Sunday, when Sinedu Tadesse, a 20-year-old junior from Ethiopia, fatally stabbed her roommate and fellow pre-med major, Trang Phuong Ho, in their dormitory room and then hanged herself.
The deaths have prompted not only horror but also wonder at what could have led to the worst act of violence in memory at the nation's premier university. All the worse, the murder-suicide follows several unwelcome episodes in what has proved to be a remarkably rocky school year: two earlier student suicides, the university's decision not to admit Gina Grant once it found that years earlier she had killed her mother in South Carolina, and embarrassment brought to the campus by an incident involving a Harvard medical school professor.
In his office today, a trim and tanned Mr. Rudenstine, who returned to his post in February, called Sunday's fatal violence "a terrible human thing" but said it should not diminish Harvard's "positive and dynamic" accomplishments during the academic year, which ends with graduation next week.
"This has been one of our best years in who gets in and who comes out," he said, citing a sharp rise in applicants, including almost twice the number of high school valedictorians as five years ago.
The president also noted that Harvard raised $300 million this year -- more, he said, than any university has ever raised before in a single year. "We couldn't raise that kind of money if Harvard were in trouble," he said.
Dr. Robert Coles, a Harvard professor of psychiatry, also urged that the recent troubles be seen in isolation, not as a reflection of some broad difficulty.
"I put in a plea for the way great novelists like Dickens or Tolstoy would see this tragedy: as a matter of fate and chance," he said. "I would not connect them to a larger story about Harvard."
As for the effect of the murder-suicide, Professor Coles said: "It does shock the students. But these students are achievers, and they go on. There's no panic, no evidence of group despair, no mass hysteria."
Albert Carnesale, provost of the university, said of the incident: "That says more about what makes the news than this university. It's clear that this university is doing quite well, although this tragedy is not part of the 'doing quite well.' "
Turning to earlier difficulties, Mr. Carnesale said: "In the Gina Grant case, people have been saying, 'What a wise decision that was!' My guess is that more people would be saying that today than a week ago."
"The same with Neil's leave," he continued. "Many people said what a courageous thing it was for him to do."
And referring to the case of John Mack, the Harvard psychiatrist who wrote a best seller on abductions by space creatures and whose professional ethics have now been questioned by a committee of his peers, Mr. Carnesale said, "Not one person has raised that with me as having anything to do with Harvard."
In the aftermath of Sunday's deaths, the university's crisis intervention teams were called in and have been concentrating on the students who knew the two dead young women, said Fred Jewett, dean of Harvard College, who, like the other officials, cautioned against drawing broad inferences from the university's unsettling year.
He observed that Harvard's graduation rate of 97 percent was, according to the National Collegiate Athletic Association, the highest in the nation. "That speaks to the kinds of success students are having here," he said.
As for the student stress that is legend at Harvard, Bill Fitzsimmons, dean of admissions and financial aid, said much of it was myth.
"They used to say, 'Look to your right and look to your left, and by the end of the year two of you will be gone,' " he said. "But Harvard is the exact opposite of that. The fact that we have the highest graduation rate in the nation proves it."
Mr. Rudenstine agreed. "Stress was a much more common problem in the 60's," he said, "when we had much less support for students."
Suzi Naiburg, senior tutor at Dunster House, where Sunday's events unfolded, is one of many who point out that Harvard's student-support programs, like those at other major colleges and universities, have increased enormously in recent years.
"At Dunster House," she said, "there are 16 resident and 31 non-resident tutors. They are predominantly graduate students. They create study breaks where the students meet socially, they eat their meals with the students, play intramural games with the students and hold 'study buddy' meetings."
Still, a Harvard professor who spoke on the condition of anonymity said students on the pre-medical track had become "hugely competitive," with corruptive results.
"Simultaneous with the recession," he said, "students and their parents came to believe that neither business nor even engineering offered financial security. And so they decided on a career in medicine. Immediately the pre-med courses became jammed, and some students began to cheat" -- for instance, by stealing graded exams that had been posted so that only they, and not other students, would know the correct answers, an advantage that might benefit them in subsequent tests.
Mr. Jewett acknowledged that there had been some suspensions for "academic dishonesty" this year. And even Mr. Rudenstine said stress on pre-med students had been a problem for as long as he could remember.
Particularly "if you have a parent who is a doctor," he said, "you get an awful lot of pressure to have a career in medicine."

HEADLINE: NO SIGN IN JAPAN OF DEAL ON TRADE

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: TOKYO, May 30

 BODY:
The senior Japanese politician managing the country's increasingly contentious trade dispute with the United States said today that he had already made "the maximum possible concession" to Washington, contending that the planned American sanctions would add to job losses in Japan.
The official, Ryutaro Hashimoto, is the Minister of International Trade and Industry and is widely viewed as a leading candidate to become the next prime minister. In a lengthy interview in his office today, Mr. Hashimoto also contended that the Clinton Administration had reneged on previous understandings regarding how the two countries would handle negotiations over automobiles and auto parts. 
 At the same time, other senior Japanese officials interviewed here indicated that they were all but resigned to seeing the Administrations's punitive trade tariffs against $5.9 billion in luxury car imports go into effect as scheduled on June 28. Officials say they expect little or no progress on the issue when President Clinton meets Prime Minister Tomiichi Murayama in mid-June in Canada at the meeting of the seven leading industrial countries. They also say they are pessimistic that talks planned for late June will resolve the dispute before the sanctions bite.
In contrast to past trade disputes with Washington, this time Tokyo appears determined to press its case to the limits. And Mr. Hashimoto has a good deal to gain from standing up to the United States, with his political advisers saying he now stands out from a crowd of politicians who seem confused and directionless after the breakup two years ago of the traditional party structure that commanded Japan for 40 years.
"He is in tune with the present assertive mood," said Yukio Okamoto, a consultant and political analyst, "to push back against American bullying."
Sitting near a desk where he has propped up a sign reading, "Our policy: Rigid flexibility," Mr. Hashimoto clearly personifies that newly toughened stance. Pointedly, he keeps his equipment for kendo, a Japanese form of fencing, propped up in the corner of his office.
Like politicians elsewhere, he made it clear today that his first concern was jobs. That is usually not a big issue in a country with an unemployment rate that generally is well under 3 percent, but Japan's full-employment system is under increasing strain from a three-year period of economic weakness.
Mr. Hashimoto said that "employment will be affected" by the sanctions, which would price nearly all Japanese luxury-car imports out of the American market. But he would not predict by how much joblessness might rise.
In fact, Japan's unemployment rate, while still low by Western standards, rose to a record 3.2 percent in April, adding to fears that the nation is slipping back into recession.
Mr. Hashimoto stands out from the typically bland Japanese official as a flamboyant and frequently fiery figure. And so far at least, he appears to have attracted nearly universal support here for his willingness to face down the United States, even from opposition politicians and bureaucrats who have often clashed with him.
Indeed, several Japanese officials and industry executives said that enduring the sanctions would be worth the cost if the long-term effect was to teach the United States that high-pressure tactics no longer work against Japan.
"In the past Japan has always bent down and yielded at the last moment," Yoshikazu Kawana, the executive vice president of the Nissan Motor Corporation and chairman of the trade committee of the Japan Automobile Manufacturers Association. But "not this time," he said, adding: "We are just trying to survive. The American requests are just too much to accept."
During the last week, Mr. Hashimoto has rather deftly turned the debate over cars from a discussion of whether the Japanese automotive market is closed to imports to one of whether the American sanctions are consistent with world trading rules. Many countries in Asia and Europe have condemned Washington's tactics, largely because they fear they might be the next targets. That chorus of complaint is music to Japan's leaders, and at a news conference with Japanese reporters today Mr. Hashimoto said that the United States should "lend an ear to global opposition."
Mr. Hashimoto argued that as recently as last November, when he met Ronald H. Brown, the Commerce Secretary, and Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, he had been led to believe that the United States would not insist on new "voluntary plans." Those plans, termed "voluntary" to avoid running afoul of international trade law, commit Japanese auto makers to buying more American parts. They were begun in 1991, when President George Bush visited here, accompanied by American automobile executives.
But Japanese officials have never been comfortable with the arrangement and "Mickey Kantor said that he agreed that voluntary plans were outside the framework of the talks and outside the Government's reach," Mr. Hashimoto said. But Mr. Brown argued, Mr. Hashimoto said, that such "voluntary plans" should be included, and Mr. Kantor quickly changed his position.
Mr. Kantor, interviewed by telephone today, said he had a different memory of those events. Although the "framework accord" signed two years ago by President Clinton and Kiichi Miyazwa, who was then the Prime Minister, made no mention of voluntary plans, Mr. Kantor said, such plans "are part of the overall discussion of autos" and have been for years.
"I'm afraid he either misunderstood or that he heard only what he wanted to hear," Mr. Kantor said.
Mr. Hashimoto's oral sparring came as Japanese auto manufacturers began to assess the potential cost of the sanctions, which will be retroactive to May 20 if they are imposed at the end of June. The Honda Motor Company said today that it estimated that the sanctions would cost it $211 million this year, assuming it was forced to stop production of the luxury cars it sends to the United States, the Acura Legend and the new Acura 3.2 TL.
The Toyota Motor Company and the Mitsubishi Motors Corporation both said today that they would essentially stop production of luxury cars headed for the United States. The Mazda Motor Corporation, which is in extreme financial distress, has already stopped its export of a car covered by the tariffs.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Ryutaro Hashimoto, Japan's chief trade official, says new sanctions will mean job losses in Japan. (Associated Press) (pg. D6)

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: Advertising;
To reach entrepreneurs and growing companies, many marketers count on Inc. magazine.

BYLINE: By Leonard Sloane 

 BODY:
AT a White House briefing this month, the leaders of the businesses on the Inc. magazine list of the 500 fastest-growing privately owned companies were greeted by top Federal officials. Then they left for Norfolk, Va., for an annual three-day conference for executives from the current Inc. 500, as well as heads of previous companies on the list.
All this is grist for the mill for Inc. as it strives to maintain its role as idea generator and advocate for the nation's small to medium-sized growing companies. Since its founding by Bernard Goldhirsh in 1979, the magazine has seen the members of its audience become the recipients of more than half of the corporate revenues in the United States. And Inc. has benefited financially by providing a forum for advertisers seeking to sell to this market. 
 "There has been a radical shift in the economy, in which small growth companies have become huge engines for job creation," said James J. Spanfeller, Inc.'s publisher.
"Competition has moved large companies toward downsizing," he added, "while many smaller companies, by contrast, are growing rapidly and investing in their infrastructure. If this swing continues, we'll become more widely recognized."
To reach the readers of Inc., owned by Goldhirsh Group Inc., marketers bought 644 advertising pages during the first six months of 1995, a gain of 23 percent from the corresponding period in 1994. That increase comes atop an 8 percent gain last year, when advertisers bought 1,221 ad pages.
The largest advertising category for Inc. is technology, followed by automotive. Other advertising mainstays include travel, insurance and financial services.
"We have to work on a number of consumer categories," Mr. Spanfeller said. "That includes apparel, jewelry, watches and upscale distilled spirits."
Many current advertisers describe themselves as enthusiastic about how Inc. has carved out a niche in the competitive field of business magazines as a title for growing companies.
"The one neat thing about Inc. is that it is not a trade magazine per se," said Kerry Dyer, a vice president of the Young & Rubicam New York unit of Young & Rubicam Inc., whose clients include AT& T, Digital Equipment personal computers and Xerox.
"It is a magazine that was about the new economy long before there was a new economy," he added. "It definitely has a unique focus."
Peter Levin, advertising director of the Cadillac division of General Motors in Warren, Mich., said: "Entrepreneurs are extremely key to our buyer prospects. Those people are looking for a car that represents a statement for them. And we can reach entrepreneurs through Inc. magazine."
Inc.'s audited paid circulation for the last half of 1994 was about 646,000 copies, maintaining the relatively flat pattern of recent years. At the beginning of 1995, though, the magazine raised its rate base, the circulation guaranteed to advertisers, by 10,000, or 1.6 percent, to 650,000.
"On the outside, we'll get up to 750,000 between now and the end of the decade," Mr. Spanfeller predicted.
Frequency has also advanced this year, from 12 to 18 issues a year, as Inc. increased its coverage of computer systems and other new technology. An additional two issues are being considered for 1996.
And along with other magazines, Inc. is stepping up what the trade calls multitiered marketing programs. In an effort to bring its readers in closer touch with the magazine, Inc. sponsors four to five conferences annually, operates a series of expositions in different cities and produces books, software and video and audio tapes.
"There are a variety of ways of talking to our marketplace," Mr. Spanfeller said. "It's all part of the economic continuum."

HEADLINE: China Said to Triple Oil Imports From Iran

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: NICOSIA, Cyprus, May 30

 BODY:
Iran will earn more than $400 million a year after winning an agreement to triple its oil exports to China, the official Iranian press agency IRNA said today.
An agreement for Iran to increase its oil sales to China to 60,000 barrels a day from the current 20,000 barrels was reached at a meeting of the eighth Iran-China joint economic commission held in Beijing, the agency reported from the Chinese capital. 
 The deal with China comes just weeks after President Clinton issued an executive order that stopped United States oil companies from buying Iranian oil. United States companies had until recently bought nearly a quarter of Iran's total crude oil exports of 2.6 million barrels. But the White House, citing Iran's support of international terrorism, has now severed this oil trade.
The new Iran-China oil export deal covers crude oil and refined petroleum products. Iran is the world's second-largest exporter of oil.
Iran's vice president for executive affairs, Hamid Mirzadeh, told the agency that Iran would also invest more than $25 million in China's oil refining business because of growing demand in the Chinese domestic energy market.

HEADLINE: M.T.A. Seeks To Call Off Mediation

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

 BODY:
For the first time in its history, the Metropolitan Transportation Authority has decided to forgo extended mediation with its railway unions, which could mean a strike against the Metro-North commuter railroad as early as June 23.
Historically, the railroad has taken full advantage of provisions in the Federal Railway Labor Act to put off a strike for as long as several years. But the M.T.A.'s chief negotiator told the unions yesterday that extended mediation has not been effective and that having a firm deadline would force serious bargaining. 
 "The dispute that we have with our work force is not going to be resolved by continued nonbinding mediation," Gary J. Dellaverson, director of labor relations, said in an interview. "It can be resolved with collective bargaining. It can be resolved without a strike, and it should be resolved without a strike."
For their part, union leaders said that if there is no agreement by the June 23 deadline, they will walk out, halting rail service for 95,000 commuters, primarily in Connecticut and Westchester County.
"There will absolutely be a strike" if there's no contract, said James T. Joyce, chairman of the Brotherhood of Railroad Signalmen, Local 160.
Anthony J. Bottalico, general chairman of the United Transportation Union, which represents Metro-North conductors, said the railroad "is trying to force a job action, so that they can run down to Congress and ask Congress to impose a settlement that they like." He added, "I'm not saying that the unions are going to walk off the property, but right now, I don't see what would stop them."
Just as the M.T.A.'s change in strategy would allow the unions to strike, it would allow the M.T.A., Metro-North's parent agency, to impose a wage freeze and work-rule changes that the unions have rejected.
Under Federal law, either side could extend mediation until October. The governors of New York and Connecticut, George E. Pataki and John G. Rowland, can also trigger extended mediation, but they are backing the M.T.A. position. Last year, while campaigning for re-election, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo directed the M.T.A. to back down on work-rule demands to end a strike on the Long Island Rail Road.
The time is unusually ripe for the M.T.A. to confront the unions, with newly elected Republican governors in both states who have pledged to reduce the size of government, who owe little to organized labor and who do not face re-election campaigns for another three years.
"Our unions have to understand, as have other public employee unions, that they have a role to play in insuring the continued stability of the region's economy by helping us to control costs," said E. Virgil Conway, the M.T.A. chairman.
But union officials note with satisfaction that a Metro-North strike would hit hardest at the core constituency of Governor Pataki, who appointed Mr. Conway, in the suburban and Hudson Valley counties north of the city. And they question how long either governor would stomach an extended walkout at Metro-North.
The decision not to prolong mediation applies to talks with 14 unions for contracts for 1995 through 1997, with wages and work rules at the core of the dispute, and it comes on the heels of a bitter struggle over contracts for 1992 through 1994.
The move demonstrates M.T.A. officials' deep frustration with the Railway Labor Act, whose delaying provisions are generally seen as favoring the railroads rather than their workers. But the officials said the act serves only to extend and worsen contract disputes while still permitting strikes, unlike New York State law, which forbids strikes and does not provide for lengthy mediation.
"If delay had any likelihood of enhancing chances of settlement or avoiding a strike, we would do it," Mr. Dellaverson said. But, he added, "the longer a dispute simmers, the harder it is to solve it."
The M.T.A move comes while memories of previous failures in mediation, like the one that led to last summer's L.I.R.R. strike, are still fresh. On Friday, engineers on the Long Island Rail Road, another M.T.A. agency, staged a one-day wildcat walkout, in part out of frustration with years of fruitless mediation.
Union leaders say that the M.T.A. has calculated that by forcing a strike, it can build favor in Congress for exempting Metro-North and the L.I.R.R., the nation's two largest commuter railroads, from the Railway Labor Act.
"They figure the Republican Congress is going to step in and be on management's side," Mr. Joyce, of the signalmen's union, said.
If Congress exempted the commuter railroads from the Federal law, the L.I.R.R. would come under New York's Taylor law, which forbids strikes. For Metro-North, which operates in two states, New York and Connecticut would have to adopt a new, joint labor law.
Some union leaders said the M.T.A. was acting now because it would rather have a strike in the summer, when there are fewer passengers and more idle school buses available for back-up service, than in October. Mr. Dellaverson agreed that a summer strike would be easier to cope with, but he said that was not a major factor in the decision.
A Federal mediation board recently recommended wage increases for all Metro-North unions of 3 percent beginning July 1, another 3 percent next Jan. 1 and 4 percent on Jan. 1, 1997, which labor leaders say most of the unions would readily accept. The M.T.A. will not discuss its salary proposals, but union leaders say the agency has offered no increase in 1995 or 1996, and a 3.5 percent increase in 1997, which the unions dismiss as unacceptable.
The Metro-North unions have long insisted on parity with the Long Island Rail Road, whose workers earn an average of 5 to 6 percent more than Metro-North employees. The railroad's offer would widen that gap.
The two sides are similarly far apart on work rules. In a prime example, the M.T.A. objects to a contract rule that says all track workers' regular shifts must be scheduled for weekdays during the day, when repairs are often limited by heavy train traffic. M.T.A. officials say this means workers are idle much of the time, and must be paid overtime to work nights and weekends.
"What the board and the unions fail to recognize is we're just not another railroad, we're a government agency," Mr. Dellaverson said. "We, like the rest of government, are constrained to do more with less."

HEADLINE: ABOUT NEW YORK;
When Times Were Slower And Crime Was Lower

BYLINE: By Michael T. Kaufman 

 BODY:
ARE things worse, crimewise, than they were in the old days? Was your grandmother right when she remembered that you could once walk anywhere at any time without worrying, or has nostalgia put a mellow gloss on years when she was young enough to skip easily out of harm's way. Weren't there people at the Five Points who would bite anyone's ear off for a quarter? What about the Plug Uglies, the Dead Rabbits, Murder Inc. and the hatchet men of the Chinese tongs who menaced the citizenry?
A group of students from John Jay College have just completed a research project that addresses this issue. Realizing that there is no index of lawlessness, no available Dow of mayhem that averages out crimes and misdemeanors on a day-to-day basis, the students spent last semester poring over court records and came up with a criminal profile of the residents of one Lower East Side building over decades of teeming immigration, overcrowding and poverty. 
 The five-story building in question is 97 Orchard Street, which now happens to be the Lower East Side Tenement Museum. It was built at the start of the Civil War and became a densely settled home for waves of immigrants from Germany, Ireland, Eastern Europe and Italy. Teams of historians and genealogists, commissioned by the museum, had earlier identified more than 1,300 people, or about half of those who passed through the building, sharing first privies, and then toilets, in the hall, as they chased their American dreams.
FOR much of its existence, 97 Orchard Street was a place of small, sometimes windowless rooms, where on occasion mattresses were rented out to people sleeping in shifts. There was much poverty and unemployment. Hygienic conditions were often dismal, and child mortality was high. In 1890, close to 100 people were living in the place. Surely crime must have been as common as grime. Surely, as former Mayor Ed Koch liked to say, "The good old days weren't so good."
"Before we looked into it, I just assumed that with such crowded conditions, crime must have been a serious problem," said Christopher Martinez, a sophomore at John Jay who worked on the project. "I know how crazy I get sometimes sharing a bathroom with just one person, so I could imagine how aggressive people must have felt to share with 20 or 30 neighbors."
As part of a course called "Immigration, People and Politics," the students checked through docket books of the old Magistrates Court for each year from 1899 to 1930, looking for defendants who were residents of 97 Orchard. These records are on microfilm at the New York City Municipal Archives, whose director, Kenneth Cobb, boasts of having the most complete annals of crime in the English-speaking world. "We've got records of every offense, misdemeanor and crime in the city from 1684 to 1966," he said.
So what did the students find? Pretty much, bubkes.
In 1903, Julia Hennesy, who was 60, was sentenced to six months in the workhouse for intoxication. In 1927, William Sher, another resident, was imprisoned for three days for obstructing the sidewalk, presumably with a pushcart. In all, 45 residents of the building had been hauled into Magistrates Court, the so-called Tribune of the People, in a three-decade period to face charges that included disorderly conduct and violation of corporate ordinance, which usually meant doing some kind of business without a license.
One man was charged with violation of the cigarette tax. A few paid fines of $1, $2 or $3 for obstruction, and one man was charged with loitering. Two people were fined for alcohol offenses during Prohibition, and one man was charged with larceny, but like almost all the other cases, the charges were dismissed.
IT turns out that even though gangsters like Monk Eastman and Big Jack Zelig were organizing gangs and shaking down businesses in the neighborhood, there were no signs that the people at 97 Orchard had guns or were involved in gunplay. They were not linked to prostitution or gambling or drug activity. One possibility, of course, is that they were very good at getting away with more serious crimes. Another is that they were overwhelmingly law-abiding and your grandmother was right.
Police Officer Ralph Torres of the Seventh Precinct, who went to the museum to look at the student's findings, which were turned over to the museum, thought they were significant in their pettiness. He thought it strange that police had the time and will to press such cases.
"It's very different what we contend with nowadays," he said. "Our most common arrests are robbery, burglary and drug offenses. I do not remember any arrests or citations for obstruction or cigarette tax violations."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bonds Surge As Weakness In Economy Feeds a Rally

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The bulls showed the credit markets yesterday that they were still in charge as Treasury securities continued their rally amid recent signs that the economy was slowing with inflation in check.
The rally was helped by optimism that statistics this week, especially the May employment report on Friday, would show further evidence of weakness. Traders said the signs were leading some investors to speculate that the Federal Reserve's next move, after pushing rates up seven times, would be to shift to easier credit to keep the economy from stalling. 
 The 30-year bond surged 17/32 , to a price of 11212/32 for a yield of 6.66 percent, down from 6.75 percent on Friday and the lowest in 15 months; the yield was 6.64 percent on Feb. 23, 1994.
Traders said the cash market outperformed the futures market in early trading yesterday, typically a bullish sign. Talk that the Fed may come into the market to buy $4 billion to $4.5 billion of bills of various maturities for its own account also buoyed the market. The Fed move would be a way of adding reserves to the banking system.
Often dealers use the Fed purchases as an opportunity to clear some of their excess inventory. They will even mark up prices in anticipation.
Another event that seemed to accelerate buying interest in the afternoon was an interview by the Market News Service with a Fed governor, Lawrence B. Lindsey, who indicated that wage gains could not be expected to fuel a reacceleration of the economy. One trader said he was surprised since comments from Fed officials were usually measured and neutral.
Much of yesterday's gains came from overnight trading as market participants said that central banks that have been buying dollars might step up purchases of Treasury securities as well.
"It's a fuzzy relationship," said James Hale, a fixed-income analyst with MMS International in San Francisco, of the connection between dollar buying and Treasury purchases. He said foreign central banks were "massive" buyers of dollars since the beginning of the year and recently of Treasuries as well.
Elsewhere in the Treasury market, the sale of new three- and six-month bills went without a hitch. But the surprising part of the auction, dealers said, was the presence of strong foreign demand, presumably from central banks and specifically the Bank of Japan.
The average rate for the three-month bill was 5.64 percent, down from 5.72 percent and the lowest since May 8, when it was 5.63 percent. For a six-month maturity, the average rate was 5.61 percent, down from 5.72 percent last week, and the lowest since Oct. 31, 1994, when it was 5.51 percent.
In the latest measure of consumer confidence, the Conference Board's May index fell to 101.6, compared with 104.6 in April. Despite the three-point drop, confidence remains at a fairly high level. While there have been signs of a slowing economy, consumers still continue to feel secure about their jobs, family income and the immediate future of the economy, the survey showed. But that does not mean they are ready to spend, according to Ray Worseck, chief economist and manager at A. G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis.
Mr. Worseck said he suspected that the reason the economy was softer than expected in the last month or so was connected with what has been happening in Washington. Discussions on spending and radical tax changes are contributing to uncertainty, and that is becoming a drag on the economy, as people delay making major spending decisions, he said. Mr. Worseck predicted that the economy's underlying fundamentals would eventually reassert themselves, however.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence Slips a Bit, Yet Remains Relatively Strong

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Americans' optimism in the economy's future performance weakened in May, the Conference Board said yesterday, though confidence levels remained relatively high.
The board, a business-sponsored research organization, conducts a monthly survey based on a sample of 5,000 households. 
 It said results of its latest survey showed consumer confidence fell 3 points, to 101.6 in May, from a revised 104.6 in April. The April reading was originally reported at 105.5.
"Although the latest confidence readings are somewhat less optimistic than in April, the current level remains strong," said Fabian Linden, executive director of the board's Consumer Research Center.
"While there have been recent signs of a slowing in economic growth, consumers still continue to feel secure about their jobs, their family income and the immediate future of the economy," he added.
The economy has cooled recently from the strong growth of last year, and some economists are concerned that it could slip into recession. Home sales fell a steep 6.4 percent last month, even though mortgage rates are at the lowest levels in a year. Orders for durable goods had their largest drop in more than three years, falling by 4 percent in April.
And unemployment is up, with the number of Americans filing for new state jobless benefits at the highest level in more than two and a half years.
The Conference Board said its survey showed about 24 percent of all consumers said that business conditions were "good," while a moderate 17 percent said they were "bad." Both figures are little changed from April.
Slightly more than 22 percent said that jobs were plentiful, while 24 percent said they were hard to find. The board said both figures were only marginally less positive than a month earlier.
Concerning the future, the board said optimists continued to outnumber pessimists by a margin of almost two to one. Nearly 17 percent expect business conditions will be better in six months, while less than 9 percent fear they will worsen.
Slightly more than one in five consumers surveyed expect their family incomes will rise in coming months, the board said, while only 8 percent foresee a drop in incomes.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Steady as Traders Await Friday's Report on Employment

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar was steady against most leading currencies yesterday, supported by expectations that the United States employment report on Friday would show enough strength to dissuade the Federal Reserve from cutting interest rates.
"Everyone is gearing up for Friday's nonfarm payrolls numbers," said Joe Francomano, a currency dealer at Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank in New York. "If the number is up, hopefully this dollar slide will end. My opinion is that there's no way the Fed will ease rates." 
 Lower interest rates in the United States would hurt the dollar by making dollar-denominated bank deposits less attractive.
The dollar was also bolstered by comments from a Bundesbank official hinting at the possibility of lower German interest rates, which would weaken the mark.
"We cannot rule out another reduction in interest rates," the official, Reimut Jochimsen, said. The central bank last cut German lending rates on March 30, when it lowered the benchmark discount rate by half a point, to a six-year low of 4 percent. The Bundesbank's policy-setting council meets tomorrow.
Trading was thin yesterday, with many dealers inactive ahead of this week's economic reports. On Monday, American markets were closed for Memorial Day, and markets in London, the world's biggest center for foreign-exchange trading, were closed for a bank holiday.
In New York late yesterday, the dollar rose to 1.3905 German marks from 1.3787 on Friday. The dollar slipped to 82.86 yen from 82.94.
Talk that the Federal Reserve might cut rates gained momentum last week after a report on Thursday showed a steeper-than-expected fall in orders for durable goods. The report suggested that the economy might be weakening to a point where the Fed would have to cut rates to stave off a recession. The combined Thursday and Friday losses for the dollar were 5 percent against the yen and 4 percent against the mark.
"The big surprise last week was that the economic news was so bad, and the market reacted sharply," said Bob Gruber, senior currency salesman at Chemical Bank.
Currency dealers said yesterday that they would scrutinize the jobs report and other economic reports this week to see whether the durable- goods report gave an accurate picture of the strength of the economy and the outlook for interest rates.
"The market needs confirmation of the durable-goods numbers," said Paul Farrell, manager of strategic currency trading at Chase Manhattan Bank. "I want to see what the employment numbers are."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Japan's Jobless Rate at Highest Level Ever

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: TOKYO, May 30

 BODY:
Japan's unemployment rate rose to its highest level ever in April, adding to fears that the world's second-largest economy was slipping back into recession and entering an era of higher joblessness.
The 3.2 percent unemployment rate was up from 3 percent in March and was the highest level since current record-keeping began in 1953. The previous high was 3.1 percent, recorded in May 1987 during an economic slowdown caused by the sharp appreciation of the yen after the 1985 Plaza Accord. 
 Economists said the jobless rate was likely to increase further in the coming months because the recent surge of the yen had once again weakened an economy that a few months ago seemed on the verge of recovering from a three-year slump.
"This is the beginning of a new era, of a new cycle," said Jesper Koll, chief economist for J. P. Morgan in Tokyo. He predicted unemployment would reach 4.3 percent in about a year.
To be sure, Japan's jobless rate is minuscule compared with the 5.8 percent recorded in the United States in April and European rates that range from 8 to 12 percent or even higher. And a rise from 3 percent to 4 percent is not likely to tear apart Japan's strong social fabric.
Still, an increase would be a psychological blow to a country that has prided itself on providing near full employment. Japan's Government and its leading companies have "sort of been committing ourselves to defending the 3 percent unemployment rate with honor," Hiroshi Okuda, an executive vice president of the Toyota Motor Corporation, said recently.
Rising joblessness could bring added pressure on Japan's already unpopular and fragile coalition Government, which is hobbled by internal fighting and a lack of strong leadership from Prime Minister Tomiichi Murayama.
Economists say Japan's unemployment figures are understated. Anyone who works an hour in the final week of a month is considered fully employed that month. The figures do not count jobless women who return to being housewives rather than looking for new employment. Women, who are often restricted to clerical work and serving tea anyway, are usually not entitled to lifetime employment.
The jobless situation is hitting young workers the hardest. Among people aged 15 to 24, the unemployment rate is 6.4 percent for men and 6.8 percent among women.
That is because Japan's lifetime employment system preserves jobs for those who already have them, forcing companies to cut costs by curtailing hiring of graduating students. April is the time when graduating students enter the work force, but this year, about 160,000 could not find jobs.
Some commentators have suggested that the trouble young people have been having finding jobs might have contributed to the spread of religious cults like Aum Shinrikyo, which the police say is responsible for the poison gas attack in the Tokyo subways in March.
The fear of spreading unemployment is one factor behind Japan's hard-line stance in automobile trade talks with the United States. Japanese auto companies are reluctant to buy more American parts because that could hurt the already struggling Japanese parts suppliers. (On the other hand, if Washington places 100 percent tariffs on imports of Japanese luxury cars, that too will lead to job losses in Japan.)
A big question is whether the need for new cost cutting to cope with the strong yen will mean the end of the lifetime employment system, in which major companies go out of their way to avoid dismissing full-time male employees. Some analysts say lifetime employment may fray but will probably not break, in that there will not be huge dismissals of employees.
"There has consistently been erosion of lifetime employment," said Mineko Sasaki-Smith, senior economist for Morgan Stanley & Company in Tokyo. "I don't think there will be a big bang and it will suddenly be ended."
The rise in unemployment has been caused by a decline in manufacturing jobs, as the high yen reduces production in Japan or forces Japanese companies to relocate overseas. In April, manufacturing employment was down 3.6 percent, or 550,000 jobs, from a year earlier.
Until now, the workers who left manufacturing have been finding jobs in the service sector. But the service companies can no longer absorb so many workers, said Robert Alan Feldman, director of Asian economic research for Salomon Brothers in Tokyo. Increased discounting is causing a slimming down of Japan's bloated distribution system.
Japan's Management and Coordination Agency, which released today's unemployment figures, said that the number of jobless people in April totaled 2.14 million, up 10.3 percent from a year earlier. The number of people with jobs decreased by three-tenths of a percent, to 64.75 million.
The Labor Ministry said today that there were only 65 jobs for every 100 job seekers in April, down from 66 jobs in March.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Soybean Prices Decline Sharply As Fears of a Poor Harvest Ease

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Soybean prices fell sharply yesterday as forecasts for sunny skies and drier weather eased concern that rain-delayed planting would reduce this year's harvest. Corn and wheat prices also fell.
"We're slaves to the weather," said Victor Lespinasse, an analyst with Dean Witter Reynolds in Chicago. "And the weather looks drier." 
 On other markets, silver prices slipped while crude oil steadied. The Commodity Research Bureau index dropped 2.72 points, to 232.75.
On Monday, the National Weather Service forecast below-normal rainfall for the next 6 to 10 days in crop areas in Minnesota, North Dakota, Iowa, Illinois, Indiana and Ohio, and normal precipitation for South Dakota, Oklahoma, and Missouri.
Clear weather ahead will allow soybean farmers ample time to plant, analysts said, with no loss of yield because of a shortened season.
Soybeans for July delivery at the Chicago Board of Trade fell 26 cents, to $5.7975 a bushel, the biggest one-day decline since last July 5.
Corn and wheat prices also fell, as the drier weather let farmers resume work following three weeks of excessive rain that left fields too wet to plant.
Soft red winter wheat for July delivery fell 12 cents to settle at $3.735 a bushel on the Chicago Board of Trade. July corn fell 4.25 cents to settle at $2.6675 a bushel.
Silver led prices for precious metals lower amid perceptions that a weakening economy offers little chance for accelerating inflation.
Consumer confidence fell during May for the first time in three months, according to the Conference Board, as economic growth cooled. The report helped to send bond prices soaring, pushing the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond to 6.66 percent, its lowest in 15 months.
"The economy is slowing down; interest rates are lower; there'll be less chance of inflation," Leonard Kaplan, chief trader at Imperial Bank in Los Angeles, said.
Silver for July delivery fell 16.5 cents, to $5.295 an ounce on the Commodity Exchange of New York, its lowest since April 10. June gold fell $1.40, to $385.20 an ounce.
Crude oil futures steadied after a 3 percent drop on Friday. Yesterday, crude oil for July delivery rose 9 cents, to $18.78 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.

HEADLINE: Tariff Won't Help American Car Makers

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The proposal by President Clinton and Mickey Kantor, the United States trade representative, to increase tariffs on Japanese luxury imports is a classic example of one step forward, two steps back. 
 The Clinton Administration is like a quarterback in the closing minutes of a tight game who breaks free up the middle but, harried, confused and pumped up with adrenalin, scores a winning touchdown for the wrong team. Dampening sales of Japanese cars to fight unfair trading practices is a long-awaited move, a step forward, breaking free up the middle.
Unfortunately, Mr. Kantor's economic advisers decided to put the tariff on upscale Japanese performance sedans, a subcategory of the United States car market in which we have no viable entries.
As a result, if the tariff goes into effect, Lexus, Infinity and Acura sales will plummet, but American car makers won't benefit. German car makers -- BMW, Mercedes -- will enjoy the fruits of our crack strategists' labors.
 
KEVIN J. CLANCY
Auburndale, Mass., May 25, 1995
The writer is chairman of a marketing investment strategy group.

GRAPHIC: Drawing.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: Vidalia-Onion Rivals Seek Use of the Name

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
BUDDY TALLEY takes pride in growing sweet onions that rival Georgia's renowned Vidalia onions, but he can't sell his Tifton Sweets at Vidalia prices.
He is among a small group of Georgia farmers who cultivate the same varieties as Vidalia growers do but are prohibited by law from using the famous name because they are outside the official growing area. 
 Under the state's 1986 Vidalia Onion Act, misuse of the Vidalia name can carry a fine of up to $20,000.
Mr. Talley, who lives in Tifton, 55 miles from the closest part of Vidalia country, and others are pushing to have the growing area expanded. It encompasses 13 counties and parts of seven others in southeastern and south-central Georgia.
Georgia's 200 certified Vidalia growers benefit from a marketing program that has made the onions famous. The promotion has helped to more than double the value of the Vidalia onion crop in 10 years.
Gary Fairchild, a professor of food and resource economics at the University of Florida, said Washington apples, Idaho potatoes and Michigan cherries benefited from similar programs. "The key is trying to restrict it to a certain area," he said. "If you can find an area that has the climatic and soil conditions that you can differentiate with the consumer, then you're limiting the supply and enhancing the price."
Vidalia, a town 80 miles west of Savannah, is where the onions were introduced. Growers claim they are the sweetest in the world because of mild winters and low-sulfur soil.
But Willie Chance, a University of Georgia onion specialist, said the soil throughout south Georgia is basically the same well-drained sandy loam, ideal for growing sweet onions.
Five of the official growers don't even live in the growing area, but they were included because they were growing before the Vidalia law was passed.
Researchers at the University of Georgia's Coastal Plain Experiment Station, near Tifton, have grown Vidalia-type onions for years for pungency and sweetness tests. When the university opened a new Vidalia onion laboratory earlier this year, it was in Tifton, not Vidalia.
Mr. Talley has been growing onions for 10 years, but his Tifton Sweets are still relatively unknown.
He said five years of tests show his onions are not only sweeter but also milder than some Vidalias. But because they do not carry the famous name, Tifton Sweets sell for 25 percent to 50 percent less than Vidalias.
Mr. Talley says he believes sweetness, not geography, should make a Vidalia a Vidalia. And the industry appears to be leaning his way.
"I think there will come a time when we will have a pungency level as a requirement," said Shirley Manchester, the manager of the Vidalia Onion Committee, an industry group.

HEADLINE: LOOKING FOR LUNCH WITH: Alix Kates Shulman;
A Feminist at Walden Pond

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL 

 BODY:
AS she strolled through Central Park, Alix Kates Shulman, the feminist novelist and social advocate, looked like any number of well-seasoned New Yorkers. The acceptance of a certain age seemed to soften the web of concern in the corners of her eyes. Her pace was still purposeful, even though these days Ms. Shulman tends to pursue the wild and the edible with the passion she once reserved for tossing off sexual conventions and gender stereotypes.
She kept pointing to and expounding on the park's edible greens. 
 "Dock! Yellow dock!" she cried out. "The leaves can be steamed with garlic, like spinach, but it has a delicious, citric taste. And it produces millions of little brown, wonderful seeds that I use baking muffins or banana bread. And dandelions! If you pick the buds before they flower and pan-fry them, they're delicious, almost as sweet as zucchini flowers."
To hear the voice that has for three decades provided a lyrical narrative of the changing position of women in American society sounding like Euell Gibbons is to worry the fate of feminism. At least initially.
But ambling along in sensible black sneakers, Ms. Shulman, who is 62, tread this troubling notion as carefully as she did the grass around clumps of dandelion greens, patches of purslane and miniature forests of wild chives in the park.
Ms. Shulman said that in 1982, feminism was stagnating on the national agenda. Her first novel, "Memoirs of an Ex-Prom Queen" (1972), sounded a clarion call for feminism, but the politics of the 1980's, along with a tepid marriage and an empty nest, she said, "were making me feel irrelevant, on the road to obsolete." And so in 1982 she went to the family's vacation shack for six months. Alone on a rocky jut of a Maine island, she tested the mettle of her tightly wrapped city soul against the vicissitudes of nature and embarked on a 10-year odyssey that she describes in "Drinking the Rain," a memoir that Farrar, Straus & Giroux published last month. Without benefit of electricity or indoor plumbing -- to say nothing of a deli -- the cause-after-cause, deadline-after-deadline, die-hard feminist eked out meals from the woods and bay, lived by the tides rather than the clock, looking for dinner, she wrote, as well as for "who I am when the tide runs out."
On the island, slogging through slippery seaweed and prodding mud for mussels, snails and clams, she stopped analyzing the world long enough to understand it as she experienced it: a complicated collage whose components are as interdependent as they are paradoxical -- male and female, rich and poor, privileged and oppressed, wild and tame, cooked and raw. Dinner is never far from Ms. Shulman's conversation.
She is not ashamed to say that she cooks every day. "It's true that cooking became a symbol of women's oppression, so there's a certain paradox in glorifying it, a certain danger politically," she said.
Besides, the cooking she talked about -- sweating dandelion greens in garlic over a low, slow flame; chopping wild apples with a Zenlike precision to make chutney; joining land and sea by seasoning steamed clams with minced wild lovage -- recalled spiritual practice more than home economy.
On one level, Ms. Shulman's book could be read as "the feminist goes to Walden Pond." Her retreat to nature, after all, occurred at a time when ecological concerns were becoming more intertwined with feminism and when reaction to technology was, among the middle class, making nature seem romantic again.
"I used to see how everything is connected through the pervasiveness of the gender system," Ms. Shulman said. "After the island, I saw even deeper levels of connection: politics, taste, environment, waste, dinner."
"Drinking the Rain" also echoes books from other leaders of the second wave of feminism. Gloria Steinem's "Revolution Within" (Little, Brown, 1993) and Betty Friedan's "The Fountain of Age" (Simon & Schuster, 1993) address the personal and political issues of aging.
"I thought I was writing of my own unique experience," said Ms. Shulman, who studied history and philosophy as a graduate student. "But when you interact with nature, one of the first things you learn is that no experience is unique, no event is isolated, nothing happens by accident."
Nature, she said, constantly weighs its own paradoxes. And so does she.
For the former full-time advocate, there is a paradox in the fact that she now lives as much of the year as possible in the crude shack in Maine, isolated from politics and ferment.
When the weather makes it impossible to live without heat, she returns to the loft in Chelsea that, for the last decade, she has shared with the high school sweetheart whom she met again after her second marriage dissolved. In New York, she remains active in the pro-choice movement as well as in a number of feminist organizations.
A modern Persephone, she is at ease shuttling between contemplativeness and advocacy. Still, she said, there are political dangers in writing a book about returning to nature and the return to self, particularly at a time when right-wing survivalists are championing nature.
AS she strolled through Central Park, Ms. Shulman identified the paradoxes of her current world view as nimbly as she catalogued her island larder: Scotch lovage, goosetongue, sea rocket, rose hips, goldenrod, sheep sorrel, charlock, bay leaves, wild lettuce and spinach, a panoply of edible seaweed. She takes a certain philosophic pleasure in the abiding, the renewing, the constantly changing.
Her prickly protectiveness of "the natural," "the environment" and the "old fashioned" are the only hints that Ms. Shulman, the contemplative one at one with the world, might tug at Ms. Shulman, the advocate.
"I think of how James Baldwin described the paradoxes of reality," she said, paraphrasing the writer: "One has to hold in mind two ideas that seemed to be in opposition. Accepting life totally without rancor, accepting people as they are, but never, in one's own life accepting reality and injustices as commonplace."
As she walked through the park, she pursed her unglossed lips at the sight of litter scuttling in the spring breeze. Considering the wastefulness of automatic dishwashers and disposable contact lenses, she yanked her short dark hair firmly behind her ear, as if readying for battle. During her walk, she championed the "virtue of frugality."
A breeze, carrying hints of pine and wet grass along with car fumes, sent litter scuttling across Central Park. And her train of thought that seemed destined for the dogma of the virtue of self-control and thrift took a spiritual turn.
"When you strip your life to essentials, you find how little you need and how much you have," she said. "You stop going to the grocery with a shopping list. You go and you look and you smell and you imagine. You interact with what's available. You live in the present tense, confronting real needs, real limits. Maybe I'm not saying this right.
"On the island I realized that corporations and advertising have created this whole consumer culture in which people are always hungry for something they don't have. Buy and dispose, buy and dispose."
The appetite to consume and dispose, she said, pollutes and distorts. "On the island I became free of the power of advertising and corporations," she said. "If you can somehow get to a place where you are not subject to those pressures, you are no longer a passive consumer -- you are a participant in life."
And robust participation breeds a certain sort of skepticism, she said, "about novelty, health fads, food fads, gadgets."
The author, whose early novels let nice girls laugh at sexual conventions, sounds convinced that sexual liberation was a mild-mannered precursor to the liberation of the senses. Particularly the sense of taste.
"Our society tends to channel all sensuality into sex," she said. "It's so limiting. You eat every day, all day. Who has sex that often?"
She's not planning to revise the sexual escapades of her ex-prom queen, or to minimize the role that the sensual played when the heroine of "Burning Questions," first published in 1978, shrugged off her Midwestern background and embraced radical feminism. She wouldn't underplay the tyranny of sex in the life of a young runaway in "On the Stroll" (1981) or the pull of passion in her book about marriage, "In Every Woman's Life . . . " (1987).
Ms. Shulman still calls herself a sexual libertarian. When asked how many times she had married she replied: "Why don't you ask me how many lovers I've had, instead?" Still, literally drinking the rain convinced her that sex was just the beginning of what the body and mind could do.
"The body was a matter of sex in my earlier books, and sex was at the center of women's predicament," she said. "Sex, if you include motherhood, is still close to the heart of women's predicament, which is quite an oppressive fact. The body is capable of all sorts of miracles: the miracle of the mind, of touch, smell, taste, sight."
Unfettering the senses seems to pull appetite squarely into the present tense, into a city whose spirit is defined not by its herbaceousness but by its ethnic varieties.
Even so, she considered a clump of tight dandelion buds in the park. And she evidenced the same thwarted desire she has for the plump orange mussels she's seen, steadily pulling streams of polluted water through the slits of their narrow shells.
"I bought all the books about wild food in the city. I can identify it, but I'd never eat it," she said, suggesting lunch in a cafe along Columbus Avenue. "Let's be in New York!"

HEADLINE: Two in Chile Get Jail Terms In U.S. Killing

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: SANTIAGO, Chile, May 30

 BODY:
The former head of the Chilean military's secret police and his deputy were sentenced to prison tonight when the Supreme Court upheld a lower court's ruling that they had ordered the assassination of the opposition leader Orlando Letelier in Washington in 1976.
The decision means that for the first time senior army officers will go to prison for human rights abuses committed under the military dictatorship of Gen. Augusto Pinochet. 
 In its ruling, the Supreme Court said the evidence against the two officers was clear: they had orchestrated the car-bombing that killed Mr. Letelier and his colleague Ronni Moffitt on Embassy Row in Washington. An American who worked for the Chilean agency and a former Chilean Army officer confessed their involvement and implicated the two officers.
The high court sentenced Gen. Manuel Contreras Sepulveda, who headed the military's National Intelligence Agency and has retired, to seven years in prison and his deputy, Brig. Gen. Pedro Espinoza Bravo, who is still in active service, to six years.
"This sentence has been given because the officers have been convicted of being authors of the premeditated murder of Orlando Letelier," said Carlos Meneses, secretary of the Supreme Court, whose reading of the decision was carried live on television.
The two officers, who have denied responsibility for the killings, saying they were the work of the United States Central Intelligence Agency, had appealed their 1993 convictions to the Supreme Court.
The ruling was a major victory for the Letelier family, left-wing politicians and human rights groups, who had declared the case a test of democratic freedoms in Chile, six years after General Pinochet gave up power to an elected Government.
Francisco Leon, an economic sociologist for the United Nations Economic Commission for Latin America and the Caribbean, said that by upholding the lower court's ruling, the Supreme Court had showed that it was an independent democratic institution, even though most of the justices were appointed by General Pinochet.
"It is important for the Chilean people to know that they showed to the world that they are more democratic today than in the past," Mr. Leon said.
Sola Sierra, president of the Association for the Disappeared and Detained, said in a television interview: "I am happy because justice has been served. This day is happier than the day that democracy returned to Chile." A Government commission found that 3,000 people disappeared or were tortured during General Pinochet's rule.
Mr. Letelier was Ambassador to the United States under the Socialist Government of President Salvador Allende Gossens, who was toppled by General Pinochet in 1973. At the time of his murder, Mr. Letelier was organizing opposition to the general.
The court's decision came after months of growing tension here in which military leaders threatened a show of force if the officers were convicted and human rights groups threatened to riot if the officers were declared not guilty.
Hundreds of police officers were deployed throughout Santiago today and barricades were placed in front of the courts and military buildings in anticipation of violence. Before the verdict was announced, thousands of people began gathering in front of the courts building, many of them chanting and carrying signs asking for justice.
Tonight, the police reported minor skirmishes at Government buildings, where masked demonstrators threw rocks and gasoline bombs at officers, who in turn fired rounds of tear gas. The demonstration was not believed to be a result of dissatisfaction with the verdict, but a display of force against the police and the Government.
The appeal had been closely watched by the United States, which had asked that the two officers be extradited to stand trial in Washington. The Supreme Court of Chile rejected the request.
"The United States applauds the successful efforts of the Chilean Government to investigate and prosecute the 1976 assassinations in Washington of Mr. Lettelier and his American aide Ronni Moffitt," the United States Embassy said in a statement tonight. "We are gratified that justice has prevailed."
General Contreras's lawyer, Sergio Miranda Carrington, said on Monday night that General Contreras was certain that he was going to be found innocent, but that he would accept the court's decision. General Contreras has been staying at his ranch in Fresia in the south of Chile.

HEADLINE: Stocks Are Mixed as Technology Issues Show Big Losses

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
The stock market closed mixed yesterday, but technology stocks lost big, in part because of the prospect of slowing sales and in part because of profit taking by traders.
Intel, the big semiconductor company, fell 5 3/8, to 110 1/2, for example, after orders for equipment used in making microchips dropped for a second consecutive month in April. 
 Intel was also the most active stock, at 15.1 million shares, or double or triple the volume of the next 12 stocks, all of which were high-tech stocks as well.
"Tech stocks have been the leader in this market, so they were the first to go," said Bill Allyn, director of listed equity trading at Jefferies & Company.
William R. Rothe, managing director of Alex. Brown & Sons, said technology prices had soared too high. "The carnage, if you will, was long overdue," he said.
But nontechnology stocks were largely spared in yesterday's rout. After falling as much as 16 points, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 9.68 points, to 4,378.68. The broader Standard & Poor's 500 closed almost unchanged at 523.58, down 0.07 point.
But the Nasdaq composite index of 4,779 stocks, many of the largest of which are technology stocks, fell for the fourth session in a row, declining 13.17 points, to 858.70.
During the day, the Nasdaq index fell as much as 15.04 points, almost matching the index's largest one-day loss of 15.15 points on Dec. 8, 1994.
Since December, the Nasdaq index's advance has been almost unbroken, rising more than 22 percent by May 23. Even after the recent decline, the index is still ahead almost 20 percent.
But yesterday's market retreat cut across a wide variety of tech stocks. Indeed, of the top 10 declining stock groups in the Standard & Poor's 500, all of the top five were high-tech groups: semiconductors, software, computers, electronic instrumentation, and telecommunications equipment.
Still, most of the damage was concentrated in computer-related stocks. The American Stock Exchange computer technology index, an index of 26 large hardware and software companies, rose more than 40 percent by May 23. But the index has declined three sessions in a row, so the advance for the period was a smaller, but still handsome, 31 percent.
The rout in tech stocks seemed to be independent of the day's news, which added to a picture of a soft economy. The technology stocks started down and stayed down. But the news may have contributed to the continued decline, analysts say.
The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence fell to 101.6 in May, far more than the 102.3 that Wall Street economists had expected. It was also a decline from 104.6 in April, the first decline in three months. Consumer confidence, which was pegged at 1985 equaling 100, has ranged as high as 120.7 in February 1989 and as low as 47.3 in February 1992.
The Semiconductor Industry Association, a trade group, said the "book-to-bill" ratio for manufacturers of equipment used to make computer chips declined to 1.23 in April from 1.29 in March, and down from a record 1.43 in February.
The ratio means the industry got $123 in orders for such equipment for every $100 of equipment actually shipped. It is used as a measure of future demand for computers, which use chips as part of their operating systems.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 0.29 point, to 281.87, with advancing stocks outnumbering declining stocks by 1,229 to 1,036. Big Board volume totaled 281 million shares. The American Stock Exchange composite index was up 0.59 point, to 491.33.
Among advancing stocks was Hi-Shear Technologies, a military contractor, which rose 3 1/8, to 12 7/8, after an analyst recommended the stock on Dan Dorfman's program on the CNBC cable television channel. Edmark, an educational software company, rose 3, to 34 1/2, after The Wall Street Journal reported favorable analyst comments about the stock.
Among declining stocks was Altera, a designer of integrated circuits, which dropped 6 1/4, to 74 3/8. LSI Logic, another integrated circuit maker, fell 5 3/4, to 67 1/4.
Also, Morrison Knudsen, the big engineering and construction company, dropped 1 1/8, to 5 3/8, after Barron's reported over the weekend that the company might be forced to file for bankruptcy protection because of contracts that might lead to losses.

CORRECTION-DATE: June 1, 1995, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day yesterday about stock trading on Tuesday referred incorrectly to a report to which a decline of technology stocks was linked. The report, on orders for equipment used to make computer chips, is issued by Semiconductor Equipment and Materials International, not the Semiconductor Industry Association.
